# Complete IT Certification Study Library
## All 9 Certifications — One Book

Version 2.0 — June 2026

---

> This combined guide contains study material for all 9 certifications in your roadmap,
> organized by career priority: **Thailand job market first → Japan job market second**.
>
> Individual guides are separated by clear section breaks for easy navigation.

---

# How to Use This Book

Start with the **Priority Roadmap** (Chapter 0) to understand WHAT to study and WHEN.
Then dive into each cert guide in the order recommended for your career stage.

**Recommended reading order (Thailand-first path):**
1. 📋 Priority Roadmap → read first, always
2. ☁️ AWS CLF-C02 → finish in progress
3. 🐍 PCEP Python → 2 weeks, $59
4. 🔒 CompTIA Security+ → most important for Thailand
5. ☁️ AWS SAA-C03 → cloud engineering role unlock
6. 🐍 PCAP Python → after landing first job
7. 🔴 eJPT → offensive security entry
8. ⚙️ CKA → Japan market powerhouse
9. 🔴 CEH v13 → compliance/government track
10. 🖥️ CompTIA A+ → reference only (consider skipping)

# ============================================================
# CHAPTER 0: PRIORITY ROADMAP — Read This First
# ============================================================

# IT Certification Priority Roadmap
## Thailand-First → Japan Career Path

> Designed for: Fresh IT grad targeting **Thailand (primary)** then **Japan (secondary)**
> Career goal: Cloud Security → Penetration Testing → Nomadic Consultant

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Why order matters

Certs don't just stack — they gate access to jobs. The wrong order means studying for months and still being unemployable because you lack the one thing hiring managers filter for at the top. This roadmap is ordered by **what actually gets you hired**, not by prestige or difficulty.

**The core rule:** Thailand hires fast for anyone with *one strong AWS cert + one programming proof*. Japan hires slowly but pays more — they want domain depth + Japanese ability. Build Thailand employability first, then upgrade for Japan.

---

# All 9 Certifications — Ordered by Priority

## Tier 0: Already Done / In Progress

### ✅ AWS CLF-C02 — Cloud Practitioner
- **Difficulty:** ⭐⭐ (Easy)
- **Time to pass:** 2–4 weeks
- **Cost:** $100 USD
- **Job value (TH):** ★★★ Opens doors at any company using AWS
- **Job value (JP):** ★★ Foundation only, not enough alone

**Why it's Tier 0:** You're already studying this. Finish it. It's the cloud literacy baseline that every employer checks. Without it, you can't credibly claim cloud knowledge.

---

## Tier 1: Do These IMMEDIATELY After CLF-C02
*These three together = Thailand-hireable in 3–4 months*

### 🥇 #1 — PCEP (Python Institute Entry-Level)
- **Difficulty:** ⭐⭐ (Easy)
- **Time to pass:** 1–2 weeks (if you code daily already)
- **Cost:** ~$59 USD → **cheapest cert in the whole list**
- **Job value (TH):** ★★★★ Every Thai tech company asks "can you code?"
- **Job value (JP):** ★★★ Python is #1 in Japanese AI/data/automation

**Why first:** Cheapest, fastest, adds Python credential to your resume. You already have an AI degree — this formalizes the Python knowledge. A $59 cert that takes 2 weeks and immediately answers "can this person code?" is the best ROI investment in this entire list.

**Pair with:** Practice Python daily on HackerRank or LeetCode Easy while studying other certs.

---

### 🥈 #2 — CompTIA Security+ SY0-701
- **Difficulty:** ⭐⭐⭐ (Medium)
- **Time to pass:** 6–10 weeks
- **Cost:** ~$392 USD
- **Job value (TH):** ★★★★★ **HIGHEST value cert for Thailand security roles**
- **Job value (JP):** ★★★★ Globally recognized, most enterprise HR know it

**Why second:** Security+ is the single most important cert for getting into cybersecurity in Thailand. Banks (SCB, Kasikorn, Krungthai), hospitals (Bangkok Dusit, Samitivej), government agencies, and MSSPs all list it on job ads. It's also DoD 8570 compliant, which matters for any role touching US-aligned organizations.

**Thailand jobs it unlocks directly:**
- SOC Analyst (฿30k–55k/month)
- Junior Security Analyst (฿35k–60k/month)
- IT Security Officer (Banking/Hospital)
- Security Consultant (MSSP)

**Honest take:** This is the most important cert in the list for Thailand. Don't skip it, don't delay it.

---

### 🥉 #3 — AWS SAA-C03 — Solutions Architect Associate
- **Difficulty:** ⭐⭐⭐⭐ (Medium-Hard)
- **Time to pass:** 8–12 weeks
- **Cost:** ~$300 USD
- **Job value (TH):** ★★★★★ **Most in-demand AWS cert in Thailand**
- **Job value (JP):** ★★★★★ Cloud engineer roles in every major Japanese tech firm

**Why third:** SAA-C03 is the cert that pushes you from "I know what cloud is" to "I can design cloud architectures." Thai cloud engineers with SAA earn 40–60% more than those with CLF only. Japanese AWS partners specifically require it.

**Thailand jobs it unlocks:**
- Cloud Engineer (฿45k–80k/month)
- Solutions Architect (฿60k–100k/month)
- DevOps Engineer (฿50k–90k/month)
- Platform Engineer

**Honest take:** CLF + SAA + Security+ as a fresh grad = genuinely competitive in Bangkok. This combination is rare enough that you'll stand out.

---

## Tier 2: Do These After Landing Your First Job
*6–12 months into working, on evenings/weekends*

### #4 — PCAP (Python Institute Associate)
- **Difficulty:** ⭐⭐⭐ (Medium)
- **Time to pass:** 4–6 weeks (after PCEP)
- **Cost:** ~$295 USD
- **Job value (TH):** ★★★★ Proves Python at engineering level (OOP, modules, generators)
- **Job value (JP):** ★★★★ Python backend + automation is highly valued

**Why fourth:** After PCEP proves you can code, PCAP proves you write real Python. OOP + file I/O + closures + decorators = the skills for automation scripts, security tools, Lambda functions. You need this before building your D3QN scanner into something professionally presentable.

**Pairs with:** Build projects using the concepts — automate your security workflow, extend your D3QN scanner, write Kubernetes automation scripts.

---

### #5 — eJPT v2 — Junior Penetration Tester
- **Difficulty:** ⭐⭐⭐ (Medium — practical)
- **Time to pass:** 4–6 weeks of hands-on lab practice
- **Cost:** $249 USD
- **Job value (TH):** ★★★ Recognized in penetration testing roles, less in general IT
- **Job value (JP):** ★★★ Less HR recognition than in Western market, but valued technically

**Why fifth:** eJPT is your first hands-on offensive security credential. It proves you can actually run Nmap, use Metasploit, crack passwords, and pivot — not just know what those things are. It's the practical proof that Security+ is the theory for. Together they make a strong junior security profile.

**Thailand:** Penetration testing firms (ACIS, NT cyfence, Deloitte Thailand CyberSecurity) specifically look for hands-on proof. eJPT fills that gap before OSCP.

**Japan:** Less recognized by HR but signals to technical reviewers that you have real skills. Japanese security teams at NTT, Fujitsu, NRI SecureTech know what it is.

**Best path:** eJPT → (eCPPTv3) → OSCP. Each builds on the previous.

---

### #6 — CKA — Certified Kubernetes Administrator
- **Difficulty:** ⭐⭐⭐⭐⭐ (Hard — performance-based)
- **Time to pass:** 8–16 weeks of serious hands-on practice
- **Cost:** ~$445 USD (includes 2 Killer Shell sessions)
- **Job value (TH):** ★★★★ Container/DevOps roles, growing fast
- **Job value (JP):** ★★★★★ **HIGHEST value for Japan** — Japan's enterprise cloud is container-heavy

**Why sixth (and why it's a monster):** CKA is the hardest cert in this list by far. It's 17 terminal tasks in 2 hours with no multiple choice. You cannot memorize your way through it — you need to actually be fast at kubectl. 8–16 weeks minimum of daily terminal practice.

**Japan specifically:** Japanese AWS Partners, NTT Data, Fujitsu, Rakuten, Line, Yahoo Japan all hire CKA holders for cloud/infrastructure roles. The Japanese container market is exploding post-pandemic. CKA here has the same weight as OSCP has in Western security.

**Thailand:** Growing fast — AWS Partners in Thailand (MFEC, SoftSquare, CloudHQ) need CKA holders. Salary premium is real: ฿70k–120k/month with CKA vs ฿50k–80k without.

---

## Tier 3: Advanced — Japan + Senior Roles

### #7 — CEH v13 — Certified Ethical Hacker
- **Difficulty:** ⭐⭐⭐ (Medium — theory-heavy MCQ)
- **Time to pass:** 6–10 weeks
- **Cost:** ~$950–$1,199 USD (**expensive**)
- **Job value (TH):** ★★★★ Strong for government, banking, military-adjacent roles
- **Job value (JP):** ★★★ Known in larger enterprises

**Why seventh — honest assessment:** CEH is the most controversial cert in this list. The technical depth is shallower than OSCP or eJPT (it's MCQ, not hands-on). But it costs ~$1,200, is DoD 8570 approved, and HR filters in Thailand's government sector and major banks actively look for it.

**Thailand:** Bank of Thailand, NECTEC, ETDA (government cyber agency), defense contractors — CEH is a checkbox requirement for many roles. If you want to work in those spaces, get it. If you're targeting private tech startups or MSSPs, eJPT + Security+ + OSCP (later) is a better combination.

**Japan:** Less HR weight than in the US/Thailand. Technical reviewers prefer OSCP. But Japanese defense contractors and government agencies know the EC-Council brand.

**Verdict:** Get this for Thailand government/banking career tracks. Skip if you're going purely technical or startup path.

---

### #8 — OSCP (Future — not covered in this book)
- **Difficulty:** ⭐⭐⭐⭐⭐ (Very Hard — 24-hour hands-on exam)
- **Time to pass:** 6–12 months after eJPT
- **Cost:** ~$1,499 USD (90-day lab access)
- **Job value (TH):** ★★★★★ If you get OSCP in Thailand, you're immediately senior
- **Job value (JP):** ★★★★★ Gold standard penetration testing credential globally

*OSCP study guide not in this book — it deserves its own volume. Preparation: eJPT → eCPPTv3 → OSCP.*

---

Focus ONLY on these in order:
1. **Finish CLF-C02** (already in progress)
2. **PCEP** (2 weeks, $59)
3. **Apply for jobs** — CLF + degree + Python claim on resume = enough to interview
4. Study Security+ while interviewing

| Month 3–4 | Study AWS SAA-C03 |

| Ongoing | Apply for jobs from Month 2 onwards |

---

## If your goal is: "Maximum earning potential (Thailand freelance + Japan consulting)"

Ultimate stack:
- **AWS:** CLF + SAA + SCS-C02 (Security Specialty)
- **Security:** Security+ + CEH + eJPT → OSCP
- **Python:** PCEP + PCAP
- **Container:** CKA
- **Language:** JLPT N2

This combination = legitimately one of the most complete profiles for a security/cloud consultant in Southeast Asia.

# Difficulty Ranking (Easiest → Hardest)

| Rank | Cert | Why |
|---|---|---|
| 1 (Easiest) | PCEP | Entry-level Python, 45 min, $59 |
| 2 | CLF-C02 | Conceptual cloud, no hands-on |
| 3 | PCAP | More Python depth, OOP, still MCQ |
| 4 | CompTIA A+ | Broad coverage, 2 exams, memorization-heavy |
| 5 | CEH v13 | Broad MCQ, 20 modules, expensive |
| 6 | CompTIA Security+ | More applied than A+, PBQs |
| 7 | eJPT | Hands-on lab, 48h, requires real skill |
| 8 | AWS SAA-C03 | Deep architectural knowledge, scenario-based |
| 9 (Hardest) | CKA | Terminal-only, speed + depth, 17 tasks in 2h |

---

# Cost-to-Value Ratio (Best → Worst)

| Rank | Cert | Cost | Value |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1 🏆 | PCEP | $59 | Cheapest cert, immediate Python proof |
| 2 | CLF-C02 | $100 | Cloud entry ticket |
| 3 | Security+ | $392 | Opens most security jobs |
| 4 | SAA-C03 | $300 | Highest AWS earning potential |
| 5 | eJPT | $249 | Hands-on proof, affordable |
| 6 | PCAP | $295 | Python level-up |
| 7 | CKA | $445 | Best Japan ROI, hardest exam |
| 8 | CompTIA A+ | $530 | Two exams, mainly for helpdesk roles |
| 9 | CEH v13 | $950–1199 | Expensive for what it tests, but required for specific tracks |

# Japan-Specific Notes

## What Thai certs translate to Japan

| Thai market cert | Japan market recognition |
|---|---|
| AWS CLF-C02 | ✅ Recognized (AWS is global) |
| AWS SAA-C03 | ✅✅ Highly valued — AWS Partners everywhere |
| Security+ | ✅ Known in enterprise/government |
| A+ | △ Less relevant — Japan uses different entry paths |
| CEH | ✅ Known in enterprise security, less so startups |
| eJPT | △ Technical respect, low HR recognition |
| CKA | ✅✅✅ **Extremely valued in Japan container world** |
| PCEP/PCAP | ✅✅ Python is huge in Japanese AI/automation |

## Japan-specific additions to consider later

| Cert | Japanese relevance |
|---|---|
| IPA 基本情報技術者 | National exam, respected in all Japanese companies, shows Japan commitment |
| IPA 応用情報技術者 | Mid-level, better than AWS equiv for traditional companies |
| IPA 情報処理安全確保支援士 | Security specialist, mandatory registration, required for gov security roles |
| JLPT N3 | Minimum for applying without recruiter buffer |
| JLPT N2 | Unlocks senior roles and direct hiring |

**Honest advice:** JLPT is as important as any technical cert for the Japan market. A candidate with N3 + CKA beats a candidate with N5 + CKA + CEH. Invest in Japanese consistently.

---

# Quick Decision Guide

**Q: I need a job in Thailand in 30 days. What do I do?**
→ Finish CLF-C02 first. Add PCEP immediately. Apply everywhere. Your degree + CLF + Python = interview-worthy. Get the job, then study Security+ evenings.

**Q: Which cert has the highest ROI?**
→ Security+ for career entry. SAA-C03 for salary increase. CKA for Japan specifically.

**Q: CEH or eJPT — which should I do first?**
→ eJPT first. It's hands-on, cheaper, builds actual skill. CEH is for compliance and HR filters. Get the skill first (eJPT), then the compliance cert (CEH) if your target roles require it.

**Q: Should I do CompTIA A+ at all?**
→ Only if you're targeting helpdesk/IT support as an explicit entry point. If you're aiming directly at cloud or security, skip A+ — it wastes 6 weeks on hardware knowledge that doesn't open cloud/security jobs in Thailand. Your degree already positions you above helpdesk.

**Q: What about JLPT?**
→ Start N3 now, regardless of everything else. Study 30 minutes a day. Japanese is the long pole in the tent for Japan employment — no amount of certs compensates for N5-level Japanese at a Japanese company.

---

# The Bottom Line

**You have a strong base:** Rangsit AI degree (GPA 3.77) + CLF in progress + D3QN vulnerability scanner + hospital internship + Japan work history + visa. This is a competitive profile.

**The problem you're solving:** Fresh grad + no recognizable security cert + Thailand isn't an OSCP market yet = need to build credibility fast and cheaply.

**The solution in 5 steps:**
1. CLF-C02 ✅ (in progress)
2. PCEP — 2 weeks, $59
3. Security+ — 6 weeks, $392
4. SAA-C03 — 8 weeks, $300
5. **Get the job.** Study the rest while employed.

Everything else (eJPT, CKA, CEH, PCAP) is the level-up you do from inside a job. The first four get you through the door.

# CHAPTER 1: AWS CLF-C02 — Cloud Practitioner

# AWS Certified Cloud Practitioner CLF-C02
## Read-to-Pass Complete Guide

**A Beginner-Friendly, Exam-Focused Study Book**

Author: *[Your Name Here]*

Version 1.0 — June 2026

Exam target: **AWS Certified Cloud Practitioner (CLF-C02)**

>
> This is an **independent study guide**. It is **not** official AWS material and is **not** affiliated with, endorsed by, or sponsored by Amazon Web Services, Inc. "AWS," "Amazon Web Services," and related marks are trademarks of Amazon.com, Inc. or its affiliates.
>
> This book contains **original practice questions** written in AWS exam style. It does **not** contain real exam questions, leaked questions, or "brain dumps." Using real exam content violates the AWS Certification Agreement and can get your certification revoked.
>
> No book can promise a "100% guaranteed pass." This guide is designed to give you the understanding and practice you need to walk in confident — but your result depends on your own study and effort. Always verify exam details against the official AWS exam guide before your test date.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Preface

## Who this book is for

This book is for you if:

- You have **basic IT knowledge** (you know what a server, a file, and a network roughly are) but you're a **beginner with AWS**.
- You want to pass the **AWS Certified Cloud Practitioner (CLF-C02)** exam without drowning in 200+ services.
- You learn better from **plain English, analogies, and practice questions** than from dense documentation.
- You're a student, a career-changer, a developer, a project manager, a salesperson, or anyone who needs **cloud literacy** plus a recognized credential.

You do **not** need any prior cloud experience. You do **not** need to write code. The Cloud Practitioner exam tests *understanding and vocabulary*, not deep hands-on engineering.

## What makes this exam different

The Cloud Practitioner is a **foundational** exam. It is wide but shallow. AWS wants to know that you can:

- Explain what the cloud is and why a business would use AWS.
- Understand who is responsible for security (you vs. AWS).
- Recognize the core services and pick the right one for a described situation.
- Understand AWS pricing, billing tools, and support plans.

You will **not** be asked to configure a VPC by hand or write an IAM policy from scratch. You **will** be asked things like *"A company needs object storage for static files. Which service?"* (Answer: Amazon S3.)

## How to use this book

1. **Read each domain chapter once, slowly.** Don't memorize yet — aim to *understand*.
2. **Do the mini quiz** at the end of each chapter. Read the explanations even for questions you got right.
3. **Study the comparison tables and the Keywords Decoder.** These two sections win you the most points for the least effort.
4. **Take the practice exams** under realistic conditions (timer on, no notes).
5. **Review every wrong answer** and find the chapter that covers it.
6. The night before, read only the **Cheatsheets** and the **Last-Day Review Sheet**.

> **Remember:** On this exam, *recognizing keywords* is half the battle. "Object storage," "serverless," "DDoS protection," "managed database" — each maps to a specific service. The Keywords Decoder chapter is built for exactly this.

## Suggested study plans

Pick the plan that matches your timeline. All three assume 1–2 focused hours per day.

### 7-Day Plan (fast track — for those with some IT background)

| Day | Focus |
|-----|-------|
| 1 | Preface + Exam Overview + Domain 1 (Cloud Concepts) + mini quiz |
| 2 | Domain 2 (Security & Compliance) + mini quiz |
| 3 | Domain 3 part 1 (Compute, Storage, Databases) + questions |
| 4 | Domain 3 part 2 (Networking, Integration, AI/ML, Management, Migration) + questions |
| 5 | Domain 4 (Billing, Pricing, Support) + Comparison Tables + Keywords Decoder |
| 6 | Mock Exam 1 + review every wrong answer |
| 7 | Mock Exam 2 + Cheatsheets + Last-Day Review Sheet |

### 14-Day Plan (recommended balance)

| Days | Focus |
|------|-------|
| 1–2 | Exam Overview + Domain 1 + quiz, re-read weak spots |
| 3–4 | Domain 2 + quiz |
| 5–7 | Domain 3 (split compute/storage/db, then networking/integration/AI, then management/migration) |
| 8 | Domain 4 + quiz |
| 9 | Comparison Tables + Keywords Decoder + Common Traps |
| 10 | 20-question beginner quiz + 40-question mixed quiz, review |
| 11 | Mock Exam 1 + review |
| 12 | Mock Exam 2 + review |
| 13 | Final Readiness Exam + review |
| 14 | Cheatsheets + Last-Day Review Sheet + light re-read |

### 30-Day Plan (thorough — for total beginners)

| Week | Focus |
|------|-------|
| Week 1 | Exam Overview + Domain 1, slow read, take notes, redo quiz twice |
| Week 2 | Domain 2 fully; build your own shared-responsibility table; quiz |
| Week 3 | Domain 3 across the week (a couple of categories per day) + per-service questions |
| Week 4 | Domain 4 + all comparison tables + traps + all 5 practice sets, reviewing wrong answers each time; finish with cheatsheets |

## How the real exam works (short version)

- It's **multiple choice** (pick 1 of 4) and **multiple response** (pick 2+ of 5+).
- **65 questions, 90 minutes.** Only **50 are scored**; 15 are unscored experimental questions you can't identify.
- You need a scaled score of **700 out of 1,000** to pass.
- **No penalty for guessing** — never leave a question blank.
- You find out **pass/fail immediately** when you finish.

## Exam mindset

> **Exam Tip:** Read the *last sentence* of the question first. It usually contains the actual ask ("Which service is MOST cost-effective?", "Which TWO are customer responsibilities?"). Then read the scenario knowing what you're looking for.

- **Don't overthink.** This is a foundational exam. If an answer feels too advanced or too obscure, it's usually a distractor.
- **Watch the qualifier words:** *MOST cost-effective, LEAST operational overhead, fully managed, serverless, highly available.* These words decide the answer.
- **Eliminate, don't just pick.** Cross out two obviously wrong answers and your odds jump.
- **Flag and move on.** Don't spend 4 minutes on one question when 64 others are waiting.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Exam Overview

Here are the verified facts about the CLF-C02 exam. Always re-check the official AWS exam guide close to your test date, because AWS updates services and occasionally tweaks details.

## Exam at a glance

| Item | Detail |
|------|--------|
| **Exam name** | AWS Certified Cloud Practitioner |
| **Exam code** | CLF-C02 (current version, in use since September 19, 2023) |
| **Number of questions** | 65 total (50 scored + 15 unscored) |
| **Time limit** | 90 minutes |
| **Passing score** | 700 out of 1,000 (scaled) |
| **Scoring model** | Compensatory — you must pass the exam *overall*, not each domain |
| **Cost** | 100 USD (plus local tax where applicable) |
| **Question types** | Multiple choice + multiple response |
| **Languages** | Offered in many languages, including English |
| **Delivery** | Online proctored (from home/office) OR at a Pearson VUE test center |
| **Validity** | 3 years |
| **Retake policy** | Wait 14 days between attempts; pay the fee each time; no limit on lifetime attempts |
| **Result** | Pass/fail shown on screen immediately; full score report follows by email |

## The four domains and their weights

| Domain | Name | Weight |
|--------|------|--------|
| 1 | Cloud Concepts | **24%** |
| 2 | Security and Compliance | **30%** |
| 3 | Cloud Technology and Services | **34%** |
| 4 | Billing, Pricing, and Support | **12%** |

> **Exam Tip:** Domains 2 and 3 together are **64%** of your score. If you're short on time, make sure Security and Services are rock-solid. Domain 4 is only 12%, but it's *easy* points — pricing and support questions are very predictable, so don't skip them.

## Question types explained

**Multiple choice (single answer):** One question, four options (A–D). Exactly one is correct; the other three are *distractors* (plausible but wrong).

**Multiple response (choose two or more):** One question, five or more options. The question will tell you exactly how many to pick.

> **Remember:**
> - **"Choose TWO"** = select exactly **2** answers. Selecting 1 or 3 is marked wrong.
> - **"Choose THREE"** = select exactly **3** answers.
> - On multiple-response questions you usually need **all** selections correct to get the point. There is no partial credit.

> **Common Trap:** On a "Choose TWO" question, test-takers find one obviously correct answer and stop reading. There are often *two strong* answers and *one tempting-but-wrong* answer. Evaluate every option before committing.

## Online proctored vs. test center

| | Online proctored | Test center |
|--|------------------|-------------|
| Where | Your home or a quiet private room | Pearson VUE center |
| Pros | Convenient, no travel | No setup hassle, fewer tech risks |
| Cons | Strict rules: clean desk, no phone, webcam on, no one enters the room | You must travel and book a slot |
| Need | Stable internet, working webcam/mic, government ID | Government ID |

> **Exam Tip:** If you choose **online**, run the system test beforehand, clear your desk completely, and make sure no one walks into the room — proctors can void your exam for these. Have your government ID ready.

## What to expect on exam day

1. Arrive (or log in) **15–30 minutes early**.
2. Show **government-issued photo ID**.
3. For online: scan your room with your webcam; remove notes, phones, drinks, and extra monitors.
4. You get an on-screen **scratchpad/whiteboard** for notes (you can't bring your own paper).
5. The clock starts. 65 questions, 90 minutes — that's about **83 seconds per question**.
6. You can **flag** questions and revisit them before submitting.
7. Submit, complete a short survey, and your **pass/fail** appears.

## Time-management strategy

- **Pass 1 (~60 min):** Answer everything you know quickly. Flag anything that takes more than ~90 seconds and move on.
- **Pass 2 (~20 min):** Return to flagged questions. Use elimination.
- **Pass 3 (~10 min):** Make sure **no question is blank** (no penalty for guessing), review flags, submit.

> **Remember:** 90 minutes for 65 questions is generous for a foundational exam. Most people finish with time to spare. Don't rush, but don't camp on hard questions either.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Domain 1: Cloud Concepts (24%)

This domain is about the **"why"** of cloud computing. AWS wants you to explain the value of the cloud to a business, recognize cloud design principles, understand migration basics, and grasp cloud economics. No deep technical knowledge needed — this is mostly vocabulary and concepts.

## 1.1 What is cloud computing?

**Cloud computing** is renting IT resources — servers, storage, databases, networking, software — over the internet, and paying only for what you use. Instead of buying and running your own physical data center, you use someone else's (AWS's), on demand.

> **Simple analogy:** Owning a data center is like buying a car: big upfront cost, you maintain it, it sits idle most of the day. The cloud is like a ride-share: you pay per trip, someone else handles maintenance, and you can summon a bigger vehicle whenever you need one.

The three big "models" of cloud (covered in detail below) are **IaaS, PaaS, SaaS**, and the three deployment types are **public, private, hybrid**.

## 1.2 Benefits of the AWS Cloud

These are the classic "six advantages of cloud" plus AWS's value proposition. Expect questions that quote these almost word-for-word.

| Benefit | Plain meaning |
|---------|---------------|
| **Trade CapEx for OpEx** | Stop paying big upfront for hardware (capital expense). Pay a flexible operating expense instead. |
| **Benefit from massive economies of scale** | AWS buys hardware for millions of customers, so it's cheaper per unit. You get those low prices. |
| **Stop guessing capacity** | Scale up or down on demand instead of over-buying servers "just in case." |
| **Increase speed and agility** | Spin up resources in minutes, not weeks. Experiment cheaply. |
| **Stop spending money running data centers** | No more racking servers, cooling, security guards. Focus on your actual product. |
| **Go global in minutes** | Deploy your app close to customers worldwide with a few clicks. |

### Key cloud terms you MUST know

These come up constantly. Learn the precise difference between them — the exam loves to mix them up.

| Term | Definition | One-line memory hook |
|------|------------|----------------------|
| **Scalability** | The ability to grow (or shrink) to handle changing load. | "Can get bigger when needed." |
| **Elasticity** | Automatically adding/removing resources to match demand in near real time. | "Auto-grows AND auto-shrinks, automatically." |
| **High availability (HA)** | System stays up and reachable most of the time, minimizing downtime. | "Rarely goes down." |
| **Fault tolerance** | System keeps working even when a component fails — no interruption. | "Keeps running through a failure." |
| **Agility** | Speed to build, test, and release; ability to experiment cheaply. | "Move fast, try things." |

> **Common Trap:** **Elasticity vs. Scalability.** Scalability is the *capability* to grow. Elasticity is *automatic* matching of resources to demand, up and down. If a question says "automatically adds and removes capacity to match traffic," that's **elasticity**.

> **Common Trap:** **High availability vs. Fault tolerance.** HA = minimal downtime (might blip briefly during failover). Fault tolerance = *zero* interruption even during a failure (more redundancy, usually more expensive). Fault tolerance is a stronger guarantee than HA.

## 1.3 CapEx vs. OpEx and cloud economics

- **CapEx (Capital Expenditure):** Large upfront purchase of assets you own — e.g., buying physical servers. Traditional data centers are CapEx-heavy.
- **OpEx (Operational Expenditure):** Ongoing pay-as-you-go spending — e.g., paying AWS monthly for what you used. The cloud shifts you from CapEx to OpEx.

**Cloud economics ideas the exam tests:**

- **Pay-as-you-go / pay-for-what-you-use:** Pay only for the resources you actually consume, with no long-term contracts required.
- **Economies of scale:** AWS's huge scale means lower prices that get passed to customers.
- **Rightsizing:** Choosing the cheapest resource that still meets your needs (e.g., a smaller EC2 instance). A core cost-optimization idea.
- **Total Cost of Ownership (TCO):** Comparing the *full* cost of on-premises (hardware, power, cooling, staff, real estate) vs. cloud. Cloud often wins because it removes hidden costs.
- **Automation reduces cost:** Automating tasks reduces labor and human error.

> **Exam Tip:** If a question contrasts "buying servers upfront" with "paying monthly for usage," it's testing **CapEx -> OpEx**. The cloud answer is almost always OpEx / pay-as-you-go.

## 1.4 Cloud deployment models

| Model | What it means | Example |
|-------|---------------|---------|
| **Public cloud (Cloud)** | Everything runs in a cloud provider like AWS. Most modern apps. | A startup running entirely on AWS. |
| **Private cloud (On-premises)** | Infrastructure in your own data center, often virtualized but not public-cloud. | A bank running its own racks. |
| **Hybrid** | A mix: some workloads in AWS, some on-premises, connected together. | A company keeping sensitive data on-prem but bursting to AWS for extra compute. |

> **Remember:** On the exam, **"on-premises"** often stands in for *private cloud / your own data center*. **"Hybrid"** = AWS **plus** on-premises connected (think **AWS Direct Connect**, **VPN**, **AWS Outposts**, **Storage Gateway**).

## 1.5 Cloud service models: IaaS, PaaS, SaaS

This is the classic "pizza-as-a-service" idea. How much does the provider manage vs. you?

| Model | You manage | Provider manages | AWS example |
|-------|-----------|------------------|-------------|
| **IaaS** (Infrastructure as a Service) | OS, apps, data, runtime | Hardware, networking, virtualization | **Amazon EC2** (you get a virtual server, you install everything) |
| **PaaS** (Platform as a Service) | Just your app and data | OS, runtime, scaling, servers | **AWS Elastic Beanstalk**, **AWS Lambda** |
| **SaaS** (Software as a Service) | Just your usage/settings | Everything else | Gmail, Salesforce, **Amazon WorkMail / Amazon Chime** |

> **Simple analogy (pizza):**
> - **IaaS** = you buy ingredients and cook at home (most control, most work).
> - **PaaS** = you buy a take-and-bake pizza (just pop it in the oven).
> - **SaaS** = you order a delivered pizza (just eat it).

> **Common Trap:** Questions ask which model gives you "the most control over the operating system." That's **IaaS** (EC2). If it says "I just want to upload my code and not manage servers," that's **PaaS** (Elastic Beanstalk / Lambda).

## 1.6 The AWS Well-Architected Framework (6 pillars)

The Well-Architected Framework is AWS's set of best practices for building good cloud systems. **CLF-C02 expanded it to SIX pillars** (Sustainability was added). You must know all six and what each one means.

| Pillar | Focus | Memory hook |
|--------|-------|-------------|
| **Operational Excellence** | Run and monitor systems, improve processes, automate. | "Run it well, keep improving." |
| **Security** | Protect data and systems; manage access; detect threats. | "Lock it down." |
| **Reliability** | Recover from failure, scale, meet demand. | "Keep it working / recover fast." |
| **Performance Efficiency** | Use resources efficiently; pick the right resource for the job. | "Right tool, used efficiently." |
| **Cost Optimization** | Avoid unnecessary costs; pay only for what you need. | "Don't waste money." |
| **Sustainability** | Minimize environmental impact of cloud workloads. | "Be green." |

> **Exam Tip:** A common question describes a goal and asks which pillar it maps to. "Reducing the carbon footprint of a workload" -> **Sustainability**. "Recovering automatically from an Availability Zone failure" -> **Reliability**. "Removing idle resources to cut the bill" -> **Cost Optimization**. "Automating deployments and monitoring" -> **Operational Excellence**.

> **Common Trap:** Reliability vs. Performance Efficiency. Reliability = staying up and recovering from failures. Performance Efficiency = using the *right-sized, most efficient* resources. If it's about failure recovery -> Reliability. If it's about choosing the most efficient instance type or using serverless for efficiency -> Performance Efficiency.

## 1.7 Migration basics and the AWS Cloud Adoption Framework (AWS CAF)

CLF-C02 added migration content. You don't need deep detail — just the high-level ideas.

**AWS Cloud Adoption Framework (AWS CAF)** helps organizations plan and execute a cloud migration. It organizes guidance into **six perspectives**:

| Perspective | Roughly about |
|-------------|---------------|
| **Business** | Business value, outcomes |
| **People** | Staff skills, roles, change management |
| **Governance** | Managing and measuring the program, risk |
| **Platform** | Building the cloud environment/architecture |
| **Security** | Compliance and protecting data |
| **Operations** | Running and supporting workloads |

> **Memory hook for CAF perspectives:** **B-P-G-P-S-O** -> "**B**ig **P**eople **G**enerally **P**refer **S**afe **O**perations." (Business, People, Governance, Platform, Security, Operations.)

**The 7 Rs of migration** (high level — you mainly need to recognize a few):

- **Rehost** ("lift and shift") — move as-is, no changes.
- **Replatform** ("lift, tinker, and shift") — small optimizations during the move.
- **Refactor / Re-architect** — redesign the app for the cloud.
- **Repurchase** — switch to a different product (often SaaS).
- **Retain** — keep some things on-premises for now.
- **Retire** — turn off what you no longer need.
- **Relocate** — move infrastructure without buying new hardware.

**Migration benefits the exam likes:** reduced business risk, lower costs, improved operational resilience, environmental sustainability, faster time to market.

> **Exam Tip:** "Move the application to AWS without any changes" = **Rehost (lift and shift)**. "Make minor optimizations during migration" = **Replatform**.

## 1.8 Domain 1 — Real exam traps

> **Common Trap 1:** Mixing up **elasticity** and **scalability**. Auto-matching demand = elasticity.

> **Common Trap 2:** Mixing up **high availability** and **fault tolerance**. Fault tolerance = no interruption at all.

> **Common Trap 3:** Calling Lambda or Elastic Beanstalk "IaaS." They're **PaaS/serverless**. EC2 is the IaaS answer.

> **Common Trap 4:** Forgetting the **6th pillar (Sustainability)**. Old CLF-C01 material lists only 5.

> **Common Trap 5:** Thinking "hybrid" means two AWS Regions. Hybrid = AWS **+ on-premises**.

## 1.9 Domain 1 — Mini Quiz

**Q1.** A company wants to stop buying physical servers upfront and instead pay only for the compute it uses each month. This is an example of:

- A. Trading OpEx for CapEx
- B. Trading CapEx for OpEx
- C. Economies of scale
- D. Fault tolerance

**Q2.** Which concept describes a system that *automatically* adds and removes capacity to match changing demand?

- A. Scalability
- B. High availability
- C. Elasticity
- D. Agility

**Q3.** Which Well-Architected pillar focuses on minimizing the environmental impact of running cloud workloads?

- A. Reliability
- B. Performance Efficiency
- C. Sustainability
- D. Operational Excellence

**Q4.** A business wants the *most control* over the operating system of its servers in AWS. Which cloud service model fits best?

- A. SaaS
- B. PaaS
- C. IaaS
- D. FaaS

**Q5.** A company keeps sensitive data in its own data center but runs its public website on AWS, with the two connected. This is which deployment model?

- A. Public cloud
- B. Private cloud
- C. Hybrid
- D. Multi-Region

**Q6.** (Choose TWO) Which of the following are benefits of cloud computing as described by AWS?

- A. Eliminating all security responsibility for the customer
- B. Trading capital expense for variable expense
- C. Going global in minutes
- D. Guaranteeing zero cost
- E. Removing the need for any monitoring

**Q7.** An application must keep running with *no interruption at all*, even if an individual component fails. Which property is this?

- A. High availability
- B. Fault tolerance
- C. Elasticity
- D. Agility

**Q8.** Which framework helps an organization *plan its overall journey* to the cloud across business, people, and governance perspectives?

- A. AWS Well-Architected Framework
- B. AWS Cloud Adoption Framework (AWS CAF)
- C. AWS Shared Responsibility Model
- D. AWS Trusted Advisor

### Domain 1 — Answers & Explanations

**Q1 -> B.** Buying servers upfront is **CapEx**; paying monthly for usage is **OpEx**. The cloud shifts you from CapEx to OpEx.
- A is the reverse. C (economies of scale) is a real benefit but not what "pay monthly instead of buying" describes. D is unrelated.

**Q2 -> C. Elasticity.** Automatic matching of resources to demand (up *and* down) is elasticity.
- A (scalability) is the *ability* to grow but doesn't imply automatic shrink. B is about uptime. D is about speed of innovation.

**Q3 -> C. Sustainability.** This is the 6th pillar, added in CLF-C02, focused on environmental impact.
- Reliability = recovery; Performance Efficiency = efficient resource use; Operational Excellence = running/improving systems.

**Q4 -> C. IaaS.** Infrastructure as a Service (e.g., EC2) gives you control of the OS and what you install.
- SaaS/PaaS hide the OS from you. "FaaS" (functions, like Lambda) gives you the *least* control.

**Q5 -> C. Hybrid.** On-premises data center connected to AWS workloads = hybrid.
- Public = all in AWS; private = all on-prem; multi-Region is about using multiple AWS Regions, not on-prem.

**Q6 -> B and C.** Trading CapEx for variable (OpEx) expense and going global in minutes are AWS-stated benefits.
- A and E are false (security and monitoring are shared/customer responsibilities). D is false — the cloud is not free.

**Q7 -> B. Fault tolerance.** No interruption during a failure is fault tolerance (stronger than HA, which allows brief blips).

**Q8 -> B. AWS CAF.** The Cloud Adoption Framework guides the overall migration journey across six perspectives. Well-Architected is about building good systems; Shared Responsibility is about security ownership; Trusted Advisor is a recommendation tool.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Domain 2: Security and Compliance (30%)

This is the **largest** domain — 30% of your score. The good news: most of it is conceptual and very learnable. Master the **Shared Responsibility Model** and **IAM** first; together they're worth a lot of points.

## 2.1 The AWS Shared Responsibility Model

This is the single most important concept in the whole exam. Memorize the boundary.

- **AWS is responsible for security OF the cloud** — the physical hardware, data centers, networking, and the software that runs AWS's managed services. Think: buildings, racks, power, the hypervisor, the global infrastructure.
- **The customer (you) is responsible for security IN the cloud** — your data, your configurations, your access management, who can log in, encryption choices, OS patching on EC2, security group rules, etc.

> **Simple analogy:** Renting an apartment. The landlord (AWS) secures the building, the foundation, and the locks on the front door of the building. You (the tenant) decide who gets a key to *your* apartment, what you keep inside, and whether you lock your own door.

| AWS handles ("OF the cloud") | Customer handles ("IN the cloud") |
|------------------------------|-----------------------------------|
| Physical data centers, buildings, guards | Customer data |
| Hardware, servers, storage drives | Identity and access management (who can do what) |
| Networking infrastructure | OS patching/updates **on EC2** |
| Virtualization layer (hypervisor) | Application code and its security |
| Managed service software (e.g., RDS engine maintenance) | Firewall config (security groups, NACLs) |
| Global infrastructure (Regions, AZs) | Client- and server-side encryption choices |
| | Network traffic protection settings |

> **Common Trap:** Responsibility **shifts** depending on the service type.
> - **EC2 (IaaS):** Customer patches the OS and everything above it. (More customer responsibility.)
> - **RDS (managed/PaaS):** AWS patches the database engine and OS; customer manages data, access, and configuration. (Less customer responsibility.)
> - **S3, DynamoDB, Lambda (fully managed/serverless):** AWS manages the underlying systems; customer manages data, permissions, and settings.

> **Remember the unshakeable rule:** **The customer is ALWAYS responsible for their own data and for who has access to it (IAM).** AWS never manages your data classification or your user permissions for you.

> **Exam Tip:** If you see "patch the guest operating system of an EC2 instance," that's the **customer**. If you see "patch the physical host / hypervisor," that's **AWS**. If you see "maintain the database engine for Amazon RDS," that's **AWS**.

## 2.2 Identity and Access Management (IAM)

**AWS IAM** controls *who* can access *what* in your AWS account. It is **free** and **global** (not tied to a Region).

### The four IAM building blocks

| Component | What it is | Analogy |
|-----------|-----------|---------|
| **User** | An identity for one person or application. Has long-term credentials. | An individual employee badge. |
| **Group** | A collection of users that share permissions. | A department ("Developers") — everyone in it inherits the same access. |
| **Role** | A temporary identity that can be *assumed* by users, applications, or AWS services. No long-term credentials. | A visitor pass you pick up, use, and return. |
| **Policy** | A JSON document that defines permissions (allow/deny actions on resources). Attached to users, groups, or roles. | The rulebook that says what a badge can open. |

> **Common Trap:** **Role vs. User.**
> - Use a **role** when an **AWS service or application** needs permissions (e.g., an EC2 instance needs to read from S3 — give the EC2 instance a **role**, never hard-code a user's keys).
> - Use a **role** for **temporary** access and for **cross-account** access.
> - Use a **user** for a long-lived human or specific app identity with its own credentials.
> - **Best practice: applications and EC2 instances should use ROLES, not access keys stored on the instance.**

### Root user best practices

The **root user** is the all-powerful account created when you first sign up (the email address you used). It can do *everything*, including closing the account.

- **Don't use the root user for daily work.** Create an IAM user (or use IAM Identity Center) instead.
- **Enable MFA on the root user.**
- **Don't create access keys for the root user** (delete them if they exist).
- Lock the root credentials away; use them only for the few tasks that *require* root.

### MFA (Multi-Factor Authentication)

MFA adds a second proof of identity (a code from a phone app or a hardware key) on top of the password. **Always enable MFA on the root user and on privileged IAM users.** "Something you know (password) + something you have (device)."

### Least privilege

Grant **only the permissions needed** to do the job — nothing more. Start with no access and add what's required. This is a core security best practice the exam repeats often.

### IAM Identity Center (formerly AWS SSO)

**AWS IAM Identity Center** lets you manage **single sign-on (SSO)** access for many users across **multiple AWS accounts** and to business apps, often connected to an existing corporate directory (like Microsoft Active Directory). Use it when you have lots of users and/or many accounts and want central login.

> **Exam Tip:** "Centrally manage user access across multiple AWS accounts with single sign-on" -> **IAM Identity Center**. "Grant an application running on EC2 access to S3" -> **IAM role**. "Limit a user to only the actions they need" -> **least privilege**.

## 2.3 AWS Organizations and Service Control Policies

**AWS Organizations** lets you centrally manage **multiple AWS accounts** as one organization. Benefits:

- **Consolidated billing** — one bill for all accounts, plus combined usage can earn volume discounts.
- **Central governance** — group accounts into **Organizational Units (OUs)** and apply policies.
- **Service Control Policies (SCPs)** — set *maximum permissions* (guardrails) for accounts/OUs.

> **Important:** An **SCP does not grant permissions** — it sets the **boundary** of what's allowed. Even an account admin cannot exceed an SCP. (Actual permissions still come from IAM policies; the SCP just caps them.)

> **Exam Tip:** "Restrict which AWS services member accounts can use, no matter what their IAM admins allow" -> **Service Control Policy (SCP)** via **AWS Organizations**.

## 2.4 Logging, monitoring, and auditing services

These three get confused constantly. Learn the one-word difference.

| Service | One-word job | What it answers |
|---------|--------------|-----------------|
| **AWS CloudTrail** | **Who did what** | "Which user deleted that S3 bucket, and when?" Records API calls / account activity for auditing. |
| **Amazon CloudWatch** | **How is it performing** | "Is CPU high? Send an alarm." Metrics, logs, alarms, dashboards. |
| **AWS Config** | **What changed / is it compliant** | "Is this resource configured against my rules? Show its config history." Tracks resource configuration and compliance over time. |

> **Common Trap (very frequent):**
> - **CloudTrail = audit trail of API activity (who/what/when).** Think "*trail*" = a record of actions.
> - **CloudWatch = performance metrics, logs, and alarms.** Think "*watch*" = watching health.
> - **Config = configuration compliance and change history.**
> If a question says "track user activity and API calls for a security investigation," it's **CloudTrail**. "Trigger an alarm when CPU exceeds 80%" is **CloudWatch**. "Continuously check whether resources comply with your configuration rules" is **AWS Config**.

## 2.5 Compliance and audit reports

**AWS Artifact** is a self-service portal to download **AWS compliance reports** (like SOC, ISO, PCI DSS) and accept agreements. If a question asks "where do I get AWS's compliance/audit documents on demand," the answer is **AWS Artifact**.

**Compliance concepts:** AWS complies with many global standards (ISO 27001, SOC 1/2/3, PCI DSS, HIPAA eligibility, GDPR, FedRAMP, etc.). Under shared responsibility, AWS is compliant for the infrastructure; **you** must configure your workloads to remain compliant.

## 2.6 Threat protection and detection services

This cluster is heavily tested. Learn what each one defends against.

| Service | Protects against / does | Memory hook |
|---------|------------------------|-------------|
| **AWS Shield** | **DDoS** (Distributed Denial of Service) attacks. Shield Standard is free and automatic; Shield Advanced is paid with extra protection and support. | "Shield blocks the flood (DDoS)." |
| **AWS WAF** (Web Application Firewall) | Filters malicious **web traffic** — **SQL injection, cross-site scripting (XSS)**, bad bots. Works at the application layer. | "WAF = Web App Firewall, blocks bad web requests." |
| **Amazon GuardDuty** | **Intelligent threat detection** — analyzes logs to find suspicious/malicious activity (e.g., unusual API calls, compromised instances). | "GuardDuty stands guard, detecting threats." |
| **Amazon Inspector** | **Automated vulnerability assessment** of EC2, containers, and Lambda — finds software vulnerabilities and unintended network exposure. | "Inspector inspects for vulnerabilities." |
| **AWS Security Hub** | **Central dashboard** that aggregates and prioritizes security findings from GuardDuty, Inspector, and more, and checks against best practices. | "Hub = one place for all security findings." |
| **Amazon Macie** | Uses ML to **discover and protect sensitive data (like PII)** stored in S3. | "Macie finds your secrets in S3." |
| **Amazon Detective** | Helps **investigate** the root cause of security findings. | "Detective investigates." |

> **Common Trap (extremely common):** **WAF vs. Shield vs. GuardDuty vs. Inspector.**
> - **DDoS attack** -> **Shield**.
> - **SQL injection / XSS / filter web requests** -> **WAF**.
> - **Detect suspicious activity / threats from logs** -> **GuardDuty**.
> - **Scan for software vulnerabilities / CVEs / patches needed** -> **Inspector**.
> - **One dashboard for everything** -> **Security Hub**.
> - **Find sensitive data (PII) in S3** -> **Macie**.

## 2.7 Encryption and secrets services

| Service | What it does |
|---------|-------------|
| **AWS KMS** (Key Management Service) | Create and manage **encryption keys**; encrypt data across AWS services. The go-to answer for "manage encryption keys." |
| **AWS CloudHSM** | Dedicated **hardware security module** for stricter key control/compliance. (Know it exists.) |
| **AWS Secrets Manager** | Store and **automatically rotate** secrets like database passwords and API keys. | 
| **AWS Certificate Manager (ACM)** | Provision and manage **SSL/TLS certificates** for HTTPS, often for free with AWS services. |
| **AWS Systems Manager Parameter Store** | Store configuration data and secrets (simpler/cheaper than Secrets Manager; no built-in auto-rotation). |

### Encryption: at rest vs. in transit

- **Encryption at rest:** Protecting **stored** data (on disk, in S3, in a database). Often done with **KMS**.
- **Encryption in transit:** Protecting data **moving** over a network (e.g., HTTPS/TLS). Often uses **ACM** certificates.

> **Exam Tip:**
> - "Manage encryption keys" -> **KMS**.
> - "Automatically rotate database credentials" -> **Secrets Manager**.
> - "Provision an SSL/TLS certificate for HTTPS" -> **ACM**.
> - "Encrypt data being stored" = encryption **at rest**; "encrypt data traveling over the network" = encryption **in transit**.

## 2.8 Domain 2 — Real exam traps

> **Common Trap 1:** Forgetting responsibility shifts by service. **You always own your data and IAM permissions**, but OS patching is yours on **EC2** and AWS's on **RDS/S3/Lambda**.

> **Common Trap 2:** **CloudTrail vs. CloudWatch vs. Config** — who-did-what vs. performance vs. configuration-compliance.

> **Common Trap 3:** Thinking an **SCP grants** permissions. It only **limits** the maximum.

> **Common Trap 4:** Using a **user's access keys** on an EC2 instance instead of an **IAM role**. Roles are the best practice.

> **Common Trap 5:** **Shield (DDoS)** vs. **WAF (SQLi/XSS)** vs. **GuardDuty (threat detection)** vs. **Inspector (vulnerability scan)**.

> **Common Trap 6:** **AWS Artifact** is for **compliance reports**, not security monitoring. Don't confuse it with Security Hub.

## 2.9 Domain 2 — Mini Quiz

**Q1.** Under the AWS Shared Responsibility Model, which task is the **customer's** responsibility?

- A. Maintaining the physical security of data centers
- B. Patching the hypervisor
- C. Configuring IAM users and permissions
- D. Maintaining the global network backbone

**Q2.** A security team needs to know **which user made a specific API call** that deleted a resource last week. Which service provides this?

- A. Amazon CloudWatch
- B. AWS CloudTrail
- C. AWS Config
- D. AWS Trusted Advisor

**Q3.** An application running on an EC2 instance needs to read objects from an S3 bucket. What is the **best practice** way to grant this access?

- A. Store the root user's access keys on the instance
- B. Create an IAM user and embed its access keys in the application
- C. Attach an IAM role to the EC2 instance
- D. Make the S3 bucket public

**Q4.** Which service protects a web application against **SQL injection and cross-site scripting**?

- A. AWS Shield
- B. AWS WAF
- C. Amazon GuardDuty
- D. Amazon Inspector

**Q5.** Where can a customer download **AWS compliance reports** such as SOC 2 and ISO certifications?

- A. AWS Security Hub
- B. AWS Artifact
- C. AWS Config
- D. Amazon Macie

**Q6.** (Choose TWO) Which are recommended **root user** best practices?

- A. Use the root user for all daily administrative tasks
- B. Enable MFA on the root user
- C. Create multiple access keys for the root user
- D. Avoid using the root user and create IAM identities instead
- E. Share the root credentials with the whole team

**Q7.** A company wants to set a **maximum permissions guardrail** across all accounts so that no member account can use a forbidden service, regardless of its own IAM settings. What should they use?

- A. An IAM policy on each user
- B. A Service Control Policy (SCP) in AWS Organizations
- C. Amazon CloudWatch alarms
- D. AWS Shield Advanced

**Q8.** Which service uses machine learning to **detect threats** by analyzing account activity and logs?

- A. Amazon Inspector
- B. Amazon GuardDuty
- C. AWS Certificate Manager
- D. AWS KMS

**Q9.** A team needs to **automatically rotate** a database password every 30 days. Which service is designed for this?

- A. AWS KMS
- B. AWS Secrets Manager
- C. AWS Artifact
- D. AWS Config

**Q10.** Which service continuously **records configuration changes** to your AWS resources and checks them against compliance rules?

- A. AWS CloudTrail
- B. Amazon CloudWatch
- C. AWS Config
- D. AWS Shield

### Domain 2 — Answers & Explanations

**Q1 -> C.** Configuring IAM users/permissions is "security **in** the cloud" — always the customer's job. A, B, and D are AWS's "security **of** the cloud" responsibilities.

**Q2 -> B. AWS CloudTrail.** It records API calls (who did what, when) for auditing. CloudWatch is performance/metrics; Config is configuration compliance; Trusted Advisor gives best-practice recommendations.

**Q3 -> C. Attach an IAM role to the EC2 instance.** Roles give temporary, rotated credentials and are the best practice. A and B hard-code long-term keys (insecure). D exposes your data publicly.

**Q4 -> B. AWS WAF.** A Web Application Firewall filters malicious web requests like SQL injection and XSS. Shield is DDoS; GuardDuty is threat detection; Inspector is vulnerability scanning.

**Q5 -> B. AWS Artifact.** It's the on-demand portal for AWS compliance documents. Security Hub aggregates findings; Config tracks configuration; Macie finds sensitive data.

**Q6 -> B and D.** Enable MFA on root, and avoid using root by creating IAM identities. A, C, and E are all insecure anti-patterns.

**Q7 -> B. Service Control Policy (SCP).** SCPs in AWS Organizations cap the maximum permissions for member accounts. IAM policies grant within those caps; CloudWatch and Shield are unrelated.

**Q8 -> B. Amazon GuardDuty.** It's the intelligent threat-detection service. Inspector scans for vulnerabilities; ACM handles certificates; KMS manages keys.

**Q9 -> B. AWS Secrets Manager.** It stores and automatically rotates secrets like DB credentials. KMS manages keys (not rotation of app secrets in this sense); Artifact is compliance docs; Config is resource compliance.

**Q10 -> C. AWS Config.** It records configuration changes and evaluates compliance over time. CloudTrail logs API activity; CloudWatch is metrics/alarms; Shield is DDoS protection.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Domain 3: Cloud Technology and Services (34%)

This is the biggest domain. It tests whether you can **recognize the right AWS service for a described need**. You don't need to configure anything — you need to know *what each core service is for*. This chapter covers the global infrastructure, ways to use AWS, and the exam-relevant services grouped by category.

For each service you'll see: **what it is**, a **simple analogy**, **when to use it**, **when NOT to use it**, **exam keywords**, **similar services**, and **two short exam-style questions**.

## 3.1 Ways to interact with AWS

You can use AWS through several entry points:

| Method | What it is |
|--------|-----------|
| **AWS Management Console** | The web-based graphical interface (click around in a browser). |
| **AWS CLI** | Command-line interface — type commands to control AWS (good for scripting/automation). |
| **AWS SDKs** | Software Development Kits — control AWS from inside code (Python, Java, JavaScript, etc.). |
| **Infrastructure as Code (IaC)** | Define infrastructure in template files (e.g., **AWS CloudFormation**) so it's repeatable and version-controlled. |

> **Exam Tip:** "Provision infrastructure in a repeatable, automated way from a template" -> **CloudFormation / Infrastructure as Code**. "Automate from the terminal" -> **AWS CLI**. "Control AWS from within my application code" -> **AWS SDK**.

## 3.2 The AWS Global Infrastructure

This is foundational and frequently tested.

| Term | Definition |
|------|-----------|
| **Region** | A geographic area (e.g., us-east-1 in N. Virginia, ap-southeast-1 in Singapore). Contains multiple Availability Zones. You choose Regions based on **latency, cost, compliance/data residency, and service availability**. |
| **Availability Zone (AZ)** | One or more discrete data centers within a Region, with independent power and networking. Each Region has **multiple AZs** (usually 3+). Spreading across AZs gives **high availability**. |
| **Edge Location** | Sites used by **Amazon CloudFront** (CDN) and Route 53 to cache content close to users for low latency. There are **far more** edge locations than Regions. |
| **Local Zones / Wavelength / Outposts** | Bring AWS closer to users or on-premises for very low latency (know they exist). |

> **Simple analogy:** A **Region** is a city. An **Availability Zone** is a separate building (data center) in that city with its own power and internet. **Edge locations** are convenience kiosks scattered everywhere to serve customers fast.

> **Common Trap:** "How do I make my application highly available?" -> deploy across **multiple Availability Zones** (not just multiple instances in one AZ). "Serve content to global users with low latency" -> **edge locations / CloudFront**. "Comply with data-residency laws" -> choose the right **Region**.

## 3.3 Compute services

### Amazon EC2 (Elastic Compute Cloud)

- **What it is:** Resizable **virtual servers** in the cloud. You pick an OS, instance size, and pay for what you run. The classic **IaaS** building block.
- **Analogy:** Renting a computer in AWS's data center that you fully control.
- **When to use:** You need full control of the OS; running traditional apps, custom software, or anything that needs a persistent server.
- **When NOT to use:** Short event-driven tasks (use **Lambda**), or when you don't want to manage servers at all.
- **Exam keywords:** "virtual server," "instances," "full control of OS," "IaaS."
- **Similar services:** Lambda (serverless), Elastic Beanstalk (managed deploy), Lightsail (simple VPS).

**EC2 instance families (beginner level):** Instances come in families optimized for different jobs:

| Family type | Best for |
|-------------|----------|
| **General purpose** | Balanced CPU/memory — web servers, dev/test. |
| **Compute optimized** | CPU-heavy work — batch processing, gaming servers. |
| **Memory optimized** | Big in-memory datasets — databases, analytics. |
| **Storage optimized** | High disk throughput — big data, data warehouses. |
| **Accelerated computing** | GPUs — machine learning, graphics. |

**EC2 purchasing options** (also relevant to Domain 4): On-Demand, Reserved Instances, Savings Plans, Spot Instances, Dedicated Hosts. (Covered in Domain 4.)

> **Exam Tip:** "Need a virtual machine where I install my own software and manage the OS" -> **EC2**.

*Practice:*
1. A company needs a virtual server with full control over the operating system to host a legacy application. Which service? **-> Amazon EC2.**
2. Which EC2 instance family is best for a workload that needs large amounts of RAM for an in-memory database? **-> Memory optimized.**

### EC2 Auto Scaling

- **What it is:** Automatically **adds or removes EC2 instances** based on demand (e.g., CPU load or a schedule).
- **Analogy:** A restaurant that calls in more waiters at lunch rush and sends them home when it's quiet.
- **When to use:** Variable traffic; you want to match capacity to demand (this delivers **elasticity** and helps **cost optimization**).
- **When NOT to use:** A fixed, predictable single-server workload.
- **Exam keywords:** "automatically scale," "match demand," "add/remove instances," "elasticity."
- **Similar services:** Often paired with **Elastic Load Balancing**.

*Practice:*
1. Traffic to a web app spikes during the day and drops at night; the company wants to automatically match EC2 capacity to demand. Which service? **-> EC2 Auto Scaling.**
2. Auto Scaling primarily helps achieve which cloud property and which Well-Architected goal? **-> Elasticity; cost optimization (and reliability).**

### Elastic Load Balancing (ELB)

- **What it is:** Automatically **distributes incoming traffic** across multiple targets (e.g., EC2 instances) in one or more AZs.
- **Analogy:** A host at a restaurant directing guests evenly to available tables.
- **When to use:** Spread load across servers; improve **high availability** and fault tolerance.
- **When NOT to use:** A single instance with no need for distribution.
- **Exam keywords:** "distribute traffic," "across multiple instances/AZs," "high availability."
- **Similar services:** Works with Auto Scaling; Route 53 (DNS-level routing) is different.

*Practice:*
1. A company wants to distribute web traffic evenly across several EC2 instances in two AZs. Which service? **-> Elastic Load Balancing.**
2. ELB + Auto Scaling together primarily improve which two properties? **-> High availability and elasticity.**

### AWS Lambda

- **What it is:** **Serverless** compute — run your code in response to events **without managing servers**. You pay only for the compute time used (per request/duration).
- **Analogy:** A vending machine for code: it runs only when someone presses the button, and you pay per use.
- **When to use:** Event-driven tasks, short functions, automation, APIs (with API Gateway), unpredictable/occasional workloads. Low operational overhead.
- **When NOT to use:** Long-running processes (functions have a time limit), or apps needing a persistent OS.
- **Exam keywords:** "serverless," "run code without provisioning servers," "event-driven," "pay per execution."
- **Similar services:** EC2 (you manage servers), Fargate (serverless containers).

> **Common Trap:** "Serverless" + "run code" almost always = **Lambda**. If it says "no servers to manage and pay only when code runs," it's Lambda.

*Practice:*
1. A developer wants to run a small function whenever a file is uploaded to S3, without managing any servers. Which service? **-> AWS Lambda.**
2. Which pricing characteristic best fits Lambda? **-> Pay only for the compute time consumed (per invocation/duration).**

### AWS Elastic Beanstalk

- **What it is:** A **PaaS** that automatically handles deployment, capacity, load balancing, and scaling for your web app — you **just upload your code**.
- **Analogy:** A managed apartment where you bring your furniture (code) and the building handles utilities, maintenance, and scaling.
- **When to use:** You want to deploy a web app quickly without managing the underlying infrastructure, but still want it built on EC2/ELB under the hood.
- **When NOT to use:** You need fine-grained control of every infrastructure detail (use raw EC2/CloudFormation), or fully serverless event functions (Lambda).
- **Exam keywords:** "just upload your code," "AWS handles deployment and scaling," "PaaS," "quickly deploy a web application."
- **Similar services:** CloudFormation (IaC, more control), Lambda (serverless functions).

*Practice:*
1. A team wants to deploy a Python web app and let AWS handle provisioning, load balancing, and scaling automatically. Which service? **-> AWS Elastic Beanstalk.**
2. Elastic Beanstalk is an example of which cloud service model? **-> PaaS.**

### Containers: Amazon ECS, Amazon EKS, AWS Fargate

- **Amazon ECS (Elastic Container Service):** AWS's own service to run and manage **Docker containers**.
- **Amazon EKS (Elastic Kubernetes Service):** Managed **Kubernetes** — use this when you specifically want/already use Kubernetes.
- **AWS Fargate:** **Serverless compute for containers** — runs ECS/EKS containers **without you managing the underlying servers**.
- **Analogy:** Containers are standardized shipping boxes for apps. ECS/EKS are the port that schedules them; Fargate means you don't own or manage the trucks (servers) hauling them.
- **When to use:** Containerized apps. Use **EKS** if you need Kubernetes; **ECS** for simpler AWS-native container orchestration; **Fargate** when you don't want to manage the servers behind your containers.
- **Exam keywords:** "containers / Docker" -> ECS/EKS; "Kubernetes" -> **EKS**; "run containers serverless / no servers to manage" -> **Fargate**.

> **Common Trap:** "Kubernetes" is the giveaway for **EKS**. "Run containers without managing servers/EC2" is **Fargate**.

*Practice:*
1. A company already uses Kubernetes and wants a managed version on AWS. Which service? **-> Amazon EKS.**
2. A team wants to run containers without provisioning or managing any EC2 instances. Which service? **-> AWS Fargate.**

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

## 3.4 Storage services

### Amazon S3 (Simple Storage Service)

- **What it is:** **Object storage** for any amount of data (files, images, backups, static websites). Virtually unlimited, highly durable (designed for 11 nines of durability), accessed over the internet via APIs.
- **Analogy:** An infinite online drive where each file is an "object" in a "bucket."
- **When to use:** Storing files/objects, backups, data lakes, static website hosting, media, logs.
- **When NOT to use:** As a hard drive attached to an EC2 instance (use **EBS**), or as a shared file system mounted by many servers (use **EFS**). S3 is **not** block storage and isn't for operating systems.
- **Exam keywords:** "object storage," "buckets," "static website hosting," "virtually unlimited," "durable."
- **Similar services:** EBS (block), EFS (file).

**S3 storage classes** (matching cost to access frequency):

| Class | Best for |
|-------|----------|
| **S3 Standard** | Frequently accessed data. |
| **S3 Intelligent-Tiering** | Unknown/changing access patterns — auto-moves data to save cost. |
| **S3 Standard-IA (Infrequent Access)** | Less-frequent access, but rapid when needed. |
| **S3 One Zone-IA** | Infrequent access, stored in a single AZ (cheaper, less resilient). |
| **S3 Glacier Instant Retrieval** | Archive needing millisecond retrieval. |
| **S3 Glacier Flexible Retrieval** | Archive, retrieval in minutes to hours. |
| **S3 Glacier Deep Archive** | Lowest cost; long-term archive; retrieval in hours. |

**S3 lifecycle policies** automatically move objects to cheaper classes (or delete them) as they age — a key **cost optimization** tool.

> **Exam Tip:** "Cheapest storage for data you rarely access and can wait hours to retrieve" -> **S3 Glacier Deep Archive**. "Access pattern is unknown/changing, optimize cost automatically" -> **S3 Intelligent-Tiering**.

*Practice:*
1. A company needs to store millions of images and serve them over the web, with virtually unlimited capacity. Which service? **-> Amazon S3.**
2. Which S3 storage class minimizes cost for long-term archives that are rarely retrieved and can tolerate hours-long retrieval? **-> S3 Glacier Deep Archive.**

### Amazon EBS (Elastic Block Store)

- **What it is:** **Block storage** volumes (virtual hard drives) you attach to a **single EC2 instance**. Persists independently of the instance lifecycle; supports snapshots.
- **Analogy:** A hard drive plugged into one specific computer.
- **When to use:** The disk for an EC2 instance — OS, databases, anything needing low-latency block storage attached to one server.
- **When NOT to use:** Sharing one volume across many instances at once (use **EFS**), or storing standalone objects (use **S3**).
- **Exam keywords:** "block storage," "volume," "attached to EC2," "snapshot."
- **Similar services:** S3 (object), EFS (shared file), instance store (temporary).

> **Common Trap:** A standard EBS volume attaches to **one** EC2 instance in **one** AZ. If a question needs **many** instances to share files simultaneously, that's **EFS**, not EBS.

*Practice:*
1. An EC2 instance needs a persistent virtual disk for its operating system and database files. Which service? **-> Amazon EBS.**
2. Can a single standard EBS volume be mounted by dozens of EC2 instances at the same time for shared access? **-> No; use Amazon EFS for shared file access.**

### Amazon EFS (Elastic File System)

- **What it is:** A **fully managed, shared file system** that **many EC2 instances** (Linux) can mount **at the same time**. Scales automatically.
- **Analogy:** A shared network drive the whole team mounts at once.
- **When to use:** Multiple instances need shared access to the same files; content management, shared home directories.
- **When NOT to use:** Single-instance block storage (EBS) or object storage (S3); Windows file shares (use **FSx**).
- **Exam keywords:** "shared file system," "mounted by multiple instances," "scales automatically," "NFS."
- **Similar services:** EBS (single-attach), FSx (Windows/specialized), S3 (objects).

*Practice:*
1. Several EC2 instances must read and write the same set of files simultaneously. Which storage service? **-> Amazon EFS.**
2. Which is shared across many instances by design: EBS or EFS? **-> EFS.**

### Amazon FSx

- **What it is:** Fully managed **third-party file systems**, most notably **FSx for Windows File Server** (SMB/Windows shares) and **FSx for Lustre** (high-performance computing).
- **Analogy:** A managed, brand-name filing system (Windows or high-performance) without you running the file server.
- **When to use:** You need a **Windows-native file share** or **high-performance** file system.
- **Exam keywords:** "Windows file server," "SMB," "Lustre," "high-performance file system."

*Practice:*
1. A company needs a managed Windows-native (SMB) file share. Which service? **-> Amazon FSx (for Windows File Server).**
2. Which service targets high-performance computing file systems? **-> Amazon FSx (for Lustre).**

### Amazon S3 Glacier

- **What it is:** Low-cost **archival** storage (now offered as S3 Glacier storage classes). For data you rarely access but must retain.
- **Analogy:** A deep, cheap long-term warehouse — slow to fetch, very cheap to store.
- **When to use:** Backups, compliance archives, records you must keep for years.
- **When NOT to use:** Data you need instantly and frequently (use S3 Standard).
- **Exam keywords:** "archive," "long-term retention," "lowest cost," "rarely accessed."

*Practice:*
1. A hospital must retain records for 10 years but rarely accesses them and wants the lowest cost. Which option? **-> S3 Glacier Deep Archive.**
2. Glacier trades low cost for what? **-> Slower retrieval times.**

### AWS Backup

- **What it is:** A **centralized backup** service to automate and manage backups across many AWS services (EBS, RDS, DynamoDB, EFS, and more).
- **Analogy:** One control panel that schedules and manages all your backups in one place.
- **When to use:** Centralized, policy-based backups across multiple services for compliance.
- **Exam keywords:** "centralized backup," "automate backups," "across services," "backup policy."

*Practice:*
1. A company wants to centrally automate and manage backups for EBS, RDS, and DynamoDB. Which service? **-> AWS Backup.**
2. AWS Backup helps meet which kind of requirement? **-> Compliance/retention via centralized, automated backups.**

## 3.5 Database services

### Amazon RDS (Relational Database Service)

- **What it is:** **Managed relational (SQL) databases** — AWS handles patching, backups, and high availability. Engines include MySQL, PostgreSQL, MariaDB, Oracle, SQL Server (and Amazon Aurora).
- **Analogy:** A relational database with a hired DBA (AWS) handling the maintenance chores.
- **When to use:** Traditional relational/SQL workloads where you want AWS to manage the database admin work; structured data with relationships, transactions.
- **When NOT to use:** Massive-scale key-value/NoSQL needs (use **DynamoDB**); data warehousing/analytics at scale (use **Redshift**); when you need full OS control of the DB host (then self-manage on EC2).
- **Exam keywords:** "managed relational database," "SQL," "MySQL/PostgreSQL/Oracle/SQL Server," "automated backups/patching."
- **Similar services:** Aurora (AWS's high-performance relational engine), DynamoDB (NoSQL), Redshift (warehouse).

> **Multi-AZ deployments** of RDS provide **high availability** (a standby copy in another AZ). **Read replicas** improve read performance/scaling.

*Practice:*
1. A company wants a managed MySQL database without handling backups and patching themselves. Which service? **-> Amazon RDS.**
2. How does RDS provide high availability? **-> Multi-AZ deployment with a standby in another Availability Zone.**

### Amazon Aurora

- **What it is:** AWS's **high-performance, fully managed relational** database, compatible with **MySQL and PostgreSQL**, with greater performance and built-in replication/durability than standard RDS engines.
- **Analogy:** A turbocharged, AWS-built version of MySQL/PostgreSQL.
- **When to use:** Relational workloads needing higher performance, scalability, and availability than standard RDS engines, while staying MySQL/PostgreSQL-compatible.
- **Exam keywords:** "MySQL/PostgreSQL compatible," "high performance," "fully managed relational," "Aurora Serverless."

*Practice:*
1. A team wants a MySQL-compatible relational database with much higher performance and availability than standard RDS MySQL. Which service? **-> Amazon Aurora.**
2. Aurora is compatible with which two open-source engines? **-> MySQL and PostgreSQL.**

### Amazon DynamoDB

- **What it is:** A **fully managed, serverless NoSQL** key-value and document database with single-digit-millisecond performance at any scale.
- **Analogy:** An infinitely scalable digital dictionary — give it a key, get a value, instantly.
- **When to use:** High-scale NoSQL needs, key-value/document data, serverless apps, gaming leaderboards, shopping carts, IoT. No servers to manage; automatic scaling.
- **When NOT to use:** Complex relational joins/SQL transactions across tables (use RDS/Aurora); analytics over huge datasets (Redshift).
- **Exam keywords:** "NoSQL," "key-value," "document database," "serverless," "single-digit-millisecond," "any scale."
- **Similar services:** RDS/Aurora (relational), ElastiCache (in-memory cache).

> **Common Trap:** "NoSQL" or "key-value" -> **DynamoDB**. "Relational / SQL / managed MySQL" -> **RDS/Aurora**. Don't pick RDS for a NoSQL question.

*Practice:*
1. A serverless app needs a NoSQL key-value store with millisecond latency at massive scale. Which service? **-> Amazon DynamoDB.**
2. DynamoDB requires you to manage the underlying servers. True or false? **-> False; it's fully managed/serverless.**

### Amazon Redshift

- **What it is:** A **fully managed data warehouse** for **analytics** on large volumes of structured data using SQL.
- **Analogy:** A giant analytics engine for running big reports across mountains of data.
- **When to use:** Business intelligence, complex analytical queries (OLAP), aggregating data from many sources for reporting.
- **When NOT to use:** Day-to-day transactional app database (use RDS/Aurora); simple key-value lookups (DynamoDB).
- **Exam keywords:** "data warehouse," "analytics," "business intelligence," "OLAP," "petabyte-scale reporting."

> **Common Trap:** **RDS vs. Redshift.** RDS = transactional app database (OLTP). Redshift = analytics/data warehouse (OLAP). "Run complex analytical queries / reporting / data warehouse" -> **Redshift**.

*Practice:*
1. A company wants to run complex analytical queries across years of sales data for BI dashboards. Which service? **-> Amazon Redshift.**
2. Is Redshift designed for transactional app workloads or analytics? **-> Analytics (data warehousing / OLAP).**

### Amazon ElastiCache

- **What it is:** A **managed in-memory cache** (Redis or Memcached) to speed up applications by storing frequently accessed data in memory.
- **Analogy:** Keeping your most-used items on your desk instead of walking to the storeroom each time.
- **When to use:** Reduce database load and latency; caching, session stores, real-time leaderboards.
- **When NOT to use:** As your primary durable database.
- **Exam keywords:** "in-memory cache," "Redis/Memcached," "reduce latency," "cache frequently accessed data."

*Practice:*
1. An app's database is overloaded by repeated identical reads; the team wants an in-memory cache to speed responses. Which service? **-> Amazon ElastiCache.**
2. ElastiCache supports which two engines? **-> Redis and Memcached.**

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

## 3.6 Networking and content delivery

### Amazon VPC (Virtual Private Cloud)

- **What it is:** Your **own private, isolated network** inside AWS where you launch resources. You control IP ranges, subnets, and routing.
- **Analogy:** Your own gated neighborhood inside AWS — you decide the streets (subnets) and who can come in or out.
- **When to use:** Essentially always — it's the network foundation for EC2, RDS, etc. Use it to isolate and secure resources.
- **Exam keywords:** "isolated network," "private network in AWS," "control IP ranges and subnets."

**VPC building blocks (know the basics):**

| Component | Plain meaning |
|-----------|---------------|
| **Subnet** | A subdivision of the VPC's IP range. **Public subnet** = can reach the internet; **private subnet** = no direct internet access. |
| **Route table** | Rules that decide where network traffic goes. |
| **Internet Gateway (IGW)** | The door that lets a VPC communicate with the public internet. |
| **NAT Gateway** | Lets resources in a **private** subnet reach the internet **outbound** (e.g., for updates) without being publicly reachable inbound. |
| **Security group** | A **virtual firewall at the instance level**. **Stateful.** Allow rules only. |
| **Network ACL (NACL)** | A firewall at the **subnet level**. **Stateless.** Allow *and* deny rules. |

> **Common Trap (very common): Security group vs. Network ACL.**
> - **Security group** = instance level, **stateful** (return traffic auto-allowed), supports **allow** rules only.
