- A. Encryption in transit
- B. Encryption at rest
- C. A denial-of-service control
- D. A load-balancing feature

**Q65.** Which combination BEST represents the AWS Cloud value proposition?

- A. Higher upfront costs but more control
- B. Pay-as-you-go pricing, global reach, elasticity, and reduced undifferentiated work
- C. Guaranteed zero downtime and zero cost
- D. Eliminating all security responsibilities for the customer

### Mock Exam 1 — Answers & Explanations (Q1–Q33)

**Q1 -> B.** *(D1 — Cloud benefits.)* Trading fixed (CapEx) for variable (OpEx) expense is a core benefit. A, C, D are false.

**Q2 -> B.** *(D3 — S3 classes.)* S3 Standard = frequent access, ms latency, high durability. Glacier is archival; EBS/EFS classes here don't fit "objects."

**Q3 -> B.** *(D2 — CloudTrail.)* CloudTrail logs API activity (who/when/where). CloudWatch = metrics; Config = config history; Trusted Advisor = recommendations.

**Q4 -> A and C.** *(D2 — Shared Responsibility.)* Customers encrypt their data and manage IAM. B, D, E are AWS responsibilities.

**Q5 -> B.** *(D3 — Lambda.)* Lambda runs event-driven code with no servers. EC2/ECS-on-EC2 need server management; Batch is for large batch compute.

**Q6 -> C.** *(D4 — EC2 pricing.)* A 3-year all-upfront Reserved Instance gives the deepest steady-state discount. Spot is cheap but interruptible; no-commitment Savings Plans give less discount.

**Q7 -> B.** *(D3 — VPC.)* VPC is your isolated private network in AWS.

**Q8 -> B.** *(D2 — GuardDuty.)* GuardDuty analyzes CloudTrail/DNS/VPC flow logs for threats. Inspector = vuln scans; WAF = web filtering; Shield = DDoS.

**Q9 -> C.** *(D4 — Pricing Calculator.)* Estimate before deploying = Pricing Calculator.

**Q10 -> B.** *(D3 — Global infrastructure.)* An AZ = one or more discrete data centers with redundant power/networking. Regions have multiple AZs.

**Q11 -> B.** *(D2 — IAM groups.)* Attach policies to a group to permission many users at once.

**Q12 -> C.** *(D3 — DynamoDB.)* DynamoDB is managed NoSQL. RDS/Aurora = relational; Redshift = warehouse.

**Q13 -> B.** *(D3 — ELB.)* ELB distributes traffic and improves availability.

**Q14 -> D.** *(D4 — Support plans.)* Enterprise = designated TAM + 15-min critical response.

**Q15 -> B.** *(D2 — WAF.)* WAF uses rules to block common web exploits. Shield = DDoS; Macie = PII; KMS = keys.

**Q16 -> B.** *(D3 — VPN.)* Site-to-Site VPN = encrypted tunnel over the internet. Direct Connect is private/not internet.

**Q17 -> A.** *(D1 — Well-Architected.)* Reliability = recover from failure and meet demand.

**Q18 -> B.** *(D3 — CloudFront.)* CloudFront caches at edge locations to cut latency.

**Q19 -> B.** *(D4 — Budgets.)* Budgets alerts on actual/forecasted thresholds.

**Q20 -> C.** *(D2 — IAM roles.)* Attach an IAM role to the instance. Embedding keys/root is insecure.

**Q21 -> B.** *(D3 — Redshift.)* Redshift = analytics/data warehouse.

**Q22 -> A and C.** *(D3 — Regions.)* Each Region is a separate geographic area; you choose one for latency/residency. B, D, E are false.

**Q23 -> A.** *(D3 — RDS.)* RDS reduces the work of running relational databases.

**Q24 -> B.** *(D3 — EFS.)* EFS = shared file storage for many Linux instances. EBS = one instance; S3 = objects; FSx for Windows = Windows.

**Q25 -> B.** *(D3/D4 — Trusted Advisor.)* Trusted Advisor gives best-practice checks across multiple categories.

**Q26 -> B.** *(D1 — Fault tolerance.)* Keep operating without interruption during a failure.

**Q27 -> B.** *(D2 — Secrets Manager.)* Stores and rotates secrets. KMS = keys; Artifact = reports; Inspector = scans.

**Q28 -> B.** *(D4 — Consolidated billing.)* Organizations gives one bill + volume discounts.

**Q29 -> B.** *(D3 — Athena.)* Athena = serverless SQL on S3. Redshift needs loading; Glue is ETL.

**Q30 -> B.** *(D3 — Internet Gateway.)* An IGW lets public-subnet resources reach the internet. NAT is for private-subnet outbound only.

**Q31 -> B.** *(D1 — Scalability.)* Ability to increase/decrease capacity to meet demand.

**Q32 -> A.** *(D2 — Macie.)* Macie finds sensitive data (PII) in S3.

**Q33 -> B.** *(D3 — Elastic Beanstalk.)* Upload code; AWS handles provisioning/scaling. CloudFormation is IaC templates; Lambda is functions.

### Mock Exam 1 — Answers & Explanations (Q34–Q65)

**Q34 -> C.** *(D4 — Spot.)* Stop/resume batch work = Spot, the cheapest interruptible option.

**Q35 -> B.** *(D2 — Shield Standard.)* Shield Standard gives automatic DDoS protection at no extra cost.

**Q36 -> B.** *(D2 — Artifact.)* Artifact provides compliance reports like SOC 2.

**Q37 -> B.** *(D4 — Lambda pricing.)* You pay per request + compute time used.

**Q38 -> B.** *(D3 — Step Functions.)* Step Functions orchestrate serverless workflows with visual steps.

**Q39 -> A.** *(D3 — CloudFormation.)* Infrastructure as code via version-controlled templates.

**Q40 -> B.** *(D1 — Economies of scale.)* AWS's scale lowers pay-as-you-go prices.

**Q41 -> A and C.** *(D1/D3 — Availability.)* ELB and Auto Scaling improve availability. Budgets/Artifact/Macie don't.

**Q42 -> B.** *(D3 — NACL.)* NACL = subnet level, stateless, can deny. Security groups are stateful, allow-only.

**Q43 -> B.** *(D3 — EKS.)* EKS = managed Kubernetes. ECS is AWS's own orchestrator; Fargate is the serverless launch type.

**Q44 -> B.** *(D3 — ElastiCache.)* In-memory cache for fast repeated reads.

**Q45 -> B.** *(Exam mechanics.)* Compensatory scoring — only the overall score (700/1000) matters.

**Q46 -> B.** *(D2 — SCPs.)* SCPs set permission guardrails across accounts in Organizations.

**Q47 -> B.** *(D3 — Route 53.)* DNS routing + domain registration.

**Q48 -> B.** *(D3 — EBS.)* EBS = block storage for a single instance.

**Q49 -> B.** *(D4 — CUR.)* The Cost and Usage Report is the most granular billing data.

**Q50 -> A.** *(D1 — Well-Architected.)* Performance Efficiency = use resources efficiently.

**Q51 -> B.** *(D3 — DMS.)* Database Migration Service migrates databases with minimal downtime. Snowball is for bulk file transfer.

**Q52 -> B.** *(D2 — MFA.)* MFA requires a second verification factor at sign-in.

**Q53 -> B.** *(D4 — Cost Explorer.)* Visualize history and forecast by service/tag/account.

**Q54 -> A.** *(D3 — Storage Gateway.)* Storage Gateway extends on-prem storage to AWS (hybrid).

**Q55 -> A.** *(D1 — Agility.)* Spinning up resources fast to experiment = agility.

**Q56 -> A and C.** *(D3 — Serverless.)* Lambda and DynamoDB are serverless. EC2, single-instance RDS, and EBS are not.

**Q57 -> B.** *(D2/D3 — CloudWatch.)* Collects metrics, triggers alarms/notifications.

**Q58 -> A.** *(D3 — Control Tower.)* Sets up a governed multi-account landing zone.

**Q59 -> B.** *(D1 — High availability.)* Remain operational with minimal downtime.

**Q60 -> A.** *(D2 — Inspector.)* Inspector scans instances/images for vulnerabilities. GuardDuty analyzes logs for threats.

**Q61 -> B.** *(D3 — SQS.)* SQS is a managed message queue for decoupling.

**Q62 -> B.** *(D4 — Free Tier.)* "Always free" services have a free amount that never expires.

**Q63 -> B.** *(D3 — Glue.)* Glue is managed ETL. Athena queries; QuickSight visualizes; Redshift warehouses.

**Q64 -> B.** *(D2 — Encryption.)* Data encrypted while stored = encryption at rest.

**Q65 -> B.** *(D1 — Value proposition.)* Pay-as-you-go, global reach, elasticity, less undifferentiated heavy lifting.

> **How did you do?** Count your correct answers out of 65. **Roughly 46+/65 (about 70%)** is passing territory, but aim higher on practice tests to leave a safety margin. Review every miss before moving on.

## Quiz 4 — Full Mock Exam 2 (65 questions)

Harder, more scenario-driven. Again: **90 minutes, closed book.**

**Q1.** A company runs a critical app across two Availability Zones with a load balancer. One AZ goes down but users notice no disruption. Which design property does this demonstrate?

- A. Elasticity
- B. High availability
- C. Vendor lock-in
- D. Economies of scale

- C. Amazon EventBridge

- E. Disable CloudTrail to save money

### Mock Exam 2 — Answers & Explanations (Q1–Q33)

**Q1 -> B.** *(D1 — HA.)* No disruption when an AZ fails = high availability via multi-AZ.

**Q2 -> B.** *(D2 — IAM roles.)* A scoped IAM role gives temporary, limited access without long-term keys.

**Q3 -> B.** *(D3 — SQS.)* SQS buffers messages so none are lost if the consumer is briefly down. SNS pushes; it doesn't store for pull-based processing the same way.

**Q4 -> C.** *(D4 — pricing.)* Predictable 24/7 multi-year = Savings Plan or Reserved Instances.

**Q5 -> A and C.** *(D2 — best practices.)* Enable root MFA and use least privilege. B, D, E are insecure.

**Q6 -> B.** *(D3 — ElastiCache.)* Microsecond/in-memory, regenerable = ElastiCache.

**Q7 -> B.** *(D2 — CloudTrail.)* CloudTrail shows who deleted the bucket (API history).

**Q8 -> B.** *(D3 — Fargate.)* ECS/EKS on Fargate runs containers with no servers/clusters to manage.

**Q9 -> B.** *(D2 — SCPs.)* SCPs restrict which services member accounts can use.

**Q10 -> B.** *(D4 — Cost Explorer.)* Analyze and compare spend across linked accounts.

**Q11 -> B.** *(D3 — S3 durability.)* S3 stores data redundantly across multiple devices/facilities for very high durability.

**Q12 -> A.** *(D3 — Regions/residency.)* Choose the Region in the required country.

**Q13 -> B.** *(D3 — QuickSight.)* Managed BI dashboards for business users.

**Q14 -> B.** *(D2 — WAF.)* WAF stops SQL injection; Shield handles DDoS, not injection.

**Q15 -> B.** *(D1 — scaling.)* Vertical = bigger instance; horizontal = more instances.

**Q16 -> B.** *(D3 — Redshift.)* Petabyte SQL analytics in a warehouse = Redshift.

**Q17 -> A.** *(D2 — IAM policies.)* Policies are JSON permission documents.

**Q18 -> B.** *(D4 — Budgets.)* Budgets alerts on forecasted/actual thresholds.

**Q19 -> B.** *(D3 — FSx.)* FSx for Windows File Server = managed Windows file system.

**Q20 -> B.** *(D1/D3 — multi-Region.)* Surviving a Region issue requires a multi-Region design.

**Q21 -> A and C.** *(D3 — Lambda.)* Lambda suits no-server management and pay-per-run. B, D, E describe EC2 needs.

**Q22 -> A.** *(D2 — Config.)* Config records config changes and flags non-compliance.

**Q23 -> A.** *(D2 — encryption in transit.)* A TLS/SSL cert (via ACM) encrypts traffic to the site.

**Q24 -> B.** *(D3 — DynamoDB.)* Managed, scalable NoSQL for profiles.

**Q25 -> B.** *(D3/D4 — Trusted Advisor.)* It warns as you near service quotas.

**Q26 -> B.** *(D3 — RDS Multi-AZ.)* Managed relational DB with automatic cross-AZ replication.

**Q27 -> B.** *(D3 — edge locations.)* Edge locations are separate sites closer to users, used by CloudFront.

**Q28 -> A.** *(D3 — Snow Family.)* Multi-petabyte + months over network = ship a Snow device.

**Q29 -> A.** *(D2 — GuardDuty.)* ML-based detection of unusual activity/compromised credentials.

**Q30 -> B.** *(D1 — loose coupling.)* Components talk via interfaces/queues so failures don't cascade.

**Q31 -> A.** *(D3 — AWS Backup.)* Centralized backups across multiple services.

**Q32 -> B.** *(D4 — support plans.)* Developer = business-hours associate guidance (lowest paid tier above Basic).

**Q33 -> B.** *(D3 — S3 Standard-IA.)* Infrequent access + instant retrieval = Standard-IA.

### Mock Exam 2 — Answers & Explanations (Q34–Q65)

**Q34 -> A.** *(D3 — Elastic Beanstalk.)* Deploy code; AWS handles capacity/scaling/health. CloudFormation provisions from templates but you define everything.

**Q35 -> B.** *(D1 — OpEx.)* A monthly usage bill is OpEx; buying hardware is CapEx.

**Q36 -> A.** *(D2 — Security Hub.)* Aggregates findings across GuardDuty/Inspector/Macie.

**Q37 -> A and C.** *(D4 — Spot.)* Spot can be interrupted and is heavily discounted. It doesn't guarantee capacity or require a 3-year term.

**Q38 -> B.** *(D3 — Direct Connect.)* Private, consistent, high-bandwidth = Direct Connect.

**Q39 -> A.** *(D4 — Cost Allocation Tags.)* Tags group costs by project/department.

**Q40 -> B.** *(D3 — CloudFront.)* Fast global delivery of video/static content.

**Q41 -> A.** *(D2 — Artifact.)* Official compliance reports/certifications come from Artifact.

**Q42 -> B.** *(D3 — Fargate.)* Serverless compute engine for containers.

**Q43 -> B.** *(D3 — SNS.)* SNS fans out one message to many endpoints (email, SMS, SQS).

**Q44 -> B.** *(D1/D4 — Cost Optimization.)* Right-sizing + Savings Plans optimize cost.

**Q45 -> B.** *(D3 — Athena.)* Serverless SQL on S3 log files.

**Q46 -> A.** *(D2 — Organizations.)* Separate accounts under one org limit blast radius and consolidate billing.

**Q47 -> B.** *(D2 — Shared Responsibility / managed services.)* For RDS, AWS patches OS/engine; customer handles data/access/config.

**Q48 -> B.** *(D3 — CloudFront.)* Edge-cache static assets to speed global reads.

**Q49 -> A.** *(D2 — KMS.)* KMS manages keys and key policies for envelope encryption.

**Q50 -> A and B.** *(D1/D3 — Auto Scaling.)* It adds capacity on spikes and removes it when idle. It doesn't encrypt, protect from DDoS, or do DNS.

**Q51 -> A.** *(D1 — Agility.)* Quickly experiment and ship faster.

**Q52 -> A.** *(D3 — CloudFormation.)* Define and recreate the whole stack from a template.

**Q53 -> A.** *(D2 — ACM.)* ACM provisions/manages TLS certificates automatically.

**Q54 -> B.** *(D4 — Budgets.)* Set budget alerts to catch overspending early.

**Q55 -> A.** *(D3 — Glue.)* Managed ETL + data catalog.

**Q56 -> A.** *(D3 — ElastiCache.)* Sub-ms cache that can be rebuilt from the source.

**Q57 -> B.** *(D2 — Shield.)* Standard = automatic baseline DDoS (free); Advanced = paid, enhanced + support.

**Q58 -> A.** *(D1 — Sustainability.)* Directly targets environmental/carbon impact.

**Q59 -> A.** *(D2 — cross-account role.)* A cross-account IAM role grants scoped access without new users or public exposure.

**Q60 -> A.** *(D4 — Trusted Advisor.)* Flags underutilized/idle resources to cut cost.

**Q61 -> A.** *(D3 — SG vs NACL.)* Security group filters at the instance; NACL filters at the subnet.

**Q62 -> A.** *(D3 — Kinesis.)* Kinesis ingests/processes streaming data in real time.

**Q63 -> A and B.** *(D4 — Free Tier.)* It has "12 months free" and "always free" offers. It is not all-free-forever or enterprise-only.

**Q64 -> A.** *(D3 — Storage Gateway.)* Hybrid bridge from on-prem storage into AWS.

**Q65 -> B.** *(D3 — Lambda + API Gateway.)* Serverless API that scales to zero = least overhead.

> **Compare your two mock scores.** Improving from Mock 1 to Mock 2 means your weak areas are closing. If a specific domain keeps costing you points, reread that chapter and redo its mini quiz before attempting the Final Readiness Exam.

## Quiz 5 — Final Readiness Exam (65 questions)

This is your "am I ready?" checkpoint. **90 minutes, closed book, no breaks.** If you comfortably clear ~80% here, you're in great shape to book the real exam.

**Q1.** Which of the following BEST defines cloud computing?

- A. Buying and maintaining your own servers
- B. On-demand delivery of IT resources over the internet with pay-as-you-go pricing
- C. A type of database
- D. A physical networking cable

- E. Classifying the customer's data

### Final Readiness Exam — Answers & Explanations (Q1–Q33)

**Q1 -> B.** *(D1.)* On-demand IT over the internet, pay-as-you-go = cloud computing.
**Q2 -> B.** *(D3.)* EC2 = virtual servers.
**Q3 -> A and C.** *(D2.)* AWS handles physical security and global infrastructure; B, D, E are customer duties.
**Q4 -> B.** *(D3.)* S3 supports lifecycle rules to tier objects to cheaper storage.
**Q5 -> A.** *(D3/D4.)* Trusted Advisor gives multi-category recommendations.
**Q6 -> B.** *(D3.)* Lambda runs on events with no idle cost.
**Q7 -> B.** *(D3.)* Route 53 = DNS + domain registration.
**Q8 -> B.** *(D1.)* Elasticity = auto scale to demand.
**Q9 -> A.** *(D4.)* Budgets = spending alerts.
**Q10 -> B.** *(D3.)* RDS = managed relational DB.
**Q11 -> B.** *(D2.)* Shield = DDoS protection.
**Q12 -> B.** *(D2.)* Roles = temporary creds, great for services/cross-account.
**Q13 -> C.** *(D4.)* Pricing Calculator = estimate before building.
**Q14 -> A.** *(D3.)* EFS = shared file storage for many Linux instances.
**Q15 -> B.** *(D2.)* CloudTrail = API auditing.
**Q16 -> C.** *(D4.)* Spot = cheapest for interruptible/fault-tolerant work.
**Q17 -> A and B.** *(D1.)* Operational Excellence and Sustainability are pillars; the others are not.
**Q18 -> A.** *(D2.)* IAM Identity Center = SSO across accounts/apps.
**Q19 -> B.** *(D3.)* DynamoDB = managed NoSQL key-value.
**Q20 -> A.** *(D3.)* VPC = isolated network with subnets.
**Q21 -> A.** *(D2.)* WAF = SQL injection/XSS filtering.
**Q22 -> A.** *(D1.)* HA = minimal downtime, often multi-AZ.
**Q23 -> A.** *(D3.)* CloudFront = edge caching/CDN.
**Q24 -> C.** *(D4.)* Business = first plan with 24/7 engineer access + full Trusted Advisor.
**Q25 -> A.** *(D4.)* Cost Explorer = analyze + forecast spend.
**Q26 -> A.** *(D3.)* CloudFormation = IaC templates.
**Q27 -> B.** *(D1.)* Cloud shifts CapEx to OpEx.
**Q28 -> A.** *(D2.)* Macie = sensitive data discovery in S3.
**Q29 -> B.** *(D3.)* Fargate = serverless containers, least overhead.
**Q30 -> B.** *(D3.)* NAT gateway = outbound internet for private subnets.
**Q31 -> B.** *(D4.)* CUR = most granular billing data.
**Q32 -> B.** *(D3.)* EKS = managed Kubernetes.
**Q33 -> B.** *(D3.)* Site-to-Site VPN = encrypted tunnel over the internet.

### Final Readiness Exam — Answers & Explanations (Q34–Q65)

**Q34 -> A.** *(D1.)* Security pillar = protect data/systems/assets.
**Q35 -> A.** *(D3.)* AWS Backup = centralized cross-service backups.
**Q36 -> B.** *(D3.)* SQS = managed message queue for decoupling.
**Q37 -> A and C.** *(D4.)* Savings Plans for steady use and Spot for interruptible jobs save money; the rest waste it.
**Q38 -> B.** *(D3.)* Security group = stateful, instance level.
**Q39 -> A.** *(D2.)* Artifact = compliance reports (SOC, PCI).
**Q40 -> A.** *(D3.)* Athena = serverless SQL on S3.
**Q41 -> A.** *(D1.)* Fault tolerance = no interruption during a component failure.
**Q42 -> A.** *(D2.)* SCPs set guardrails like allowed Regions.
**Q43 -> A.** *(D3.)* Redshift = managed data warehouse.
**Q44 -> B.** *(D2.)* In transit = data encrypted while moving (TLS).
**Q45 -> A.** *(D3.)* Elastic Beanstalk = simplest app deploy with managed scaling.
**Q46 -> A.** *(D2.)* GuardDuty = ML threat detection from logs.
**Q47 -> A and B.** *(D3.)* A Region has multiple AZs; each AZ is one or more data centers. C, D, E are false.
**Q48 -> A.** *(D2.)* Secrets Manager = store + rotate secrets.
**Q49 -> B.** *(D4.)* Consolidated billing = one bill + volume discounts.
**Q50 -> A.** *(D3.)* ElastiCache = in-memory cache for read-heavy workloads.
**Q51 -> A.** *(D3.)* Snow Family = ship a device for bulk data.
**Q52 -> B.** *(D2/D3.)* CloudWatch = metrics, dashboards, alarms.
**Q53 -> B.** *(D1/D4.)* Pay only for what you use, no big upfront cost.
**Q54 -> A.** *(D2.)* Config = track/evaluate configuration changes.
**Q55 -> A.** *(D3.)* Glue = managed ETL + data catalog.
**Q56 -> A.** *(D1.)* Agility = provision fast, innovate faster.
**Q57 -> A.** *(D3.)* EC2 Auto Scaling = scale capacity to demand.
**Q58 -> A.** *(D2.)* ACM = manage SSL/TLS certificates.
**Q59 -> A and B.** *(D2.)* MFA and least privilege improve security; the rest are dangerous.
**Q60 -> A.** *(D3.)* QuickSight = BI dashboards.
**Q61 -> A.** *(D3.)* CloudFront = lower latency for global static content.
**Q62 -> B.** *(Exam mechanics.)* Compensatory scoring; 700/1000 overall passes.
**Q63 -> A.** *(D3.)* Control Tower = governed multi-account landing zone.
**Q64 -> A.** *(D3.)* DMS = database migration with minimal downtime.
**Q65 -> B.** *(D1.)* Pay-as-you-go, elasticity, global reach, less heavy lifting.

> **Final check:** If you scored ~80%+ here and on both mocks, and you understand *why* each answer is right (not just which letter), you are ready to schedule the real exam with confidence.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Final Review

You've studied the domains and drilled the questions. This chapter is about turning that into a calm, confident exam-day performance.

## How to review your wrong answers

Getting a question wrong is **good** during study — it shows you exactly what to fix. For every miss, ask:

1. **Did I misread the question?** Look for the qualifier words you skipped (MOST cost-effective, LEAST overhead, HIGHLY available).
2. **Did I confuse two similar services?** If so, go back to the relevant comparison table and the Keywords Decoder.
3. **Did I genuinely not know the concept?** Reread that section, then redo the domain mini quiz.

Keep a small "miss list" — a sheet of the topics you keep getting wrong. The night before the exam, you review only that list, not the whole book.

> **Exam Tip:** Don't just memorize the correct letter. The real exam reworps the same idea into a new scenario. If you understand *why*, you'll get the new version right too.

## What score means you're ready

- On the three 65-question mocks, aim to consistently score **around 80% or higher**.
- The real passing mark is **700 out of 1000 (scaled)** — roughly 70% — but you want a safety margin, because exam-day nerves and unfamiliar wording cost a few points.
- More important than the percentage: can you explain your reasoning out loud? If yes, you're ready.

## Final 24-hour plan

**The day before:**

- Do a light review of the five cheat sheets — especially "Last Day Before the Exam."
- Re-skim your personal miss list.
- Do **not** cram new material. It raises anxiety and rarely helps.
- Confirm exam logistics (time, location or online setup, ID).
- Sleep well. Rest beats one more hour of cramming.

**Exam morning:**

- Eat something, hydrate.
- Read the cheat-sheet "10 things people most often mix up" one final time.
- Arrive early (test center) or set up early (online) to avoid stress.

## Exam-day checklist (all formats)

- Valid, unexpired government-issued ID with name matching your registration.
- Know your AWS/Pearson VUE login details.
- Arrive/log in at least 30 minutes early.
- Calm mindset: you only need 700/1000, and there's no penalty for guessing.

## Online proctored exam checklist

- Quiet, private room; no one else may enter.
- Clean desk: no notes, phones, second monitors, headphones (unless approved), or smart devices.
- Stable internet and a working webcam + microphone.
- Run the system/compatibility check **before** exam day.
- Be ready to show your ID and scan the room with your camera.
- No talking out loud, no leaving the camera's view.

## Test-center checklist

- Bring the required ID(s).
- Arrive early to check in.
- Lockers are usually provided for personal items.
- Note-taking materials are provided by the center; you can't bring your own.

## Time-management strategy (during the exam)

- 65 questions in 90 minutes = about **1 minute 20 seconds each**.
- **First pass:** answer everything you know quickly. Flag anything that needs thought and move on — never get stuck.
- **Second pass:** return to flagged questions with your remaining time.
- **Always answer every question** — blanks score zero, and there's no penalty for a wrong guess.
- For tough multiple-choice, **eliminate** the clearly wrong options first to improve your odds.
- For "Choose TWO/THREE," make sure you select exactly the number requested.
- Read the **final sentence** of long scenarios first; it usually tells you what's actually being asked.

> **Remember:** Most people finish with time to spare. Slow down enough to read carefully, but trust your first instinct — second-guessing flips more right answers to wrong than the reverse.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Glossary

Beginner-friendly definitions of the terms you'll meet most often. Keep this handy while studying.

**Agility** — The ability to provision resources quickly and experiment without large upfront cost, so you can innovate faster.

**Availability Zone (AZ)** — One or more discrete data centers with redundant power and networking inside a Region. Used together for high availability.

**AWS CAF (Cloud Adoption Framework)** — AWS guidance for planning a cloud migration across six perspectives: Business, People, Governance, Platform, Security, Operations.

**CapEx (Capital Expenditure)** — Large upfront spending on assets like servers. The cloud reduces this.

**CDN (Content Delivery Network)** — A network of edge locations that cache content close to users to reduce latency. On AWS, this is CloudFront.

**Compensatory scoring** — A grading model where only your overall score matters, not your score in each individual domain.

**Compute** — Processing power for running applications (e.g., EC2, Lambda, containers).

**Durability** — How well stored data is protected from loss. S3 is designed for very high durability by storing copies redundantly.

**Edge location** — A site separate from full Regions, closer to end users, used by services like CloudFront to cache content.

**Elasticity** — Automatically adding and removing resources to match demand.

**Encryption at rest** — Protecting data while it is stored (on disk, in S3, in a database).

**Encryption in transit** — Protecting data while it moves across a network, typically using TLS/SSL.

**Fault tolerance** — The ability to keep operating with no interruption even when a component fails. Stronger than high availability.

**High availability (HA)** — Designing systems to minimize downtime, often by spreading across multiple AZs.

**Hybrid cloud** — A mix of on-premises infrastructure connected to the AWS Cloud.

**IaaS (Infrastructure as a Service)** — Cloud model giving you control of OS and infrastructure building blocks (e.g., EC2).

**IAM (Identity and Access Management)** — The service for managing who can access what in AWS, using users, groups, roles, and policies.

**IAM policy** — A JSON document that defines permissions (allow/deny).

**IAM role** — An identity with temporary credentials that can be assumed by services, applications, or other accounts. Best practice for granting access to AWS resources.

**Least privilege** — Granting only the permissions needed to do a task, and nothing more.

**MFA (Multi-Factor Authentication)** — Requiring a second verification factor (like a code) in addition to a password.

**NACL (Network ACL)** — A stateless firewall at the subnet level that can allow and deny traffic.

**NoSQL** — A non-relational database type (e.g., key-value). DynamoDB is AWS's NoSQL service.

**Object storage** — Storing data as objects (files plus metadata), accessed over the internet. S3 is object storage.

**On-premises** — IT infrastructure that a company runs in its own data center, not in the cloud.

**OpEx (Operational Expenditure)** — Ongoing, usage-based spending (like a monthly AWS bill). The cloud favors OpEx.

**PaaS (Platform as a Service)** — Cloud model where you focus on your app and AWS manages the platform (e.g., Elastic Beanstalk).

**Pay-as-you-go** — Paying only for the resources you actually use, with no long-term commitment required.

**Region** — A separate geographic area containing multiple Availability Zones. You choose Regions for latency, cost, and data residency.

**Relational database (SQL)** — A structured database using tables and SQL. RDS and Aurora are relational on AWS.

**Reserved Instances (RIs)** — A 1- or 3-year commitment for a discount on steady-state EC2 usage.

**SaaS (Software as a Service)** — Ready-to-use software delivered over the internet; the vendor manages everything (e.g., webmail).

**Savings Plans** — Flexible commitment to a consistent amount of compute usage for 1 or 3 years in exchange for lower prices.

**Scalability** — The ability to increase (or decrease) capacity to handle changing load. Vertical = bigger resource; horizontal = more resources.

**Security group** — A stateful firewall at the instance level that allows (but does not explicitly deny) traffic.

**Serverless** — A model where you don't manage servers; AWS runs and scales the infrastructure (e.g., Lambda, DynamoDB, Fargate).

**Shared Responsibility Model** — AWS secures the cloud (infrastructure); the customer secures what they put in the cloud (data, access, configuration).

**Spot Instances** — Heavily discounted spare EC2 capacity that AWS can reclaim with little notice; for interruptible workloads.

**TAM (Technical Account Manager)** — A designated AWS contact included with the Enterprise Support plan.

**VPC (Virtual Private Cloud)** — Your logically isolated private network within AWS, where you create subnets and launch resources.

**Well-Architected Framework** — AWS best-practice guidance built on six pillars: Operational Excellence, Security, Reliability, Performance Efficiency, Cost Optimization, and Sustainability.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Appendix

## Official AWS resources to verify (always check the latest)

AWS updates services and exam details over time. Before your exam, confirm current information from official sources:

- **AWS Certified Cloud Practitioner page** — the official exam overview, including current cost, format, and language availability.
- **CLF-C02 Exam Guide (PDF)** — the authoritative list of domains, weightings, and in-scope task statements. This is the single most important document to skim before booking.
- **AWS Skill Builder** — official digital training, including the free "AWS Cloud Practitioner Essentials" course and official practice question sets.
- **AWS Whitepapers** — especially *Overview of Amazon Web Services*, the *AWS Well-Architected Framework*, and *How AWS Pricing Works*.
- **AWS Documentation** — per-service docs for anything you want to dig deeper on.
- **AWS Free Tier page** — current free-tier offers and limits.

> **Remember:** This book is an independent study guide. If anything here ever conflicts with the official AWS exam guide, **the official AWS source wins.** Always verify the latest details yourself.

## Suggested hands-on labs

You can pass this exam without deep hands-on work, but doing a few simple things in a free-tier account makes concepts stick:

1. **Create an IAM user and group**, attach a policy, and enable MFA on the root user.
2. **Launch a small EC2 instance**, connect to it, then stop and terminate it (watch the billing implications).
3. **Create an S3 bucket**, upload a file, explore storage classes, and set a lifecycle rule.
4. **Set up an AWS Budget** with an email alert at a low threshold (e.g., $5).
5. **Explore the Billing Dashboard and Cost Explorer** to see where charges come from.
6. **Open Trusted Advisor** and review the available checks for your account.
7. **Create a simple Lambda function** from a blueprint and trigger it once.

> **Exam Tip:** Even an hour of clicking around the console turns abstract terms (Region, AZ, bucket, instance) into things you've actually seen — which makes scenario questions much easier.

## Recommended free study resources

- AWS Skill Builder: *AWS Cloud Practitioner Essentials* (free digital course).
- The official CLF-C02 sample/practice questions on AWS Skill Builder.
- AWS Free Tier account for hands-on practice.
- AWS whitepapers and FAQs (especially the EC2, S3, and billing FAQs).
- This book's quizzes and mock exams — redo them until you understand every explanation.

## Revision checklist

Tick these off before booking the exam:

- [ ] I can explain the Shared Responsibility Model with examples of AWS vs. customer duties.
- [ ] I can name the 6 Well-Architected pillars.
- [ ] I can match keywords to services (object storage -> S3, serverless code -> Lambda, etc.).
- [ ] I know the difference between CloudTrail, CloudWatch, and Config.
- [ ] I know S3 vs. EBS vs. EFS.
- [ ] I know WAF vs. Shield vs. GuardDuty vs. Inspector.
- [ ] I know security group vs. NACL.
- [ ] I know RDS vs. DynamoDB vs. Redshift.
- [ ] I know the EC2 pricing options (On-Demand, Reserved, Savings Plans, Spot, Dedicated Hosts).
- [ ] I know Pricing Calculator vs. Cost Explorer vs. Budgets.
- [ ] I know the 5 support plans and which include a TAM and full Trusted Advisor.
- [ ] I understand HA vs. fault tolerance vs. elasticity vs. scalability.
- [ ] I scored ~80%+ on all three full mock exams.
- [ ] I reviewed every wrong answer and understand *why*.

When all boxes are ticked, you're ready. Book it.

# Appendix B: Exporting This Book to PDF and EPUB

This book is written in clean Markdown so you can convert it to a polished PDF or EPUB using **Pandoc**, a free open-source document converter.

## Install Pandoc

- Download from the official Pandoc website, or install via a package manager:
  - macOS: `brew install pandoc`
  - Windows: `choco install pandoc` (or use the installer)
  - Linux (Debian/Ubuntu): `sudo apt-get install pandoc`
- For PDF output you also need a LaTeX engine. A lightweight option is **TinyTeX**; full options include **TeX Live** or **MiKTeX** (Windows).

## Markdown to PDF

Basic command (run in the folder containing the Markdown file):

```
pandoc aws-clf-c02-guide.md -o aws-clf-c02-guide.pdf
```

A nicer version with a table of contents, numbered sections, and readable margins:

```
pandoc aws-clf-c02-guide.md \
  -o aws-clf-c02-guide.pdf \
  --toc \
  --toc-depth=2 \
  --number-sections \
  -V geometry:margin=1in \
  -V fontsize=11pt \
  -V mainfont="Helvetica" \
  --pdf-engine=xelatex
```

> **Note on page breaks:** The `<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>` markers in this book are HTML. They work best in HTML/EPUB output. For LaTeX-based PDF output, Pandoc may ignore them; if you need hard page breaks in PDF, you can replace those markers with a raw LaTeX command like `\newpage` (wrapped appropriately) or convert via HTML first.

### Suggested PDF styling

- Margins: 1 inch all around.
- Font size: 11pt for body text.
- A clean sans-serif (Helvetica/Arial) for body, or a serif (e.g., Latin Modern) for a "book" feel.
- Include `--toc --toc-depth=2` so chapters and major sections are navigable.
- Use `--number-sections` if you want numbered headings.

## Markdown to EPUB

Basic command:

```
pandoc aws-clf-c02-guide.md -o aws-clf-c02-guide.epub
```

With a table of contents and metadata file:

```
pandoc aws-clf-c02-guide.md \
  -o aws-clf-c02-guide.epub \
  --toc \
  --toc-depth=2 \
  --epub-metadata=metadata.xml
```

You can also add a cover image:

```
pandoc aws-clf-c02-guide.md -o aws-clf-c02-guide.epub --toc --epub-cover-image=cover.png
```

### Suggested EPUB metadata

Create a file named `metadata.xml` like this:

```
<dc:title>AWS Certified Cloud Practitioner CLF-C02: Read-to-Pass Complete Guide</dc:title>
<dc:creator>Your Name</dc:creator>
<dc:language>en</dc:language>
<dc:date>2026</dc:date>
<dc:publisher>Independent</dc:publisher>
<dc:description>An independent beginner-friendly study guide for the AWS Certified Cloud Practitioner (CLF-C02) exam.</dc:description>
<dc:rights>All rights reserved. Independent study guide; not affiliated with or endorsed by Amazon Web Services.</dc:rights>
```

### EPUB-friendly formatting notes

- The tables in this book are kept **simple** (no merged cells or nested HTML) so they render reliably in EPUB readers.
- Diagrams are described in text/alt-text style rather than embedded images, so nothing breaks on conversion.
- Headings use a clean hierarchy (`#` chapter, `##` section, `###` subsection) which Pandoc turns into a proper EPUB/PDF navigation tree.

## Quick HTML option (optional)

If you just want a shareable web page:

```
pandoc aws-clf-c02-guide.md -o aws-clf-c02-guide.html --toc --standalone
```

The HTML output honors the `page-break-after` markers when printed from a browser to PDF, which can be the simplest path to a nicely paginated PDF.

# Preface

## Why Python certifications matter for your career path

Python is the #1 language in security, cloud automation, data science, and AI — all areas directly relevant to your career goals. PCEP proves you can code Python. PCAP proves you understand OOP, modules, and real-world patterns. Together they make your resume credible as a Python developer before you have significant work history.

For Thailand: Python skills + AWS + Security = strong combination for banking, healthcare IT, fintech roles.
For Japan: Python + Kubernetes (CKA) = backend/infrastructure engineer profile.

**PCEP-30-02** → Entry-level. ~45 min, 30 questions, 70% to pass. **~$59 USD**.
**PCAP-31-03** → Associate level. ~65 min, 45 questions, 70% to pass. **~$295 USD**.

Both exams include code-reading questions where you must predict output, find errors, or complete code. There is no writing code — everything is multiple choice — but you must understand code deeply.

> **Exam Tip:** The hardest part of both exams is **edge cases and tricky output questions**. Python's operator precedence, mutable default arguments, scope rules, and string slicing behavior are the most common trick topics. Read every question twice and trace the code manually.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# PCEP: Certified Entry-Level Python Programmer

## PCEP Exam Facts

| Item | Detail |
|---|---|
| Exam code | PCEP-30-02 |
| Questions | ~30 |
| Time | 45 minutes |
| Passing | 70% (21/30) |
| Cost | ~$59 USD |
| Mode | Online (non-proctored) or proctored at testing center |
| Validity | Lifetime (does not expire) |
| Prerequisites | None |

## PCEP Topics

1. Computer programming basics and Python fundamentals
2. Control flow — conditionals and loops
3. Data collections — lists, tuples, dictionaries, strings
4. Functions and exceptions

---

# PCEP Section 1: Python Fundamentals

## 1.1 Python Basics

```python
# Python is interpreted, dynamically typed, garbage-collected
print("Hello, World!")       # basic output
print("Value:", 42)          # multiple args separated by spaces
print(1, 2, 3, sep="-")      # custom separator: 1-2-3
print("end", end="!")        # custom end: end! (no newline)

# Comments
# This is a single-line comment
"""
This is a multi-line
docstring / comment
"""

# Variables — no declaration needed, type inferred
x = 10
name = "Alice"
pi = 3.14159
is_valid = True

# Multiple assignment
a, b, c = 1, 2, 3
x = y = z = 0          # all assigned same value

# Swap variables (Python idiom)
a, b = b, a
```

## 1.2 Data Types

```python
# Built-in types
type(42)         # <class 'int'>
type(3.14)       # <class 'float'>
type("hello")    # <class 'str'>
type(True)       # <class 'bool'>
type(None)       # <class 'NoneType'>

# Type conversion
int("42")        # 42
float("3.14")    # 3.14
str(100)         # "100"
bool(0)          # False
bool(1)          # True
bool("")         # False
bool("a")        # True

# Integer operations
10 // 3          # 3    (floor division)
10 % 3           # 1    (modulo/remainder)
2 ** 10          # 1024 (exponentiation)
abs(-5)          # 5
round(3.7)       # 4
round(3.5)       # 4    (banker's rounding — rounds to even)
round(2.5)       # 2    ← EXAM TRICK!

# Operator precedence (PEMDAS in Python):
# ** (exponent) > unary - > *, /, //, % > +, -
2 + 3 * 4        # 14 (not 20)
(2 + 3) * 4      # 20
2 ** 3 ** 2      # 512 (right-to-left: 3**2=9, 2**9=512)
```

> **Exam Trick:** `round(2.5)` returns `2` in Python 3 (banker's rounding, rounds to nearest even). `round(3.5)` returns `4`. This trips up many candidates.

## 1.3 Strings ⭐

```python
s = "Hello, World!"

# Indexing (0-based, negative from end)
s[0]        # 'H'
s[-1]       # '!'
s[-2]       # 'd'

# Slicing s[start:stop:step]
s[0:5]      # 'Hello'      (stop is exclusive)
s[7:]       # 'World!'
s[:5]       # 'Hello'
s[::2]      # 'Hlo ol!'   (every 2nd character)
s[::-1]     # '!dlroW ,olleH' (reversed)

# String methods (return new string — strings are immutable)
"hello".upper()         # 'HELLO'
"HELLO".lower()         # 'hello'
"hello world".title()   # 'Hello World'
"  hello  ".strip()     # 'hello'
"  hello  ".lstrip()    # 'hello  '
"  hello  ".rstrip()    # '  hello'
"hello".replace("l", "r")  # 'herro'
"hello world".split()   # ['hello', 'world']
"hello world".split("o") # ['hell', ' w', 'rld']
",".join(["a", "b", "c"]) # 'a,b,c'
"hello".find("l")       # 2 (first occurrence index)
"hello".count("l")      # 2
"hello".startswith("he") # True
"hello".endswith("lo")   # True
"123".isdigit()         # True
"abc".isalpha()         # True
"abc123".isalnum()      # True

# String formatting
name = "Alice"
age = 25
f"Hello, {name}! You are {age} years old."  # f-string ⭐
"Hello, {}!".format(name)                    # .format()
"Hello, %s!" % name                         # %-formatting (legacy)

# Escape characters
"\n"   # newline
"\t"   # tab
"\\"   # backslash
"\'"   # single quote
"\""   # double quote

# Multiline strings
text = """
Line 1
Line 2
Line 3
"""

# Length
len("hello")    # 5
```

## 1.4 Input and Output

```python
# Input always returns a string
name = input("Enter your name: ")
age = int(input("Enter your age: "))    # must convert

# Print with formatting
print(f"Name: {name}, Age: {age}")
print("Pi is approximately {:.2f}".format(3.14159))  # 'Pi is approximately 3.14'
print("{:10}".format("left"))     # right-padded to 10 chars
print("{:>10}".format("right"))   # right-aligned
print("{:0>5}".format(42))        # '00042'
```

---

# PCEP Section 2: Control Flow

## 2.1 Conditionals

```python
# if / elif / else
x = 10
if x > 0:
    print("positive")
elif x < 0:
    print("negative")
else:
    print("zero")

# Comparison operators
==   # equal
!=   # not equal
<    # less than
>    # greater than
<=   # less than or equal
>=   # greater than or equal

# Logical operators
and  # both must be True
or   # at least one must be True
not  # inverts

# Truthy and Falsy values ⭐ (exam trick!)
# FALSY: 0, 0.0, "", [], {}, (), None, False
# TRUTHY: everything else (any non-zero number, non-empty string/list/dict)

if []:       # False — empty list is falsy
    print("never runs")
if [0]:      # True — list with one element (even if element is 0!)
    print("runs")

# Ternary operator
x = 10
result = "positive" if x > 0 else "non-positive"
```

## 2.2 Loops

```python
# for loop — iterate over sequences
for i in range(5):        # 0, 1, 2, 3, 4
    print(i)

for i in range(2, 10, 2): # 2, 4, 6, 8 (start, stop, step)
    print(i)

for char in "hello":
    print(char)

for item in [1, 2, 3]:
    print(item)

# enumerate — get index AND value
for i, val in enumerate(["a", "b", "c"]):
    print(i, val)    # 0 a, 1 b, 2 c

# while loop
count = 0
while count < 5:
    print(count)
    count += 1

# break — exit loop
for i in range(10):
    if i == 5:
        break        # stops loop at i=5

# continue — skip to next iteration
for i in range(10):
    if i % 2 == 0:
        continue     # skip even numbers
    print(i)         # prints 1, 3, 5, 7, 9

# for...else / while...else ⭐ (EXAM TRICK!)
# else block runs if loop completed WITHOUT hitting break
for i in range(5):
    if i == 10:
        break
else:
    print("Loop finished without break")  # This RUNS

for i in range(5):
    if i == 3:
        break
else:
    print("This does NOT run")  # break was hit

# Nested loops
for i in range(3):
    for j in range(3):
        print(i, j)
```

---

# PCEP Section 3: Data Collections

## 3.1 Lists ⭐

```python
# Lists — ordered, mutable, allows duplicates
my_list = [1, 2, 3, "hello", True]
empty = []

# Indexing and slicing (same as strings)
my_list[0]        # 1
my_list[-1]       # True
my_list[1:3]      # [2, 3]

# List methods
lst = [3, 1, 4, 1, 5, 9]
lst.append(2)           # add to end: [3,1,4,1,5,9,2]
lst.insert(0, 0)        # insert at index 0: [0,3,1,4,1,5,9,2]
lst.remove(1)           # removes FIRST occurrence of 1
lst.pop()               # removes and returns last element
lst.pop(0)              # removes and returns element at index 0
lst.sort()              # sort in-place (ascending)
lst.sort(reverse=True)  # sort descending
lst.reverse()           # reverse in-place
lst.count(1)            # count occurrences
lst.index(4)            # find index of first occurrence
lst.extend([10, 11])    # extend with another list
lst.clear()             # remove all elements

# Copying lists ⭐ (EXAM TRICK — mutable objects!)
a = [1, 2, 3]
b = a           # b is SAME OBJECT as a (not a copy!)
b.append(4)
print(a)        # [1, 2, 3, 4] ← a is modified too!

c = a.copy()    # shallow copy — c is independent
c = a[:]        # also a shallow copy
c = list(a)     # also a copy

# List comprehension ⭐
squares = [x**2 for x in range(5)]          # [0, 1, 4, 9, 16]
evens = [x for x in range(10) if x % 2 == 0]  # [0, 2, 4, 6, 8]
matrix = [[i*j for j in range(3)] for i in range(3)]  # nested

# in operator
3 in [1, 2, 3]     # True
5 not in [1, 2, 3] # True

# len, max, min, sum
len([1,2,3])   # 3
max([1,2,3])   # 3
min([1,2,3])   # 1
sum([1,2,3])   # 6

# sorted() vs .sort()
sorted([3,1,2])    # returns NEW sorted list, original unchanged
[3,1,2].sort()     # sorts in-place, returns None
```

## 3.2 Tuples

```python
# Tuples — ordered, IMMUTABLE, allows duplicates
t = (1, 2, 3)
single = (1,)       # single-element tuple NEEDS comma!
not_tuple = (1)     # this is just integer 1!

t[0]                # 1 (indexing works)
t[0] = 10           # TypeError! Tuples are immutable

# Tuple unpacking
x, y, z = (1, 2, 3)
first, *rest = (1, 2, 3, 4)   # first=1, rest=[2,3,4]

# Common tuple operations
len(t)              # 3
3 in t              # True
t.count(1)          # 1
t.index(2)          # 1
t + (4, 5)          # (1, 2, 3, 4, 5)
t * 2               # (1, 2, 3, 1, 2, 3)
```

## 3.3 Dictionaries ⭐

```python
# Dictionaries — key-value pairs, unordered (ordered in 3.7+), mutable
d = {"name": "Alice", "age": 25, "city": "Bangkok"}
empty_dict = {}

# Access
d["name"]               # "Alice"
d.get("name")           # "Alice"
d.get("phone", "N/A")   # "N/A" (default if key missing — no KeyError)
d["phone"]              # KeyError if not found!

# Modify
d["age"] = 26           # update existing
d["email"] = "a@b.com"  # add new key

# Methods
d.keys()                # dict_keys(["name", "age", "city", "email"])
d.values()              # dict_values(["Alice", 26, "Bangkok", "a@b.com"])
d.items()               # dict_items([("name","Alice"), ...])
d.pop("email")          # removes and returns value
d.update({"x": 1, "y": 2})  # merge another dict
"name" in d             # True (checks keys)
del d["city"]           # delete a key

# Iteration
for key in d:
    print(key)

for key, value in d.items():
    print(f"{key}: {value}")

# Dict comprehension
squares = {x: x**2 for x in range(5)}  # {0:0, 1:1, 2:4, 3:9, 4:16}
```

## 3.4 Sets

```python
# Sets — unordered, unique elements only, mutable
s = {1, 2, 3, 4}
empty_set = set()   # NOT {} (that's an empty dict!)

# Set operations
s.add(5)
s.remove(3)         # raises KeyError if not found
s.discard(99)       # no error if not found

# Set math
a = {1, 2, 3, 4}
b = {3, 4, 5, 6}
a | b    # union: {1,2,3,4,5,6}
a & b    # intersection: {3,4}
a - b    # difference: {1,2}
a ^ b    # symmetric difference: {1,2,5,6}

# Remove duplicates from list using set
unique = list(set([1, 2, 2, 3, 3, 3]))   # [1, 2, 3] (order not guaranteed)
```

---

# PCEP Section 4: Functions and Exceptions

## 4.1 Functions ⭐

```python
# Define and call
def greet(name):
    return f"Hello, {name}!"

result = greet("Alice")

# Default parameters ⭐ (EXAM TRICK!)
def greet(name, greeting="Hello"):
    return f"{greeting}, {name}!"

greet("Alice")              # "Hello, Alice!"
greet("Alice", "Hi")        # "Hi, Alice!"

# DANGER: mutable default argument ⭐⭐ (MAJOR EXAM TRAP!)
def add_item(item, my_list=[]):    # BAD! shared across calls!
    my_list.append(item)
    return my_list

add_item(1)     # [1]
add_item(2)     # [1, 2] ← list persists between calls!

# CORRECT approach:
def add_item(item, my_list=None):
    if my_list is None:
        my_list = []
    my_list.append(item)
    return my_list

# *args — variable positional arguments
def sum_all(*args):
    return sum(args)

sum_all(1, 2, 3)      # 6
sum_all(1, 2, 3, 4)   # 10

# **kwargs — variable keyword arguments
def show_info(**kwargs):
    for key, value in kwargs.items():
        print(f"{key}: {value}")

show_info(name="Alice", age=25)

# Return multiple values (returns a tuple)
def min_max(lst):
    return min(lst), max(lst)

lo, hi = min_max([3, 1, 4, 1, 5])   # lo=1, hi=5

# Scope — LEGB rule ⭐
x = "global"

def outer():
    x = "enclosing"
    def inner():
        x = "local"
        print(x)     # "local"
    inner()
    print(x)         # "enclosing"

outer()
print(x)             # "global"

# global keyword — modify global from inside function
count = 0
def increment():
    global count
    count += 1

# nonlocal keyword — modify enclosing scope variable
def outer():
    x = 0
    def inner():
        nonlocal x
        x += 1
    inner()
    return x   # 1
```

## 4.2 Lambda Functions

```python
# Lambda — anonymous, single-expression function
square = lambda x: x ** 2
square(5)    # 25

add = lambda x, y: x + y
add(3, 4)    # 7

# Common use: sorting with lambda
people = [("Alice", 30), ("Bob", 25), ("Charlie", 35)]
people.sort(key=lambda person: person[1])   # sort by age
# Result: [("Bob",25), ("Alice",30), ("Charlie",35)]

# With map, filter, sorted
numbers = [1, 2, 3, 4, 5]
squared = list(map(lambda x: x**2, numbers))     # [1,4,9,16,25]
evens = list(filter(lambda x: x%2==0, numbers))  # [2,4]
sorted_nums = sorted(numbers, key=lambda x: -x)  # [5,4,3,2,1]
```

## 4.3 Exceptions ⭐

```python
# try / except / else / finally
try:
    x = int(input("Enter a number: "))
    result = 10 / x
except ValueError:
    print("Not a valid number!")
except ZeroDivisionError:
    print("Cannot divide by zero!")
except (TypeError, AttributeError) as e:
    print(f"Error: {e}")
except Exception as e:
    print(f"Unexpected error: {e}")
else:
    print(f"Result: {result}")   # runs ONLY if no exception
finally:
    print("This ALWAYS runs")    # runs regardless of exception

# Common exception types ⭐ (must know for exam)
# ValueError — wrong type/value: int("hello")
# TypeError — wrong type operation: 1 + "hello"
# ZeroDivisionError — division by zero: 10/0
# IndexError — out of range: [1,2,3][10]
# KeyError — missing key: {"a":1}["b"]
# AttributeError — object has no attribute
# NameError — variable not defined
# FileNotFoundError — file doesn't exist
# ImportError — can't import module
# StopIteration — iterator exhausted

# Raise exceptions
def divide(a, b):
    if b == 0:
        raise ValueError("Denominator cannot be zero")
    return a / b

# Custom exception
class MyError(Exception):
    pass

raise MyError("Something went wrong")

# assert
x = 10
assert x > 0, "x must be positive"   # raises AssertionError if False
```

## PCEP Practice Questions (20 questions)

**Q1.** What does the following code print?
```python
print(type(1/2))
```
- A. `<class 'int'>`  B. `<class 'float'>`  C. `<class 'double'>`  D. Error

**Q2.** What does `round(2.5)` return in Python 3?
- A. 3  B. 2  C. 2.5  D. Error

**Q3.** What is the output?
```python
x = [1, 2, 3]
y = x
y.append(4)
print(len(x))
```
- A. 3  B. 4  C. 1  D. Error

**Q4.** Which is a valid empty set literal?
- A. `{}`  B. `set()`  C. `[]`  D. `()`

**Q5.** What does the following print?
```python
for i in range(3):
    pass
else:
    print("done")
```
- A. Nothing  B. `done`  C. Error  D. `0 1 2 done`

**Q6.** What is the value of `2 ** 3 ** 2`?
- A. 64  B. 512  C. 72  D. 8

**Q7.** What does this print?
```python
x = "hello"
print(x[::-1])
```
- A. `hello`  B. `h`  C. `olleh`  D. Error

**Q8.** Which of the following is FALSY?
- A. `[0]`  B. `"False"`  C. `()`  D. `1`

**Q9.** What is the output?
```python
def f(x, lst=[]):
    lst.append(x)
    return lst

print(f(1))
print(f(2))
```
- A. `[1]` then `[2]`  B. `[1]` then `[1, 2]`  C. Error  D. `[1,2]` twice

**Q10.** What does `"hello".split("l")` return?
- A. `['he', 'lo']`  B. `['he', '', 'o']`  C. `['hel', 'lo']`  D. Error

**Q11.** What is the output?
```python
a = (1, 2, 3)
a[0] = 10
print(a)
```
- A. `(10, 2, 3)`  B. `(1, 2, 3)`  C. `TypeError`  D. `[10, 2, 3]`

**Q12.** What does this evaluate to?
```python
bool("") or bool([1]) and bool(0)
```
- A. True  B. False  C. 0  D. []

**Q13.** What is the output?
```python
x = 5
def f():
    x = 10
    print(x)
f()
print(x)
```
- A. `10` then `10`  B. `10` then `5`  C. `5` then `5`  D. Error

**Q14.** Which correctly creates a list of squares of even numbers from 0–9?
- A. `[x**2 for x in range(10) if x%2==0]`
- B. `[x**2 if x%2==0 for x in range(10)]`
- C. `{x**2 for x in range(10) if x%2==0}`
- D. `(x**2 for x in range(10) if x%2==0)`

**Q15.** What does this print?
```python
try:
    x = 1/0
except ZeroDivisionError:
    print("A")
else:
    print("B")
finally:
    print("C")
```
- A. `A C`  B. `B C`  C. `A B C`  D. `C`

**Q16.** What is the value of `10 % 3`?
- A. 3  B. 1  C. 0.33  D. 3.33

**Q17.** What does `sorted([3,1,2], reverse=True)` return?
- A. `[1,2,3]`  B. `[3,2,1]`  C. `None`  D. Error

**Q18.** What is the output?
```python
d = {"a": 1, "b": 2}
print(d.get("c", 99))
```
- A. `None`  B. `KeyError`  C. `99`  D. `0`

**Q19.** Which statement about `*args` is TRUE?
- A. `*args` must be the first parameter  
- B. `*args` collects extra positional arguments as a tuple  
- C. `*args` collects extra keyword arguments as a dict  
- D. Only one function can use `*args`

**Q20.** What does this print?
```python
x = [1, 2, 3, 4, 5]
print(x[1:4:2])
```
- A. `[1, 3, 5]`  B. `[2, 4]`  C. `[2, 3, 4]`  D. `[1, 4]`

### PCEP Answers

**Q1->B.** `1/2` in Python 3 = `0.5` (float). Use `//` for integer division.
**Q2->B.** `round(2.5)` = 2. Python 3 uses banker's rounding (round to nearest even).
**Q3->B.** `y = x` — y points to same list. Appending to y modifies the same object. `len(x)` = 4.
**Q4->B.** `{}` is empty dict! `set()` is empty set. No literal for empty set.
**Q5->B.** `else` on a `for` loop runs if loop completes without `break`. Prints "done".
**Q6->B.** `**` is right-associative: `3**2=9`, then `2**9=512`.
**Q7->C.** `[::-1]` reverses a string: "olleh".
**Q8->C.** `()` empty tuple = falsy. `[0]` is a list with one element (truthy). `"False"` is non-empty string (truthy).
**Q9->B.** Mutable default argument! The list persists across calls: `[1]` then `[1, 2]`.
**Q10->B.** `"hello".split("l")` → `['he', '', 'o']` — two l's create an empty string between them.
**Q11->C.** Tuples are immutable. Trying to assign to index raises `TypeError`.
**Q12->B.** `bool("") = False`, `bool([1]) = True`, `bool(0) = False`. `False or (True and False)` = `False or False` = False.
**Q13->B.** `x = 10` inside `f()` creates a local variable. Global `x = 5` unchanged. Prints 10 then 5.
**Q14->A.** Correct list comprehension syntax. B has wrong syntax. C creates a set. D creates a generator.
**Q15->A.** ZeroDivisionError → except block prints "A". `else` is skipped (exception occurred). `finally` always prints "C".
**Q16->B.** `10 % 3` = 1 (10 = 3×3 + 1, remainder is 1).
**Q17->B.** `sorted()` returns a new sorted list. `reverse=True` → descending → `[3,2,1]`.
**Q18->C.** `d.get("c", 99)` returns 99 if key "c" not found (default value, no KeyError).
**Q19->B.** `*args` collects extra positional args as a TUPLE (not list). `**kwargs` is for keyword args.
**Q20->B.** `x[1:4:2]` → start at index 1, stop before 4, step 2 → indices 1, 3 → `[2, 4]`.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# PCAP Section 1: Modules and Packages

## 1.1 Importing Modules

```python
import math
math.sqrt(16)        # 4.0
math.pi              # 3.141592...
math.floor(3.7)      # 3
math.ceil(3.2)       # 4

import random
random.random()      # float between 0.0 and 1.0
random.randint(1,6)  # random int from 1 to 6 (inclusive)
random.choice([1,2,3])   # random element from list
random.shuffle(lst)  # shuffle list in-place

import datetime
today = datetime.date.today()
now = datetime.datetime.now()
print(now.year, now.month, now.day)
print(now.strftime("%Y-%m-%d %H:%M:%S"))

import os
os.getcwd()                   # current working directory
os.listdir(".")               # list files in directory
os.path.exists("file.txt")    # check if path exists
os.path.join("dir", "file")   # join paths (cross-platform)

import sys
sys.argv          # command line arguments
sys.path          # Python module search path
sys.exit(0)       # exit program

# Different import styles
from math import sqrt, pi            # import specific names
from math import sqrt as sq          # alias
from math import *                   # import everything (not recommended)
import math as m                     # module alias
m.sqrt(16)
```

## 1.2 Creating Your Own Modules and Packages

```python
# A Python file IS a module
# mymodule.py:
def greet(name):
    return f"Hello, {name}!"

MY_CONSTANT = 42

# Use it:
import mymodule
mymodule.greet("Alice")

# __name__ variable ⭐ (EXAM TRICK!)
# When a file is run directly: __name__ == "__main__"
# When a file is imported: __name__ == "mymodule" (the module name)

# Standard pattern:
if __name__ == "__main__":
    # This only runs when script is executed directly
    # Not when imported
    greet("World")

# Packages — directories with __init__.py
# mypackage/
#   __init__.py
#   module1.py
#   module2.py

import mypackage.module1
from mypackage import module2
```

# PCAP Section 2: Object-Oriented Programming ⭐

## 2.1 Classes and Objects

```python
class Dog:
    # Class variable — shared by ALL instances
    species = "Canis lupus familiaris"
    population = 0

    # __init__ — constructor (called when object is created)
    def __init__(self, name, age):
        # Instance variables — unique to each instance
        self.name = name
        self.age = age
        Dog.population += 1

    # Instance method
    def bark(self):
        return f"{self.name} says Woof!"

    # String representation ⭐
    def __str__(self):
        return f"Dog({self.name}, {self.age})"

    def __repr__(self):
        return f"Dog(name={self.name!r}, age={self.age!r})"

# Create instances
dog1 = Dog("Buddy", 3)
dog2 = Dog("Max", 5)

print(dog1.name)        # Buddy
print(dog1.bark())      # Buddy says Woof!
print(str(dog1))        # Dog(Buddy, 3)
print(Dog.population)   # 2
print(dog1.species)     # Canis lupus familiaris (access via instance or class)
```

## 2.2 Inheritance ⭐

```python
class Animal:
    def __init__(self, name):
        self.name = name

    def speak(self):
        raise NotImplementedError("Subclass must implement speak()")

    def __str__(self):
        return f"Animal: {self.name}"

class Dog(Animal):
    def speak(self):
        return f"{self.name}: Woof!"

class Cat(Animal):
    def speak(self):
        return f"{self.name}: Meow!"

    def __str__(self):
        return f"Cat: {self.name}"    # overrides parent __str__

# Using inherited class
dog = Dog("Buddy")
cat = Cat("Whiskers")
print(dog.speak())      # Buddy: Woof!
print(cat.speak())      # Whiskers: Meow!
print(str(cat))         # Cat: Whiskers (uses Cat's __str__)
print(str(dog))         # Animal: Buddy (uses Animal's __str__)

# super() — call parent class method
class GuideDog(Dog):
    def __init__(self, name, owner):
        super().__init__(name)    # call Dog's (and Animal's) __init__
        self.owner = owner

    def speak(self):
        base = super().speak()     # call Dog's speak()
        return f"{base} (guide dog for {self.owner})"

guide = GuideDog("Rex", "John")
print(guide.speak())   # Rex: Woof! (guide dog for John)

# isinstance and issubclass ⭐
isinstance(dog, Dog)     # True
isinstance(dog, Animal)  # True (Dog inherits Animal)
isinstance(dog, Cat)     # False
issubclass(Dog, Animal)  # True
issubclass(Cat, Dog)     # False
```

## 2.3 Dunder (Magic/Special) Methods ⭐

```python
class Vector:
    def __init__(self, x, y):
        self.x = x
        self.y = y

    def __str__(self):     # str(obj) and print(obj)
        return f"Vector({self.x}, {self.y})"

    def __repr__(self):    # repr(obj) and in REPL
        return f"Vector(x={self.x}, y={self.y})"

    def __add__(self, other):    # v1 + v2
        return Vector(self.x + other.x, self.y + other.y)

    def __sub__(self, other):    # v1 - v2
        return Vector(self.x - other.x, self.y - other.y)

    def __mul__(self, scalar):   # v * 3
        return Vector(self.x * scalar, self.y * scalar)

    def __eq__(self, other):     # v1 == v2
        return self.x == other.x and self.y == other.y

    def __len__(self):           # len(v)
        return 2

    def __bool__(self):          # bool(v) or if v:
        return bool(self.x or self.y)

    def __contains__(self, val): # val in v
        return val in (self.x, self.y)

v1 = Vector(1, 2)
v2 = Vector(3, 4)
print(v1 + v2)     # Vector(4, 6)
print(len(v1))     # 2
print(1 in v1)     # True
```

## 2.4 Encapsulation and Property

```python
class BankAccount:
    def __init__(self, balance):
        self.__balance = balance    # private (name mangling: _BankAccount__balance)
        self._owner = "Unknown"     # protected (convention only, not enforced)

    @property
    def balance(self):             # getter — access like attribute: account.balance
        return self.__balance

    @balance.setter
    def balance(self, value):      # setter — account.balance = 1000
        if value < 0:
            raise ValueError("Balance cannot be negative")
        self.__balance = value

    @balance.deleter
    def balance(self):             # deleter — del account.balance
        del self.__balance

account = BankAccount(1000)
print(account.balance)    # 1000 (uses getter)
account.balance = 500     # uses setter
# account.__balance        # AttributeError (private)
# account._BankAccount__balance  # works but bad practice
```

## 2.5 Class Methods and Static Methods

```python
class Counter:
    count = 0

    def __init__(self):
        Counter.count += 1

    @classmethod
    def get_count(cls):      # cls = the class itself (like self but for class)
        return cls.count

    @staticmethod
    def is_positive(x):      # no self or cls — just a utility function in the class
        return x > 0

    @classmethod
    def create_default(cls): # alternative constructor
        return cls()

print(Counter.get_count())  # 0 (called on class)
c1 = Counter()
c2 = Counter()
print(Counter.get_count())  # 2
print(Counter.is_positive(-1))  # False
```

---

# PCAP Section 3: Generators, Closures, Decorators

## 3.1 Generators and Iterators ⭐

```python
# Generator function — uses yield instead of return
def count_up(start, end):
    current = start
    while current <= end:
        yield current        # pause here, return value, resume next call
        current += 1

gen = count_up(1, 5)
next(gen)    # 1
next(gen)    # 2
list(gen)    # [3, 4, 5]  ← consumes remaining

# Generator expression (like list comprehension but lazy)
gen = (x**2 for x in range(1000000))  # doesn't calculate all at once!
list_comp = [x**2 for x in range(1000000)]  # calculates everything immediately

# The iterator protocol
class CountUp:
    def __init__(self, start, end):
        self.current = start
        self.end = end

    def __iter__(self):   # makes object iterable
        return self

    def __next__(self):   # defines what "next" returns
        if self.current > self.end:
            raise StopIteration
        val = self.current
        self.current += 1
        return val

for n in CountUp(1, 5):
    print(n)     # 1 2 3 4 5
```

## 3.2 Closures ⭐

```python
# A closure is a function that remembers variables from its enclosing scope
def make_multiplier(n):
    def multiplier(x):
        return x * n      # n is from the enclosing scope — "closed over"
    return multiplier     # return the inner function

times3 = make_multiplier(3)
times5 = make_multiplier(5)

print(times3(10))   # 30
print(times5(10))   # 50
print(times3(4))    # 12

# The closure "remembers" the value of n from when it was created
```

## 3.3 Decorators ⭐

```python
# Decorator — a function that wraps another function
def my_decorator(func):
    def wrapper(*args, **kwargs):
        print("Before function call")
        result = func(*args, **kwargs)
        print("After function call")
        return result
    return wrapper

@my_decorator          # syntactic sugar for: greet = my_decorator(greet)
def greet(name):
    print(f"Hello, {name}!")

greet("Alice")
# Output:
# Before function call
# Hello, Alice!
# After function call

# Decorator with arguments
def repeat(times):
    def decorator(func):
        def wrapper(*args, **kwargs):
            for _ in range(times):
                func(*args, **kwargs)
        return wrapper
    return decorator

@repeat(3)
def say_hello():
    print("Hello!")

say_hello()   # prints "Hello!" 3 times

# Built-in decorators ⭐
class MyClass:
    class_var = 0

    @staticmethod
    def static_method():      # no self/cls
        pass

    @classmethod
    def class_method(cls):    # cls = class
        pass

    @property
    def my_prop(self):        # access like attribute
        return self._value
```

---

# PCAP Section 4: File I/O and String Operations

## 4.1 File Operations ⭐

```python
# Open and close (basic)
f = open("file.txt", "r")
content = f.read()
f.close()

# Better: context manager (auto-closes)
with open("file.txt", "r") as f:
    content = f.read()       # read entire file as string
    
with open("file.txt", "r") as f:
    lines = f.readlines()    # read as list of lines
    
with open("file.txt", "r") as f:
    for line in f:           # iterate line by line (memory efficient)
        print(line.strip())

# File modes
# "r" — read (default), text mode
# "w" — write (create or overwrite)
# "a" — append
# "r+" — read and write
# "rb" — read binary
# "wb" — write binary

# Writing files
with open("output.txt", "w") as f:
    f.write("Hello\n")
    f.write("World\n")
    f.writelines(["Line1\n", "Line2\n"])

# Appending
with open("log.txt", "a") as f:
    f.write("New log entry\n")

# File position
with open("file.txt", "r") as f:
    f.read(5)       # read 5 characters
    f.tell()        # current position
    f.seek(0)       # go back to start
```

## 4.2 Advanced String Operations

```python
# String formatting — detailed
name = "Alice"
score = 95.678

# f-strings (Python 3.6+) ⭐
f"{score:.2f}"           # '95.68' — 2 decimal places
f"{score:8.2f}"          # '   95.68' — width 8, 2 decimal
f"{name:>10}"            # '     Alice' — right-align width 10
f"{name:<10}"            # 'Alice     ' — left-align
f"{name:^10}"            # '  Alice   ' — center
f"{42:08b}"              # '00101010' — binary with zero-padding
f"{255:x}"               # 'ff' — hexadecimal
f"{1_000_000:,}"         # '1,000,000' — thousands separator

# String methods (review advanced ones)
"hello world".capitalize()      # 'Hello world'
"hello\nworld".splitlines()     # ['hello', 'world']
"   ".isspace()                 # True
"Hello World".swapcase()        # 'hELLO wORLD'
"hello".center(11)              # '   hello   '
"hello".center(11, "*")         # '***hello***'
"hello".ljust(10, ".")          # 'hello.....'
"hello".rjust(10, ".")          # '.....hello'
"abc".zfill(5)                  # '00abc'

# Regular expressions
import re
pattern = r"\d+"                # one or more digits
re.match(r"\d+", "123abc")      # matches at start
re.search(r"\d+", "abc123")     # search anywhere
re.findall(r"\d+", "a1b2c3")    # ['1', '2', '3']
re.sub(r"\d", "X", "a1b2c3")   # 'aXbXcX' — replace
re.split(r"\s+", "hello world") # ['hello', 'world']
```

## PCAP Practice Questions (25 questions)

**Q1.** What is the output?
```python
class A:
    def __init__(self):
        self.x = 1
class B(A):
    def __init__(self):
        super().__init__()
        self.y = 2
b = B()
print(b.x, b.y)
```
- A. Error  B. `1 2`  C. `None 2`  D. `1 None`

**Q2.** What does `__name__ == "__main__"` evaluate to when a file is imported as a module?
- A. True  B. False  C. AttributeError  D. None

**Q3.** What is the output?
```python
def gen():
    yield 1
    yield 2
    yield 3

g = gen()
print(next(g))
print(next(g))
```
- A. `1` then `2`  B. `1` then `1`  C. `[1,2,3]` then `[1,2,3]`  D. Error

**Q4.** Which decorator makes a method callable on the class itself rather than instances, receiving `cls` as first arg?
- A. `@staticmethod`  B. `@property`  C. `@classmethod`  D. `@abstractmethod`

**Q5.** What is the output?
```python
class Counter:
    count = 0
    def __init__(self):
        Counter.count += 1

a = Counter()
b = Counter()
print(a.count)
```
- A. 0  B. 1  C. 2  D. Error

**Q6.** What does a `@property` decorator do?
- A. Makes the method a class method  B. Makes the method a static method  
- C. Allows the method to be called without parentheses (like an attribute)  D. Makes the method private

**Q7.** What is the output?
```python
def make_adder(n):
    def adder(x):
        return x + n
    return adder

add5 = make_adder(5)
print(add5(3))
```
- A. 8  B. 5  C. 3  D. Error

**Q8.** What exception does `next()` raise when a generator is exhausted?
- A. `IndexError`  B. `GeneratorExit`  C. `StopIteration`  D. `RuntimeError`

**Q9.** What is the output?
```python
class A:
    x = "class"
    def __init__(self):
        self.x = "instance"

a = A()
del a.x
print(a.x)
```
- A. Error  B. `None`  C. `"instance"`  D. `"class"`

**Q10.** Which file mode opens a file for reading binary data?
- A. `"r"`  B. `"rb"`  C. `"br"`  D. `"b"`

**Q11.** What does `isinstance([], (list, tuple))` return?
- A. True  B. False  C. Error  D. `list`

**Q12.** What is the output?
```python
def decorator(func):
    def wrapper():
        print("before")
        func()
        print("after")
    return wrapper

@decorator
def hello():
    print("hello")

hello()
```
- A. `hello`  B. `before hello after`  C. `before after`  D. Error

**Q13.** What method must a class implement to make its instances iterable (for use in for loops)?
- A. `__next__` only  B. `__iter__` and `__next__`  C. `__loop__`  D. `__generate__`

**Q14.** What is the result of `f"{3.14159:.2f}"`?
- A. `"3.14"`  B. `"3.1"`  C. `"3.14159"`  D. `"3.15"`

**Q15.** What is the output?
```python
class Animal:
    def speak(self):
        return "..."

class Dog(Animal):
    def speak(self):
        return "Woof"

animals = [Animal(), Dog(), Dog()]
for a in animals:
    print(a.speak(), end=" ")
```
- A. `... ... ...`  B. `... Woof Woof`  C. `Woof Woof Woof`  D. Error

**Q16.** In a closure, if an inner function modifies a variable from the enclosing scope, what keyword must be used?
- A. `global`  B. `enclosed`  C. `nonlocal`  D. No keyword needed

**Q17.** What does `with open("f.txt", "w") as f:` guarantee?
- A. The file is opened in binary mode  B. The file is closed automatically when the with block exits  
- C. The file cannot be read  D. The file is opened for appending

**Q18.** What is the output?
```python
x = [1, 2, 3]
gen = (i**2 for i in x)
x.append(4)
print(list(gen))
```
- A. `[1, 4, 9]`  B. `[1, 4, 9, 16]`  C. Error  D. `[1, 4, 9, 16, ...]`

**Q19.** Which dunder method is called when `+` is used between two objects?
- A. `__plus__`  B. `__add__`  C. `__sum__`  D. `__concat__`

**Q20.** What does `re.findall(r"\d+", "price: 42 items: 7")` return?
- A. `"42"`  B. `["42", "7"]`  C. `["price:", "items:"]`  D. `42`

**Q21.** What is the output?
```python
class A:
    def method(self):
        print("A")

class B(A):
    pass

class C(A):
    def method(self):
        print("C")

class D(B, C):
    pass

D().method()
```
- A. `A`  B. `B`  C. `C`  D. Error

**Q22.** A generator expression `(x for x in range(10))` vs list comprehension `[x for x in range(10)]` — what is the key difference?
- A. Generator is faster for all cases  B. Generator computes values lazily (on demand), saving memory  
- C. List comprehension can only use integers  D. They are identical in behavior and memory

**Q23.** What is the output?
```python
import math
print(math.floor(-3.2), math.ceil(-3.2))
```
- A. `-3 -3`  B. `-4 -3`  C. `-3 -4`  D. `-4 -4`

**Q24.** Which import syntax allows using `sqrt` directly without the `math.` prefix?
- A. `import math`  B. `import math.sqrt`  C. `from math import sqrt`  D. `import sqrt from math`

**Q25.** What is the output?
```python
class Foo:
    def __bool__(self):
        return False
    def __len__(self):
        return 5

f = Foo()
print(bool(f))
```
- A. `True`  B. `False`  C. `5`  D. Error

### PCAP Answers

**Q1->B.** `super().__init__()` calls A's constructor, setting `x=1`. B's constructor also sets `y=2`. Both attributes exist.
**Q2->B.** False. `__name__` equals the module name (not `"__main__"`) when imported.
**Q3->A.** Each `next()` advances the generator to the next `yield`. First → 1, second → 2.
**Q4->C.** `@classmethod` receives `cls` as first argument. Callable on class and instances.
**Q5->C.** `Counter.count` is a class variable. After creating 2 instances, `count=2`. `a.count` accesses the class variable.
**Q6->C.** `@property` lets a method be accessed as `obj.method` without parentheses.
**Q7->A.** Closure: `adder` closes over `n=5`. `add5(3)` → `3 + 5 = 8`.
**Q8->C.** `StopIteration` is raised when a generator/iterator has no more values.
**Q9->D.** `del a.x` deletes the instance attribute. Python then falls back to the class attribute `"class"`.
**Q10->B.** `"rb"` = read binary mode.
**Q11->A.** `isinstance` accepts a tuple of types. `[]` is a list → True.
**Q12->B.** Decorator wraps `hello`. Calling `hello()` calls `wrapper()`: prints before, calls original `hello()`, prints after.
**Q13->B.** Both `__iter__` (returns the iterator) and `__next__` (returns next value) are needed for the iterator protocol.
**Q14->A.** `:.2f` = 2 decimal places. `3.14159` rounded to 2 = `3.14`.
**Q15->B.** Polymorphism: `Animal()` uses `Animal.speak()` → "...", `Dog()` uses overridden `Dog.speak()` → "Woof".
**Q16->C.** `nonlocal` keyword allows modification of enclosing scope (not global) variables.
**Q17->B.** Context manager (`with`) guarantees file close even if an exception occurs.
**Q18->B.** Generator expressions evaluate lazily — `x` is read when list is created, by which time `x=[1,2,3,4]`. Output: `[1, 4, 9, 16]`.
**Q19->B.** `__add__` is called for the `+` operator.
**Q20->B.** `\d+` matches one or more digits. Returns a list of all matches: `["42", "7"]`.
**Q21->C.** Python MRO (Method Resolution Order): D → B → C → A. B doesn't define `method`, so C's is used. Prints "C".
**Q22->B.** Generator is lazy — yields one value at a time. List comprehension creates the entire list in memory immediately.
**Q23->B.** `math.floor(-3.2) = -4` (rounds down toward −∞). `math.ceil(-3.2) = -3` (rounds up toward +∞).
**Q24->C.** `from math import sqrt` → use `sqrt()` directly.
**Q25->B.** `__bool__` takes priority over `__len__`. Returns `False`.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Python Cheatsheets

## Built-in Functions Quick Reference ⭐

| Function | Description | Example |
|---|---|---|
| `len(x)` | Length | `len([1,2,3])` → 3 |
| `range(n)` | 0 to n-1 | `list(range(3))` → [0,1,2] |
| `type(x)` | Type of object | `type(42)` → int |
| `int/float/str/bool(x)` | Type conversion | `int("42")` → 42 |
| `sorted(x)` | Return sorted list | `sorted([3,1,2])` → [1,2,3] |
| `reversed(x)` | Return reversed iterator | `list(reversed([1,2,3]))` → [3,2,1] |
| `enumerate(x)` | (index, value) pairs | `list(enumerate("ab"))` → [(0,'a'),(1,'b')] |
| `zip(a,b)` | Pair elements | `list(zip([1,2],[3,4]))` → [(1,3),(2,4)] |
| `map(f, x)` | Apply function | `list(map(str,[1,2]))` → ['1','2'] |
| `filter(f, x)` | Filter by function | `list(filter(bool,[0,1,2]))` → [1,2] |
| `sum(x)` | Sum elements | `sum([1,2,3])` → 6 |
| `max/min(x)` | Max/min | `max([3,1,2])` → 3 |
| `abs(x)` | Absolute value | `abs(-5)` → 5 |
| `round(x,n)` | Round to n decimals | `round(3.14159,2)` → 3.14 |
| `id(x)` | Object identity (memory address) | `id(a) == id(b)` checks same object |
| `hash(x)` | Hash value | `hash("hello")` |
| `dir(x)` | List attributes | `dir([])` → list of list methods |
| `isinstance(x,T)` | Type check | `isinstance(1, int)` → True |
| `issubclass(A,B)` | Inheritance check | `issubclass(bool, int)` → True |

## Common Exam Traps

1. **`round(2.5)` = 2** (banker's rounding, not 3)
2. **Mutable default arguments persist** between calls
3. **`{}`** is empty dict, not empty set. Empty set = `set()`
4. **`y = x`** (list) makes y point to same object — not a copy
5. **`for...else`**: `else` runs only when loop finishes without `break`
6. **`**` is right-to-left**: `2**3**2 = 2**9 = 512`, not `8**2 = 64`
7. **`[0]` is truthy** — list with one element (even if that element is 0)
8. **`"hello".split("l")`** → `['he', '', 'o']` — double separator creates empty string
9. **`MRO (Method Resolution Order)`**: left to right depth-first (D → B → C → A for `class D(B,C)`)
10. **Generator expressions are lazy** — they don't evaluate until consumed
11. **`del a.x`** deletes the instance attribute — class attribute still accessible via `a.x`
12. **`__bool__` overrides `__len__`** for truthiness check
13. **`nonlocal` for enclosing scope**, `global` for module scope
14. **`@property` method accessed without `()`** — `obj.prop` not `obj.prop()`
15. **Static methods don't receive `self` or `cls`** — pure utility functions

# Preface

## Why Security+ matters

Security+ (SY0-701) is the global entry-level cybersecurity certification. It meets DoD 8570/8140 requirements, meaning if you want to work for US government contractors, financial institutions, healthcare organizations, or large enterprises in virtually any country, employers recognize this cert. It's your proof that you understand how attackers think and how defenders respond.

If you're coming from CLF-C02 and the AWS path, you already know a lot of the cloud security concepts. This cert extends that into vendor-neutral territory: Windows, Linux, networks, cryptography, governance, incident response, and threat intelligence.

## Performance-Based Questions (PBQs)

PBQs simulate real scenarios: configure a firewall, analyze a packet capture, identify vulnerabilities in a network diagram. They appear early in the exam and take 3–5 minutes each.

**PBQ strategy:** When the exam starts, SKIP the PBQs and answer all multiple-choice questions first. Then return with remaining time. A flagged PBQ with a random guess is better than running out of time on the MCQs.

## Study plan

**6-week plan:**
- Weeks 1–2: Domains 1 & 2 (concepts + threats)
- Weeks 3–4: Domains 3 & 4 (architecture + operations)
- Week 5: Domain 5 + comparison tables + cheat sheets + Mock 1
- Week 6: Mock 2 + Mock 3 + wrong-answer review

> **Exam Tip:** Security+ loves "best" answers. Many questions have two answers that are both technically correct — you must pick the ONE that is MOST appropriate, MOST effective, or FIRST in the process. Read every option before deciding.
