# Domain 1: General Security Concepts (12%)

## 1.1 Security Fundamentals

### CIA Triad

The foundation of all security thinking:

**Confidentiality** — only authorized people can read the data. Broken by: data leaks, eavesdropping, unauthorized access.

**Integrity** — data hasn't been altered without authorization. Broken by: tampering, man-in-the-middle attacks that modify data, malware that corrupts files. Protected by: hashing, digital signatures.

**Availability** — systems are accessible when needed. Broken by: DDoS attacks, ransomware, hardware failures. Protected by: redundancy, backups, DDoS mitigation.

### Additional properties

**Authentication** — verify who you are (you are who you claim to be).
**Authorization** — what are you allowed to do (access control after authentication).
**Accounting (Non-repudiation)** — you can prove an action occurred (audit logs, digital signatures).

These three together form **AAA** (Authentication, Authorization, Accounting).

**Non-repudiation** — you cannot deny having performed an action. Achieved via digital signatures and comprehensive audit logs.

### Security control types

Controls are classified two ways — by **category** and by **function**:

**By category:**
- **Technical** (also called logical) — implemented in technology: firewalls, IDS, encryption, access control lists
- **Administrative** (also called managerial) — policies, procedures, training, security awareness
- **Physical** — fences, locks, cameras, guards, security badges

**By function:**
- **Preventive** — prevent an incident from occurring (firewall, encryption, access control)
- **Detective** — identify that an incident has occurred (IDS, audit logs, SIEM)
- **Corrective** — fix things after an incident (patch management, restoring from backups)
- **Deterrent** — discourage attackers (warning signs, visible cameras, legal notices)
- **Compensating** — alternative control when primary isn't possible (manual review instead of automated)
- **Directive** — tell people what to do (policies, procedures, mandatory training)

> **Exam Tip:** A firewall is both a Technical control and a Preventive control. A camera is both a Physical control and a Detective/Deterrent control. Questions often ask about BOTH classifications.

### Zero Trust Architecture

Traditional "castle and moat" security trusted everything inside the network. Zero Trust assumes breach:

- **Never trust, always verify** — every request is authenticated and authorized, regardless of where it comes from
- **Least privilege access** — minimal permissions for everything
- **Assume breach** — act as if attackers are already inside; monitor everything
- **Microsegmentation** — divide the network into small segments so a breach in one doesn't spread

Components: Identity providers (IdP), Multi-factor authentication, device compliance checks, policy enforcement points.

## 1.2 Cryptography Basics

### Symmetric encryption

One key for both encryption and decryption. Fast. Good for bulk data.

Common algorithms:
- **AES (Advanced Encryption Standard)** — current standard. Key sizes: 128, 192, 256 bits. 256-bit is strongest. Used everywhere.
- **3DES (Triple DES)** — legacy, being phased out. Three passes of DES. Much slower than AES.
- **ChaCha20** — stream cipher, used in TLS 1.3 and mobile devices (fast without hardware acceleration)

Problem: How do you share the key securely? → Use asymmetric encryption to exchange symmetric keys (this is what TLS does).

### Asymmetric encryption

Two mathematically related keys: **public key** (share freely) and **private key** (keep secret).

- Encrypt with public key → only the private key can decrypt (confidentiality)
- Sign with private key → anyone with the public key can verify the signature (authentication/non-repudiation)

Common algorithms:
- **RSA** — most widely used. Key sizes: 2048, 3072, 4096 bits (2048+ is current minimum).
- **ECC (Elliptic Curve Cryptography)** — same security as RSA but with much smaller keys (256-bit ECC ≈ 3072-bit RSA). Faster, less power. Used in TLS, mobile, IoT.
- **Diffie-Hellman** — key exchange algorithm (not encryption itself). Allows two parties to establish a shared secret over a public channel. **ECDH** is the elliptic curve version. **DHE/ECDHE** adds **Perfect Forward Secrecy** (PFS) — even if the server's private key is later compromised, past sessions can't be decrypted.

> **Common Trap:** RSA can both encrypt AND sign. ECC is mainly used for signing and key exchange. Diffie-Hellman is ONLY for key exchange, not encryption.

### Hashing

One-way mathematical function. Same input always produces same output (hash/digest). Cannot reverse the hash to get the original data. Used to verify integrity.

Common hash algorithms:
- **MD5** — 128-bit output. BROKEN (collision attacks). Don't use for security.
- **SHA-1** — 160-bit. DEPRECATED. Don't use for new applications.
- **SHA-256** (part of SHA-2) — 256-bit. Current standard.
- **SHA-3** — newer standard, different algorithm from SHA-2.
- **bcrypt / scrypt / Argon2** — specifically designed for password hashing. Slow by design (makes brute force expensive). Includes salt.

**Salt** — random data added to a password before hashing. Prevents rainbow table attacks (precomputed hash tables). Each user's hash is unique even with the same password.

**HMAC (Hash-based Message Authentication Code)** — combines a hash with a secret key. Provides both integrity AND authentication. Used to verify that data wasn't tampered with AND came from the expected sender.

### Key exchange and PKI

**PKI (Public Key Infrastructure)** — the system for issuing and managing digital certificates.

**Digital Certificate** — ties a public key to an identity. Issued and signed by a Certificate Authority (CA). Contains: subject name, public key, issuer (CA), validity period, serial number, signature.

**Certificate Authority (CA)** — trusted entity that issues certificates. Root CA is at the top; Intermediate CAs issue to end entities.

**Chain of trust** — end-entity cert → intermediate CA cert → root CA cert. Browser trusts the root CA and validates the chain.

**CRL (Certificate Revocation List)** — list of revoked certificates. Published by CA.
**OCSP (Online Certificate Status Protocol)** — real-time certificate status check. Faster than downloading CRL.
**OCSP Stapling** — server includes OCSP response in TLS handshake. Faster than client checking OCSP.

**Certificate types:**
- **DV (Domain Validation)** — verifies domain ownership only. Fastest/cheapest. For most websites.
- **OV (Organization Validation)** — verifies the organization. For businesses.
- **EV (Extended Validation)** — extensive verification. Shows organization name in browser.
- **Wildcard** — valid for `*.domain.com` and all subdomains.
- **SAN (Subject Alternative Name)** — valid for multiple different domains in one cert.

## 1.3 Authentication and Access Control

### Authentication factors (MFA)

Three classic factors:
- **Something you know** — password, PIN, security question
- **Something you have** — hardware token (FIDO2/WebAuthn key), soft token (TOTP app), smart card
- **Something you are** — biometrics: fingerprint, face, iris, voice

**Multi-factor authentication (MFA)** — requires two or more DIFFERENT factor types. Password + TOTP = MFA. Two passwords = NOT MFA (same factor type).

Additional factors:
- **Somewhere you are** — geolocation, IP address range
- **Something you do** — behavioral biometrics (typing pattern, gait)

### Authentication protocols

**Kerberos** — ticket-based authentication for Windows domains. Uses a Key Distribution Center (KDC) with two parts: Authentication Server (AS) and Ticket Granting Server (TGS). Provides SSO within the domain. Vulnerable to: Pass-the-ticket, Pass-the-hash, Golden Ticket (forged TGT), Silver Ticket (forged service ticket).

**LDAP (Lightweight Directory Access Protocol)** — protocol for accessing directory services (like Active Directory). Runs on port 389 (plaintext) or 636 (LDAPS/TLS-encrypted).

**NTLM (New Technology LAN Manager)** — older Microsoft authentication. Uses challenge-response. Vulnerable to pass-the-hash attacks. Being replaced by Kerberos.

**RADIUS (Remote Authentication Dial-In User Service)** — used for network access control (VPN, wireless). Authenticates users against a central database. Runs on UDP ports 1812 (auth) and 1813 (accounting).

**TACACS+ (Terminal Access Controller Access Control System Plus)** — Cisco's AAA protocol. Encrypts the entire payload (vs RADIUS which only encrypts password). Used for device administration (routers, switches).

**OAuth 2.0** — authorization framework for third-party access (e.g., "Login with Google"). Provides access tokens. NOT an authentication protocol itself.

**OpenID Connect (OIDC)** — authentication layer built ON TOP of OAuth 2.0. Provides ID tokens (JWTs) that authenticate users. "Login with Google" uses both.

**SAML (Security Assertion Markup Language)** — XML-based federation standard. Used for enterprise SSO. Browser-based. Example: logging into corporate apps via your Active Directory credentials.

> **Exam Tip:** "Enterprise SSO with Active Directory" → SAML. "Web app 'Login with Google'" → OAuth 2.0/OIDC. "Network VPN authentication" → RADIUS. "Cisco device admin" → TACACS+. "Windows domain authentication" → Kerberos.

### Access control models

**DAC (Discretionary Access Control)** — resource owner sets permissions. Most flexible. Example: file owner grants access to others. Linux file permissions with user/group/other.

**MAC (Mandatory Access Control)** — system (not users) enforces access based on labels (Top Secret, Secret, Confidential). Users cannot change labels. Used in military/government. Example: SELinux.

**RBAC (Role-Based Access Control)** — permissions assigned to roles; users assigned to roles. Most common in enterprise. Example: HR role has access to HR systems; Finance role to finance systems.

**ABAC (Attribute-Based Access Control)** — access based on attributes (user department, device location, time of day). Most granular and flexible. Example: Nurse can access patient records only during their shift hours.

**Rule-Based Access Control** — access based on rules (firewall ACLs, time-of-day rules). Not the same as RBAC despite similar name.

### Privileged Access Management (PAM)

Controls, monitors, and audits access to privileged accounts (admin accounts, root, service accounts).

Features: Just-In-Time (JIT) access (temporary privilege elevation), session recording, password vaulting, privilege escalation workflows.

**Principle of Least Privilege** — grant only the minimum access needed for the task.

**Separation of Duties** — no single person has end-to-end control of a critical process. Example: the person who requests a payment can't also approve it.

**Need to Know** — access only to information needed for the job function.

## 1.4 Domain 1 Mini Quiz (8 questions)

**Q1.** A user cannot access a file they own because a label says "Confidential" and their clearance is "Secret." Which access control model is this?

- A. DAC
- B. RBAC
- C. MAC
- D. ABAC

**Q2.** An attacker intercepts communication between a client and server and reads the message. Which CIA property was violated?

- A. Integrity
- B. Availability
- C. Confidentiality
- D. Non-repudiation

**Q3.** Which cryptographic algorithm is designed SPECIFICALLY for password storage with intentional slowness?

- A. SHA-256
- B. MD5
- C. AES-256
- D. bcrypt

**Q4.** A company uses RADIUS for VPN authentication. What does RADIUS provide?

- A. Encrypts all VPN traffic end-to-end
- B. Centralized authentication for network access
- C. Encrypts the entire RADIUS payload
- D. Provides device administration AAA

**Q5.** Which key exchange mechanism provides Perfect Forward Secrecy?

- A. RSA key exchange
- B. DH (static Diffie-Hellman)
- C. ECDHE (Ephemeral Elliptic Curve Diffie-Hellman)
- D. AES key wrapping

**Q6.** A security camera in a parking lot is an example of which type of control?

- A. Technical, Preventive
- B. Physical, Detective and Deterrent
- C. Administrative, Corrective
- D. Technical, Detective

**Q7.** An employee needs admin access to a server for exactly 2 hours to perform maintenance. Which PAM concept provides this?

- A. Separation of Duties
- B. Principle of Least Privilege
- C. Just-In-Time (JIT) access
- D. RBAC role assignment

**Q8.** Which protocol is used for enterprise SSO where a user logs in once to Active Directory and gains access to cloud apps?

- A. OAuth 2.0
- B. RADIUS
- C. SAML
- D. TACACS+

### Domain 1 Answers

**Q1 -> C.** MAC uses system-enforced labels; users can't override. The label ("Confidential") and clearance ("Secret") are MAC concepts.
**Q2 -> C.** Reading intercepted data = confidentiality violation (unauthorized disclosure).
**Q3 -> D.** bcrypt (and Argon2, scrypt) are designed for password hashing with deliberate slowness. SHA-256/MD5 are too fast for passwords.
**Q4 -> B.** RADIUS = centralized authentication for network access (VPN, WiFi). TACACS+ encrypts the full payload (not RADIUS).
**Q5 -> C.** ECDHE (and DHE) are ephemeral — new key pair each session = PFS. RSA key exchange uses the same private key = no PFS.
**Q6 -> B.** Camera = Physical (not digital) + Detective (captures what happened) + Deterrent (visible camera discourages attackers).
**Q7 -> C.** JIT = temporary privilege elevation for a defined time window.
**Q8 -> C.** SAML = enterprise browser-based SSO federation (AD → cloud apps).

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Domain 2: Threats, Vulnerabilities, and Mitigations (22%)

This is the domain where you learn how attackers think. Know the attack types, how to recognize them, and what stops them.

## 2.1 Malware Types

**Virus** — self-replicating code that attaches to legitimate files. Requires user action to spread (opening an infected file). Types: file infector, macro virus, boot sector virus.

**Worm** — self-replicating, spreads automatically over networks WITHOUT user action. Example: WannaCry, SQL Slammer.

**Trojan** — malware disguised as legitimate software. Does NOT self-replicate. Relies on user installing it. Example: fake antivirus software.

**Ransomware** — encrypts victim's files, demands payment for the decryption key. Modern ransomware also exfiltrates data ("double extortion"). Defense: offline backups, MFA, email filtering, EDR.

**Spyware** — secretly monitors activity (keystrokes, screenshots, webcam). Collects information without consent.

**Adware** — displays unwanted ads. Usually less harmful but often bundled with spyware.

**Rootkit** — hides itself and other malware in the OS kernel or bootloader. Very hard to detect. May require offline scanning or re-imaging.

**Bootkit** — rootkit that infects the Master Boot Record (MBR). Loads before the OS and AV software.

**Keylogger** — records keystrokes. Can be software or hardware (physical device attached to keyboard port).

**RAT (Remote Access Trojan)** — gives attacker remote control of the victim's system. Often used as part of an APT.

**Fileless malware** — lives entirely in memory, doesn't write to disk. Uses legitimate tools (PowerShell, WMI) to execute. Hard to detect with traditional AV.

**Botnet** — network of compromised computers (bots) controlled by an attacker (C2/C&C server). Used for: DDoS, spam, credential stuffing, cryptocurrency mining.

**Command and Control (C2/C&C)** — infrastructure used to control malware. Modern botnets use HTTP/HTTPS or peer-to-peer C2 to blend with normal traffic.

> **Common Trap:** Worms spread without user action. Viruses and Trojans need user action. All three can carry payloads (ransomware, spyware, etc.).

## 2.2 Social Engineering

Social engineering exploits human psychology, not technical vulnerabilities.

### Phishing variants

**Phishing** — mass email campaign pretending to be a trusted entity to steal credentials or deliver malware.

**Spear phishing** — targeted phishing at a specific individual or organization. More convincing because it's personalized.

**Whaling** — spear phishing targeting senior executives (CEO, CFO). High-value target.

**Vishing (voice phishing)** — phone calls pretending to be bank, IRS, IT support.

**Smishing (SMS phishing)** — phishing via text message.

**Pharming** — redirects users from a legitimate website to a fake one. Can involve DNS poisoning or modifying the hosts file.

### Other social engineering techniques

**Pretexting** — creating a fabricated scenario (pretext) to manipulate someone. Example: "I'm from IT, I need your password to fix your account."

**Baiting** — leaving infected USB drives in parking lots, waiting for curious employees to plug them in.

**Tailgating/Piggybacking** — following an authorized person through a secured door without badging in.

**Impersonation** — pretending to be someone else (IT support, vendor, executive).

**Business Email Compromise (BEC)** — compromising or impersonating executive email to trick employees into wire transfers or changing payee details.

**Watering hole attack** — infecting a website that the target's employees frequently visit.

**Typosquatting (URL hijacking)** — registering domains that look like legitimate ones (gooogle.com, amaz0n.com).

### Social engineering defenses

- Security awareness training
- Phishing simulation exercises
- Email filtering and DMARC/DKIM/SPF
- Multi-factor authentication (stolen passwords alone won't work)
- Verification procedures (call back to known number, not the one provided by caller)

## 2.3 Network-Based Attacks

### Denial of Service (DoS/DDoS)

**DoS** — single attacker floods a target to make it unavailable.

**DDoS (Distributed Denial of Service)** — thousands of compromised hosts (botnet) flood a target simultaneously.

Attack types:
- **Volumetric** — flood with traffic (UDP flood, ICMP flood). Measured in Gbps.
- **Protocol** — exploit protocol weaknesses (SYN flood — fills TCP connection table with half-open connections).
- **Application layer (Layer 7)** — target the application specifically (HTTP flood, Slowloris).

**DDoS mitigation:** CDN (CloudFront), DDoS protection services (AWS Shield, Cloudflare), rate limiting, WAF.

### Man-in-the-Middle (MITM) / On-Path Attacks

Attacker intercepts communication between two parties. Can read, modify, or inject traffic.

**ARP Poisoning** — sends fake ARP replies to associate attacker's MAC with victim's IP. Works on local network.
**DNS Poisoning (DNS Cache Poisoning)** — injects false DNS records to redirect traffic.
**SSL Stripping** — downgrades HTTPS to HTTP. Prevented by HSTS (HTTP Strict Transport Security).
**Evil Twin** — rogue wireless access point with same SSID as legitimate one.

**Defense:** TLS/HTTPS, certificate pinning, VPN on untrusted networks, HSTS.

### Wireless attacks

**Deauthentication attack** — sends forged 802.11 deauth frames to disconnect clients. Used to force clients to reconnect (capturing WPA2 handshake for cracking).

**WPS (Wi-Fi Protected Setup) attack** — WPS PIN is brute-forceable (only 8 digits, split in half = 11,000 combinations). Disable WPS.

**WEP cracking** — WEP is completely broken. Statistical analysis of IVs breaks it quickly. Never use WEP.

**WPA2 attacks** — capture 4-way handshake, then offline dictionary/brute-force attack on the pre-shared key.

**WPA3** — current best standard. Uses SAE (Simultaneous Authentication of Equals) instead of PSK. Resistant to offline dictionary attacks.

**Rogue AP** — unauthorized access point on the network. Can be used for MITM.

### Other network attacks

**Replay attack** — capturing a valid authentication token and replaying it. Prevented by nonces and timestamps.

**Session hijacking** — stealing a session cookie after authentication to impersonate the user.

**Pass-the-Hash (PtH)** — using a captured NTLM hash directly (without cracking it) to authenticate. Prevented by: Kerberos (doesn't use NTLM hashes), MFA, network segmentation, Credential Guard.

**Pass-the-Ticket** — stealing Kerberos tickets for lateral movement.

**Golden Ticket attack** — compromising the Kerberos KDC and forging Ticket Granting Tickets (TGTs). Allows persistent admin access. Very difficult to remediate.

**DNS attacks:**
- **DNS spoofing** — providing false DNS responses
- **DNS amplification** — DDoS technique using open DNS resolvers
- **DNS tunneling** — encoding data in DNS queries to bypass firewalls

## 2.4 Application Attacks

### Injection attacks

**SQL Injection (SQLi)** — inserting SQL commands into user input fields. Can read/modify/delete database data, bypass authentication.

Example: `' OR '1'='1' --` in a login field makes the query always true.

Prevention: parameterized queries (prepared statements), input validation, WAF.

**Command Injection** — injecting OS commands through vulnerable applications. Example: `; rm -rf /` in a file name field.

**LDAP Injection** — injecting LDAP queries.

**XXE (XML External Entity)** — exploiting XML parsers to read files or do SSRF.

### Cross-Site Scripting (XSS)

Injecting malicious scripts into web pages that execute in other users' browsers.

**Reflected XSS** — script is in the URL, reflected back to the user. URL-based.
**Stored (Persistent) XSS** — script is stored in the database and served to all visitors. More dangerous.
**DOM-based XSS** — script executes in the browser using DOM manipulation.

Used to: steal cookies, redirect users, perform actions as the victim.

Prevention: Content Security Policy (CSP), input validation, output encoding, HttpOnly cookie flag.

### Cross-Site Request Forgery (CSRF)

Tricks an authenticated user's browser into making unwanted requests to a site they're logged into.

Example: victim visits malicious page → page contains hidden form that submits to bank.com → transfers money as the logged-in user.

Prevention: CSRF tokens (random tokens validated server-side), SameSite cookie attribute.

### Buffer overflow

Writing more data to a buffer than it can hold, overwriting adjacent memory. Can overwrite return addresses to redirect code execution.

**Stack overflow** — overflow on the stack (overwrites return address).
**Heap overflow** — overflow on the heap.

Prevention: ASLR (Address Space Layout Randomization), DEP/NX (Data Execution Prevention), stack canaries, safe languages.

### Other application vulnerabilities

**Insecure Direct Object Reference (IDOR)** — accessing resources by manipulating IDs in URLs (change `user_id=123` to `user_id=124`). Fixed by access control checks.

**Directory traversal (Path traversal)** — using `../` to navigate outside the web root and read sensitive files.

**Race condition** — timing bug where the outcome depends on the sequence of events. TOCTOU (Time of Check Time of Use).

**Insecure deserialization** — deserializing untrusted data can lead to code execution.

**Server-Side Request Forgery (SSRF)** — tricking a server into making requests to internal resources. Can expose cloud metadata endpoints.

## 2.5 Threat Intelligence and Analysis

### Threat intelligence concepts

**Threat actor types:**
- **Nation-state** — government-sponsored, highly sophisticated, well-resourced. Targets: critical infrastructure, government, defense. APT (Advanced Persistent Threat).
- **Cybercriminal** — financially motivated. Ransomware, BEC, fraud.
- **Hacktivist** — ideologically motivated. DDoS, defacement, data leaks.
- **Insider threat** — employee, contractor, or business partner who misuses access. Can be malicious or accidental.
- **Script kiddie** — low skill, uses existing tools without understanding them.
- **Competitor** — corporate espionage.

**Threat attributes:**
- **APT (Advanced Persistent Threat)** — sophisticated, long-term attack, often nation-state. Evades detection, maintains access for months/years.

**Indicators of Compromise (IoC)** — evidence that an attack has occurred:
- Unusual outbound traffic to unknown IPs
- Unexpected account activity (login from unusual location/time)
- Unexplained files or processes
- Changed file hashes
- Abnormal registry entries
- Known malicious IP/domain/file hash

**TTPs (Tactics, Techniques, and Procedures)** — how threat actors operate. Used to understand attacker behavior and improve defenses.

**MITRE ATT&CK Framework** — globally accessible knowledge base of adversary tactics and techniques based on real-world observations. Organized by phases of an attack (Initial Access → Execution → Persistence → Privilege Escalation → etc.).

**Threat feeds / OSINT (Open Source Intelligence)** — sources of threat intelligence:
- Commercial threat feeds (CrowdStrike, Recorded Future)
- Government: US-CERT, CISA, ISAC
- Open source: VirusTotal, MISP, AbuseIPDB, Shodan

### Vulnerability scanning and assessment

**Vulnerability scan** — automated scan of systems against a database of known vulnerabilities. Non-destructive.
- **Credentialed scan** — scanner logs into the target (more thorough, finds more vulnerabilities, lower false positives)
- **Non-credentialed (unauthenticated) scan** — no credentials (misses internal vulnerabilities, as seen from outside)
- **Active scan** — sends probes to the target
- **Passive scan** — monitors traffic, doesn't send probes

**Penetration testing** — authorized attack to find exploitable vulnerabilities.
- **Black box** — no prior knowledge (simulates external attacker)
- **White box** — full knowledge of the environment (most thorough)
- **Gray box** — partial knowledge (simulates insider or external attacker with some information)
- **Red team** — adversarial simulation with offensive TTPs, physical access, social engineering included

**CVSS (Common Vulnerability Scoring System)** — standardized 0–10 score for vulnerability severity. Critical (9.0–10), High (7.0–8.9), Medium (4.0–6.9), Low (0.1–3.9).

**CVE (Common Vulnerabilities and Exposures)** — standard naming for vulnerabilities (e.g., CVE-2021-44228 = Log4Shell).

## 2.6 Domain 2 Mini Quiz (10 questions)

**Q1.** Malware that spreads automatically across a network without user interaction is which type?

- A. Virus
- B. Trojan
- C. Worm
- D. Ransomware

**Q2.** An attacker calls an employee pretending to be from the IT helpdesk and asks for their password to "fix an issue." This is:

- A. Phishing
- B. Pretexting
- C. Baiting
- D. Tailgating

**Q3.** An attacker inserts `' OR '1'='1' --` into a login field and bypasses authentication. What attack type is this?

- A. XSS
- B. CSRF
- C. SQL Injection
- D. Buffer overflow

**Q4.** An attacker captures a valid WPA2 4-way handshake and performs an offline dictionary attack. What is the BEST defense against this?

- A. Switch to WEP
- B. Disable SSID broadcast
- C. Use a long, complex passphrase or migrate to WPA3
- D. Enable WPS

**Q5.** Which type of malware is most difficult to detect because it runs entirely in memory without writing files to disk?

- A. Rootkit
- B. Fileless malware
- C. Spyware
- D. Adware

**Q6.** A website stores user-submitted comments in a database. When other users visit the page, a malicious script executes in their browsers. What type of XSS attack is this?

- A. Reflected XSS
- B. DOM-based XSS
- C. Stored (Persistent) XSS
- D. CSRF

**Q7.** An attacker sends a SYN flood to a web server, filling up the TCP connection table. What type of attack is this?

- A. Volumetric DDoS
- B. Protocol-level DDoS (TCP SYN flood)
- C. Application layer DDoS
- D. DNS amplification

**Q8.** Which framework provides a knowledge base of adversary tactics and techniques organized by attack phases?

- A. NIST CSF
- B. MITRE ATT&CK
- C. ISO 27001
- D. CVE

**Q9.** An authorized security consultant performs a penetration test with full knowledge of the network diagrams, system documentation, and credentials. What is this called?

- A. Black box
- B. Gray box
- C. White box
- D. Red team

**Q10.** A targeted phishing email sent specifically to a company's CFO is called:

- A. Phishing
- B. Spear phishing
- C. Whaling
- D. Vishing

### Domain 2 Answers

**Q1 -> C.** Worms self-replicate and spread automatically. Viruses need user action; Trojans disguise themselves; ransomware encrypts files.

**Q2 -> B.** Pretexting = fabricated scenario (pretext) to manipulate someone. Phone-based impersonation often involves pretexting.

**Q3 -> C.** SQL injection inserts SQL commands into input fields.

**Q4 -> C.** Long complex passphrase makes dictionary attack impractical. WPA3 uses SAE (resistant to offline dictionary attacks).

**Q5 -> B.** Fileless malware = no files written to disk, runs in memory, uses legitimate OS tools.

**Q6 -> C.** Stored XSS = script stored in DB, served to all visitors.

**Q7 -> B.** SYN flood = protocol-level DDoS exploiting TCP 3-way handshake weakness.

**Q8 -> B.** MITRE ATT&CK = adversary TTPs knowledge base organized by attack phases.

**Q9 -> C.** White box = full knowledge. Black box = no knowledge. Gray box = partial knowledge.

**Q10 -> C.** Whaling = spear phishing targeting senior executives.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Domain 3: Security Architecture (18%)

## 3.1 Network Security Design

### Network segmentation

Dividing a network into zones so that a breach in one zone doesn't automatically compromise others.

**DMZ (Demilitarized Zone)** — a perimeter network between the internet and the internal network. Public-facing servers (web, mail, DNS) go here. Even if the DMZ is compromised, the internal network is protected by an additional firewall.

**VLANs (Virtual Local Area Networks)** — logical network segments on the same physical infrastructure. Isolate traffic between departments. VLANs are logical; subnets are IP-level. Both are used together.

**VLAN hopping** — attacker sends double-tagged 802.1Q frames to jump between VLANs. Mitigated by disabling DTP, setting native VLAN to unused VLAN, pruning VLANs.

**Microsegmentation** — applying zero-trust, granular firewall rules between individual workloads (not just between network zones). Common in SDN and cloud environments.

**Air gap** — physically isolated network with no connection to other networks or the internet. Highest security (nuclear control systems, classified military networks). Covert channels: USB drives, electromagnetic emissions.

### Firewall types

**Packet filtering firewall** — inspects individual packets based on IP, port, and protocol. Stateless. Fast but limited.

**Stateful inspection firewall** — tracks connection state. Knows a packet is part of an established connection. Much smarter than packet filtering.

**Application-aware (Layer 7) firewall / NGFW (Next-Generation Firewall)** — deep packet inspection, understands application protocols (HTTP, DNS, SSL), can block specific apps. Includes IPS, URL filtering, user identity.

**WAF (Web Application Firewall)** — Layer 7, specifically for HTTP/HTTPS traffic. Protects against SQLi, XSS, OWASP Top 10.

**UTM (Unified Threat Management)** — all-in-one security appliance: firewall + IPS + antivirus + spam filter + VPN. Common in small businesses.

### IDS vs IPS

**IDS (Intrusion Detection System)** — monitors and ALERTS on suspicious activity. Does NOT block traffic. Passive.

**IPS (Intrusion Prevention System)** — monitors and BLOCKS suspicious activity. Active. Can be a bottleneck if misconfigured (false positives block legitimate traffic).

Detection methods:
- **Signature-based** — matches traffic against known attack patterns. Fast, accurate for known attacks. Cannot detect zero-days.
- **Anomaly-based (behavior-based)** — establishes a baseline of normal behavior, alerts on deviations. Can detect zero-days. Higher false positive rate.
- **Heuristic** — uses rules and algorithms to identify suspicious behavior patterns.

**Network-based (NIDS/NIPS)** — monitors network traffic. Placed inline (IPS) or on a SPAN/TAP port (IDS).

**Host-based (HIDS/HIPS)** — runs on individual hosts. Monitors system calls, file changes, log entries.

### Proxy servers and gateways

**Forward proxy** — sits between clients and the internet. Clients send all traffic through it. Used for: content filtering, anonymization, caching.

**Reverse proxy** — sits in front of servers, hides server infrastructure from clients. Used for: load balancing, SSL termination, WAF, caching.

**Transparent proxy** — intercepts traffic without client configuration. Clients may not know it exists.

**Content filter** — inspects and blocks traffic based on content categories (adult, gambling, malware sites).

### VPN types

**Remote access VPN** — individual user connects to the corporate network from anywhere. Client-to-site. Software client on user's device.

**Site-to-site VPN** — connects two networks (data center to branch office). Router-to-router. No individual client software needed.

**SSL/TLS VPN** — uses HTTPS port (443). Works through firewalls and proxies. Browser-based or thin client. Common for remote access.

**IPSec VPN** — network layer security. Two modes:
- **Transport mode** — encrypts only the payload, not the IP header. Used for host-to-host.
- **Tunnel mode** — encrypts the entire original packet and adds new IP headers. Used for VPNs.

IPSec components:
- **AH (Authentication Header)** — provides authentication and integrity, NO encryption.
- **ESP (Encapsulating Security Payload)** — provides authentication, integrity, AND encryption.
- **IKE (Internet Key Exchange)** — negotiates keys for IPSec.

> **Exam Tip:** IPSec ESP provides confidentiality (encryption). AH does NOT encrypt. For full protection, use ESP (or ESP + AH together).

### Split tunneling

VPN configuration where only corporate traffic goes through the VPN, and internet traffic goes directly. Improves VPN performance but reduces control. Security concern: infected device can be on both the internet AND the VPN simultaneously.

## 3.2 Cloud and Hybrid Security

### Cloud deployment models

**Public cloud** — resources shared across multiple customers (AWS, Azure, GCP). Lower cost, less control.
**Private cloud** — dedicated resources for one organization. More control, higher cost.
**Hybrid cloud** — mix of on-premises/private + public. Most common enterprise approach.
**Community cloud** — shared by organizations with similar requirements (government, healthcare).

### Cloud service models (security responsibility)

**IaaS (Infrastructure as a Service)** — AWS EC2, Azure VMs. Customer manages: OS, middleware, apps, data. Provider manages: hardware, networking, hypervisor.

**PaaS (Platform as a Service)** — AWS Elastic Beanstalk, Azure App Service. Customer manages: apps and data. Provider manages: OS, runtime, middleware.

**SaaS (Software as a Service)** — Office 365, Salesforce. Customer manages: data configuration, user access. Provider manages: everything else.

**Shared Responsibility Model** — security responsibilities are shared between the customer and the cloud provider. The split depends on the service model.

> **Exam Tip:** In IaaS, you're responsible for OS hardening, patching, and application security. In SaaS, the provider handles almost everything — you're responsible for data protection and access management.

### Cloud security controls

**CASB (Cloud Access Security Broker)** — security policy enforcement point between cloud service users and providers. Provides: visibility into shadow IT, data loss prevention, access control, threat protection.

**SASE (Secure Access Service Edge)** — converges networking (SD-WAN) and security (CASB, ZTNA, SWG, FWaaS) into a single cloud-delivered service.

**CNAPP (Cloud Native Application Protection Platform)** — unified security for cloud workloads: CWPP + CSPM combined.

**CSPM (Cloud Security Posture Management)** — continuously monitors cloud configurations for misconfigurations (e.g., S3 bucket publicly accessible, security group too open).

**CWPP (Cloud Workload Protection Platform)** — secures cloud workloads (VMs, containers, serverless).

### Container security

**Container** — isolated, portable unit containing an application and its dependencies. Docker is the most common runtime.

**Container image** — read-only template used to create containers. Should be scanned for vulnerabilities before deployment.

**Orchestration** — Kubernetes manages many containers. Kubernetes security: RBAC, network policies, pod security standards, secrets management.

**Immutable infrastructure** — containers are not patched; they're replaced with updated images.

Security concerns: vulnerable base images, secrets in environment variables, excessive container privileges (running as root), shared kernel vulnerabilities.

## 3.3 Identity Architecture

### Federation and SSO

**Identity federation** — trusting identity assertions from another organization's identity provider. Example: corporate employees using their AD credentials to access a partner's cloud app.

**SSO (Single Sign-On)** — log in once, access many systems without re-authenticating.

**Identity Provider (IdP)** — service that authenticates users and provides identity assertions (Okta, Azure AD, Google Workspace).

**Service Provider (SP)** — the application the user is trying to access.

**SAML flow:** User → SP → redirected to IdP → authenticates → IdP sends SAML assertion back to SP → access granted.

**OAuth 2.0 / OIDC** — for API authorization and web app authentication. OIDC = OAuth 2.0 + authentication.

### Directory services

**LDAP** — protocol for accessing directories. Active Directory uses LDAP.

**Active Directory (AD)** — Microsoft's directory service. Manages user accounts, computers, groups, and GPOs (Group Policy Objects).

**AD DS (Domain Services)** — core AD component. Stores objects (users, computers, groups).

**AD FS (Federation Services)** — enables SAML-based SSO between AD and external apps.

**Azure AD / Entra ID** — cloud-based directory for Microsoft 365 and Azure.

### PAM and privileged access

**Privileged Access Workstation (PAW)** — dedicated workstation used ONLY for privileged administrative tasks. Isolated from email, web browsing, etc.

**Credential vaulting** — storing privileged account credentials in an encrypted vault. Retrieved only when needed, with logging.

**Account types to monitor:**
- Shared accounts (no accountability)
- Service accounts (often over-privileged, rarely rotated)
- Default accounts (admin, guest — should be disabled or renamed)
- Dormant accounts (inactive users — disable promptly)

## 3.4 Resilience and Recovery

### High availability and redundancy

**RAID (Redundant Array of Independent Disks):**
- **RAID 0** — striping. Performance only. NO redundancy (lose one disk = lose all data).
- **RAID 1** — mirroring. Redundancy. Each disk has an identical copy.
- **RAID 5** — striping with parity. Can survive one disk failure. Minimum 3 disks.
- **RAID 6** — striping with double parity. Can survive two disk failures.
- **RAID 10 (1+0)** — mirrored stripes. High performance + high redundancy. Minimum 4 disks.

> **Common Trap:** RAID is for availability, not a backup solution. RAID protects against disk failure but not against ransomware, accidental deletion, or fire.

**Failover clustering** — multiple servers act as one. If one fails, others continue.

**Geographic redundancy** — data center in multiple locations for disaster resilience.

### Backup strategies

**Backup types:**
- **Full backup** — backs up everything. Slow. Full recovery possible from one tape.
- **Incremental backup** — backs up only changes since the LAST backup (any type). Fast to back up, slow to restore (need full + all incrementals).
- **Differential backup** — backs up changes since the LAST FULL backup. Faster to restore than incremental (only need full + latest differential).

**3-2-1 rule:**
- 3 copies of data
- 2 different media types
- 1 offsite copy

**Restore testing** — backups are useless if they can't be restored. Test regularly.

**Immutable backups** — backup files that cannot be modified or deleted for a defined period. Critical for ransomware defense.

### Business Continuity / Disaster Recovery

**BCP (Business Continuity Plan)** — how the organization continues operating during a disruption.

**DRP (Disaster Recovery Plan)** — specific procedures for recovering IT systems after a disaster. Subset of BCP.

**RTO (Recovery Time Objective)** — how long until systems are back online.

**RPO (Recovery Point Objective)** — maximum acceptable data loss (how far back can we restore from?).

**MTD (Maximum Tolerable Downtime)** — how long can the business survive without the system?

**Recovery sites:**
- **Hot site** — fully equipped, real-time replication. Can take over immediately. Most expensive.
- **Warm site** — partially equipped. Takes hours to days to become operational.
- **Cold site** — physical space only. Need to bring hardware and restore data. Cheapest.
- **Mobile site** — self-contained unit (trailer, vehicle). Can be moved to disaster area.

### Domain 3 Mini Quiz (8 questions)

**Q1.** A company places its web server between two firewalls — one facing the internet and one facing the internal network. What is this zone called?

- A. VLAN
- B. DMZ
- C. Air gap
- D. Microsegmented zone

**Q2.** Which firewall type performs deep packet inspection and can identify and block specific applications?

- A. Packet filtering
- B. Stateful inspection
- C. Next-Generation Firewall (NGFW)
- D. Circuit-level gateway

**Q3.** An IDS generates too many false positives. Which detection method is most likely causing this?

- A. Signature-based detection
- B. Anomaly-based (behavior-based) detection
- C. Heuristic detection (limited false positives)
- D. Protocol analysis

**Q4.** Which RAID level stripes data with single parity and can survive ONE disk failure?

- A. RAID 0
- B. RAID 1
- C. RAID 5
- D. RAID 10

**Q5.** A company uses AWS and wants a service that monitors for cloud configuration mistakes (e.g., public S3 buckets). Which tool?

- A. CWPP
- B. CASB
- C. CSPM
- D. SIEM

**Q6.** For fastest recovery time with minimal data loss after a disaster, which site type should be used?

- A. Cold site
- B. Warm site
- C. Hot site
- D. Mobile site

**Q7.** An organization backs up full on Sunday and incrementals Monday through Saturday. On Saturday, the system fails. How many backup sets are needed to restore?

- A. 1 (just Saturday's incremental)
- B. 2 (Sunday full + Saturday incremental)
- C. 7 (Sunday full + all 6 daily incrementals)
- D. Just the most recent full backup

**Q8.** Which IPSec component provides both confidentiality (encryption) AND integrity/authentication?

- A. AH (Authentication Header)
- B. IKE
- C. ESP (Encapsulating Security Payload)
- D. SSL

### Domain 3 Answers

**Q1 -> B.** DMZ = perimeter zone between internet-facing and internal firewalls. Public servers live here.

**Q2 -> C.** NGFW = deep packet inspection + application awareness + user identity. WAF is only for HTTP.

**Q3 -> B.** Anomaly-based = learns baseline; deviations alert. High false positive rate because "unusual" isn't always malicious.

**Q4 -> C.** RAID 5 = striping + single parity. Survives 1 disk failure.

**Q5 -> C.** CSPM = Cloud Security Posture Management = scans for cloud misconfigurations.

**Q6 -> C.** Hot site = immediately operational. Cold site = slowest. Warm site = middle.

**Q7 -> C.** Incremental restoration: Full (Sunday) + Mon + Tue + Wed + Thu + Fri + Sat = 7 sets. This is why differentials are easier to restore (only full + 1 differential).

**Q8 -> C.** ESP provides confidentiality (encryption) + integrity + authentication. AH provides authentication/integrity but NO encryption.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Domain 4: Security Operations (28%)

This is the HEAVIEST domain. Focus especially on: vulnerability management, monitoring, incident response lifecycle, and digital forensics.

## 4.1 Vulnerability Management

### Vulnerability management lifecycle

1. **Identify** — scan for vulnerabilities using automated tools (Nessus, Qualys, OpenVAS)
2. **Analyze** — prioritize by CVSS score, asset criticality, and exploitability
3. **Remediate** — patch, mitigate, or accept based on risk
4. **Verify** — confirm that remediation worked (re-scan)
5. **Report** — document findings and remediation for stakeholders

### Patch management

**Patch** — software update that fixes a vulnerability or bug.

**Patch management process:**
1. Inventory — know what you have
2. Test patches in a dev/staging environment before production
3. Deploy patches (use WSUS for Windows, Satellite for RHEL, Configuration Manager, Ansible, etc.)
4. Verify installation and monitor for issues
5. Report compliance

**Zero-day** — vulnerability with no available patch. Threat actors may exploit it before the vendor knows it exists. Mitigations: WAF, IPS, compensating controls.

**EOL/EOS (End of Life/End of Support)** — vendor no longer provides patches. Running EOL software is a serious risk. Mitigate by: network isolation, compensating controls, expedited migration.

**Vulnerability scanners:**
- **Nessus** — most widely used commercial vulnerability scanner
- **OpenVAS** — open-source alternative
- **Qualys** — cloud-based scanner
- **Nikto** — web server scanner

### Configuration management and hardening

**Hardening** — reducing the attack surface by removing unnecessary software, closing unused ports, disabling default accounts, enforcing strong passwords.

**Secure baseline** — documented minimum security configuration for a system type. All systems must meet this baseline.

**CIS Benchmarks** — free, consensus-based configuration guidelines from the Center for Internet Security.

**STIG (Security Technical Implementation Guide)** — DoD-specific hardening guides.

**Change management** — formal process for making changes to systems to prevent unauthorized changes and document what changed (useful for forensics after an incident).

## 4.2 Security Monitoring and SIEM

### Log types and sources

**Log sources:**
- **Firewall logs** — allowed/denied connections, source/destination IPs, ports
- **IDS/IPS logs** — alerts on suspicious traffic
- **Authentication logs** — login success/failure, account lockouts
- **System logs (Syslog)** — OS events, service starts/stops, errors
- **Application logs** — application-specific events
- **Web server logs** — HTTP requests, status codes, user agents
- **DNS logs** — domain lookups (can reveal C2 communication)
- **NetFlow / IPFIX** — network flow data (who talked to whom, how much data)

> **Key log review focus:** Multiple failed logins → brute force. Login at 3am from unusual IP → compromised account. DNS queries to random-looking domains → possible DNS tunneling or C2.

### SIEM (Security Information and Event Management)

Centralized platform that collects, aggregates, correlates, and analyzes log data from many sources.

Features:
- **Log aggregation** — collects logs from all sources
- **Correlation** — links related events across systems (failed login on firewall + successful login on server = lateral movement?)
- **Alerting** — fires alerts when correlation rules match
- **Dashboard** — security status visualization
- **Retention** — long-term storage for forensics and compliance

Common SIEMs: Splunk, IBM QRadar, Microsoft Sentinel, ArcSight, SumoLogic.

**SOAR (Security Orchestration, Automation, and Response)** — automates repetitive security tasks (blocking an IP, quarantining a host, creating tickets). Integrates with SIEM and other security tools.

**XDR (Extended Detection and Response)** — unified detection/response across endpoint, network, cloud, and email. Goes beyond SIEM to correlate across security tools and automated response.

**EDR (Endpoint Detection and Response)** — agent-based security for endpoints. Continuous monitoring, behavioral detection, containment, forensics. Examples: CrowdStrike Falcon, Microsoft Defender for Endpoint.

**MDR (Managed Detection and Response)** — outsourced security operations (24/7 SOC) that provides EDR/XDR monitoring and incident response.

### Threat hunting

Proactive search for threats that have evaded automated detection. Analyst-driven. Uses hypothesis-based approach ("assume breach, look for signs of attacker presence").

Different from incident response (reactive) — threat hunting is proactive.

## 4.3 Incident Response

### Incident Response Lifecycle (NIST SP 800-61)

The standard IR lifecycle has 4 phases:

**1. Preparation** — build the IR capability before incidents happen. Includes: IR plan, team (CSIRT/CIRT), tools, training, communication plan.

**2. Detection and Analysis** — identify that an incident occurred and understand its scope. Sources: SIEM alerts, IDS alerts, user reports, threat intelligence.

**3. Containment, Eradication, and Recovery:**
- **Containment** — stop the spread (isolate systems, block IPs, disable accounts)
- **Eradication** — remove the root cause (malware, attacker's backdoors)
- **Recovery** — restore systems to normal operation, verify clean state

**4. Post-Incident Activity (Lessons Learned)** — after the incident: root cause analysis, update the IR plan, improve controls to prevent recurrence.

> **Exam Tip:** The NIST phases are always tested. Memorize: Preparation → Detection & Analysis → Containment/Eradication/Recovery → Post-Incident. The exam may phrase them slightly differently.

### Incident response team roles

- **CSIRT (Computer Security Incident Response Team)** — the team responsible for handling incidents
- **Incident Commander** — overall lead during the incident
- **Forensic analyst** — collects and analyzes evidence
- **Communications lead** — handles internal and external communications
- **Legal/compliance** — advises on legal obligations (breach notification)

### Incident types and response

**Malware infection:** Isolate the host, identify malware type, clean or re-image, restore from backup, identify entry point.

**Data breach:** Contain exfiltration (block outbound to attacker IPs), identify what was taken, notify affected parties (GDPR: 72 hours to notify regulator), preserve evidence.

**Ransomware:** Isolate infected systems, do NOT pay (no guarantee of recovery, funds criminals), restore from clean backups, identify entry point (email, RDP, vulnerability).

**Insider threat:** Preserve evidence, work with HR/Legal, revoke access immediately, investigate.

## 4.4 Digital Forensics

### Order of volatility (what to collect first)

Collect evidence from MOST volatile to LEAST volatile:
1. CPU registers, cache
2. RAM (memory contents — processes, network connections, decryption keys)
3. Network connections (current sessions)
4. Running processes
5. Disk (hard drive / SSD)
6. Remote logging (SIEM)
7. Physical media (archival storage)

> **Exam Tip:** Always collect RAM first — it's lost when the system is powered off. Disk evidence persists.

### Chain of custody

Documentation tracking who collected evidence, how it was handled, and where it's been. Critical for evidence admissibility in court.

Every transfer of evidence must be documented. Break in chain = evidence may be inadmissible.

### Evidence handling

**Legal hold** — notification to preserve relevant data when litigation is anticipated.

**Forensic copy** — bit-for-bit copy of a storage device. Also called a forensic image. Use write blockers to prevent modifying the original. Verify integrity with hash comparison (MD5 or SHA-256 hash of original vs copy must match).

**Steganography** — hiding data within other data (e.g., hiding text within an image's pixel values). Forensic tool: steganalysis.

**File metadata** — creation date, modification date, access date, owner. Useful for establishing timeline.

**Log analysis** — correlating timestamps across logs to reconstruct attack timeline.

### Forensic tools

- **FTK (Forensic Toolkit)** — commercial forensic analysis platform
- **Autopsy** — open-source digital forensics platform
- **Volatility** — memory forensics framework (analyzes RAM dumps)
- **Wireshark** — packet capture and analysis
- **dd** — Linux command for creating raw disk images

## 4.5 Endpoint and Application Security

### Endpoint protection platforms

**EDR vs AV:**
- Traditional AV = signature-based, reactive, limited detection
- EDR = continuous monitoring, behavioral detection, containment, forensics, threat hunting capability

**Host-based controls:**
- Host firewall (Windows Firewall, iptables)
- HIDS/HIPS
- Application whitelisting — only approved applications can run
- Application blacklisting — specific applications are blocked
- Full disk encryption (BitLocker for Windows, FileVault for Mac)
- Secure boot — ensures only trusted OS loaders run at startup

**Mobile Device Management (MDM)** — centrally manage mobile devices. Can: enforce policies, remotely wipe, manage apps, encrypt storage, enforce PINs.

**UEM (Unified Endpoint Management)** — MDM extended to manage all endpoint types (desktops, laptops, mobile, IoT).

### Secure coding and application security

**SDLC (Software Development Lifecycle)** — the phases of software development. Security should be integrated at EVERY phase (DevSecOps/Shift Left).

**SAST (Static Application Security Testing)** — analyzes source code without running it. Finds vulnerabilities early in development.

**DAST (Dynamic Application Security Testing)** — tests the running application. Black-box testing.

**IAST (Interactive Application Security Testing)** — combines SAST and DAST, instruments the application.

**Fuzzing (fuzz testing)** — sending random or malformed input to find crashes and vulnerabilities.

**Code review** — manual or automated review of source code for security issues.

**OWASP Top 10** — list of the most critical web application security risks. Current categories include: Injection, Broken Authentication, XSS, Insecure Deserialization, SSRF, etc.

## 4.6 Domain 4 Mini Quiz (10 questions)

**Q1.** According to the NIST incident response framework, what is the CORRECT order of phases?

- A. Detection → Containment → Preparation → Recovery
- B. Preparation → Detection & Analysis → Containment/Eradication/Recovery → Post-Incident
- C. Containment → Detection → Eradication → Preparation
- D. Identification → Containment → Eradication → Recovery → Lessons Learned (different framework)

**Q2.** During a forensic investigation of a compromised server, what should the analyst collect FIRST?

- A. Hard drive image
- B. Remote logs from SIEM
- C. RAM (memory dump)
- D. Network configuration

**Q3.** A SIEM alerts that a user failed 50 logins in 2 minutes across multiple systems. What does this most likely indicate?

- A. User forgot their password
- B. Credential stuffing or brute force attack
- C. Normal activity
- D. Ransomware

**Q4.** Which tool specializes in analyzing RAM dumps to find running processes, network connections, and decryption keys?

- A. Wireshark
- B. Autopsy
- C. Volatility
- D. Nessus

**Q5.** A company's antivirus failed to detect new malware. Which endpoint security technology would MOST likely have detected it through behavioral analysis?

- A. Traditional AV with daily signature updates
- B. EDR (Endpoint Detection and Response)
- C. HIDS
- D. Network firewall

**Q6.** During incident response, a server is found to be infected with ransomware. What is the FIRST action the team should take?

- A. Pay the ransom immediately
- B. Eradicate the malware
- C. Contain — isolate the infected system from the network
- D. Restore from backup

**Q7.** A web application has a vulnerability where users can view other users' data by changing the user ID in the URL. Which type of vulnerability is this?

- A. SQL Injection
- B. XSS
- C. Insecure Direct Object Reference (IDOR)
- D. CSRF

**Q8.** Which testing methodology analyzes source code to find vulnerabilities without executing the program?

- A. DAST
- B. SAST
- C. Penetration testing
- D. Fuzzing

**Q9.** An organization wants to proactively search for threats that automated tools may have missed. What is this activity called?

- A. Vulnerability scanning
- B. Penetration testing
- C. Threat hunting
- D. Security auditing

**Q10.** A forensic investigator creates a bit-for-bit copy of a hard drive. Which step is ESSENTIAL to ensure the copy's integrity?

- A. Encrypt the copy
- B. Compare the hash (MD5/SHA-256) of the original and the copy to verify they match
- C. Store the copy on the same server
- D. Use a write blocker only after copying

### Domain 4 Answers

**Q1 -> B.** NIST IR lifecycle: Preparation → Detection & Analysis → Containment/Eradication/Recovery → Post-Incident. Option D describes SANS PICERL (different but similar).

**Q2 -> C.** RAM is most volatile — lost when system powers off. Collect first per order of volatility.

**Q3 -> B.** 50 failed logins in 2 minutes across multiple systems = brute force or credential stuffing attack.

**Q4 -> C.** Volatility = memory forensics. Wireshark = network packets. Autopsy = disk forensics. Nessus = vulnerability scanning.

**Q5 -> B.** EDR uses behavioral analysis and can detect novel malware that hasn't been seen before (no signature needed).

**Q6 -> C.** Containment FIRST — stop the spread. Then eradicate (clean), then recover (restore).

**Q7 -> C.** IDOR = accessing other users' data by manipulating object references (IDs) in URLs/parameters.

**Q8 -> B.** SAST = static code analysis (no execution). DAST = dynamic (running app).

**Q9 -> C.** Threat hunting = proactive search for threats that evaded detection.

**Q10 -> B.** Hash verification (MD5/SHA-256 match of original and copy) = confirms forensic integrity.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Domain 5: Security Program Management and Oversight (20%)

## 5.1 Risk Management

### Risk fundamentals

**Risk** = Threat × Vulnerability × Impact

More precisely: Risk = Likelihood (of threat exploiting vulnerability) × Impact (damage if it does).

**Threat** — potential cause of harm (external attacker, natural disaster, insider).
**Vulnerability** — weakness that can be exploited.
**Asset** — what you're protecting (data, systems, reputation).
**Exposure** — degree to which an asset is subject to harm.
**Risk** — potential for loss or harm.

### Risk analysis types

**Qualitative risk analysis** — ranks risks using subjective categories (High/Medium/Low). Uses a risk matrix. Faster, no complex math. Used when you can't accurately quantify losses.

**Quantitative risk analysis** — uses numbers and formulas.

Key formulas:
- **AV (Asset Value)** — how much is the asset worth?
- **EF (Exposure Factor)** — percentage of asset lost if risk occurs. (If fire destroys 40% of the building → EF = 0.40)
- **SLE (Single Loss Expectancy)** = AV × EF — how much is lost in one incident?
- **ARO (Annual Rate of Occurrence)** — how many times per year is this expected to happen?
- **ALE (Annual Loss Expectancy)** = SLE × ARO — expected annual loss from this risk.

Example: Server worth $100,000. Fire would damage 50% (EF = 0.5). Fire expected once every 10 years (ARO = 0.1).
- SLE = $100,000 × 0.5 = $50,000
- ALE = $50,000 × 0.1 = $5,000/year

If a fire suppression system costs $3,000/year → worth it. If it costs $8,000/year → not worth it purely on numbers.

### Risk responses

**Risk avoidance** — don't do the activity that creates the risk. (Don't collect SSNs if you don't need to = avoid data breach risk from them)

**Risk mitigation/reduction** — implement controls to reduce likelihood or impact. Most common response.

**Risk transference/transfer** — shift the financial impact to another party. Cyber insurance, outsourcing.

**Risk acceptance** — acknowledge the risk and accept it without controls. Appropriate when: cost of mitigation > cost of risk, or risk is very low. Must be formally documented.

> **Exam Tip:** Risk acceptance is NOT the same as ignoring risk. It requires a documented decision by management.

**Residual risk** — risk that remains AFTER controls are applied. No control eliminates 100% of risk.

**Control risk** — risk introduced by the controls themselves (a firewall that crashes disrupts availability).

## 5.2 Compliance and Governance

### Security frameworks

**NIST Cybersecurity Framework (CSF)** — voluntary framework for managing cybersecurity risk. Five functions:
1. **Identify** — understand your assets, risks, governance
2. **Protect** — implement safeguards
3. **Detect** — implement activities to identify incidents
4. **Respond** — take action on detected incidents
5. **Recover** — restore capabilities after incident

**NIST SP 800-53** — security controls catalog for federal systems. More prescriptive than CSF.

**NIST SP 800-171** — security requirements for protecting CUI (Controlled Unclassified Information) in non-federal systems.

**ISO/IEC 27001** — international standard for Information Security Management System (ISMS). Certification-based.

**ISO/IEC 27002** — code of practice for information security controls (complements 27001).

**SOC 2 (Service Organization Control 2)** — audit report for service providers. Based on Trust Services Criteria: Security, Availability, Processing Integrity, Confidentiality, Privacy.

**PCI DSS (Payment Card Industry Data Security Standard)** — required for any organization handling cardholder data. 12 requirements.

**HIPAA (Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act)** — US law protecting PHI (Protected Health Information). Security Rule covers electronic PHI.

**GDPR (General Data Protection Regulation)** — EU regulation for personal data protection. Key requirements: lawful basis for processing, data subject rights, breach notification within 72 hours, Data Protection Officer (DPO).

**CMMC (Cybersecurity Maturity Model Certification)** — DoD requirement for defense contractors. Multiple levels of maturity.

> **Exam Tip:** Know which regulation/framework applies to which industry:
> - Healthcare → HIPAA
> - Credit cards → PCI DSS
> - EU personal data → GDPR
> - Federal systems → NIST SP 800-53
> - Any organization (best practice) → NIST CSF, ISO 27001

### Security policies

**AUP (Acceptable Use Policy)** — defines acceptable use of company IT resources. First policy employees typically sign.

**Information Security Policy** — overall security governance document.

**Data classification policy** — how data is categorized (Public, Internal, Confidential, Top Secret) and handled at each level.

**Password policy** — minimum length, complexity, rotation, history requirements.

**BYOD (Bring Your Own Device) policy** — rules for personal devices accessing company resources.

**Remote work / Telework policy** — requirements for working from home or remote locations.

**Change management policy** — process for making changes to IT systems.

**Incident response policy** — how incidents are handled, escalation procedures.

### Data security and privacy

**Data classification:**
- **Public** — can be shared freely
- **Internal/Private** — for employees only, not sensitive
- **Confidential** — sensitive, access restricted
- **Restricted/Top Secret** — highest sensitivity, very limited access

**Data lifecycle management:**
- Collect → Store → Use → Share → Archive → Destroy

**Data destruction methods:**
- **Degaussing** — strong magnetic field destroys magnetic media
- **Shredding** — physical destruction (paper, drives)
- **Wiping (overwriting)** — overwriting data multiple times (DoD 5220.22-M standard)
- **Crypto-shredding** — destroy the encryption keys so encrypted data becomes irrecoverable
- **Incineration** — physical destruction by fire

> **Exam Tip:** For SSDs, degaussing doesn't work well (no magnetic media). Use shredding, crypto-shredding, or certified destruction.

**PII (Personally Identifiable Information)** — any data that can identify an individual. SSN, email, phone, IP address (in many jurisdictions).

**PHI (Protected Health Information)** — health information tied to an individual. Protected by HIPAA.

**Data Loss Prevention (DLP)** — tools that detect and prevent unauthorized transfer of sensitive data. Can monitor email, USB, web, cloud uploads.

## 5.3 Security Awareness and Training

**Security awareness training** — educating employees on security threats and their roles. Most effective control against social engineering.

**Phishing simulations** — send fake phishing emails to test employee response. Those who click receive additional training.

**Tabletop exercises** — discussion-based exercises where the team walks through a scenario (e.g., "What would we do if ransomware hit us?") to identify gaps in the IR plan.

**Red team / Blue team exercises:**
- **Red team** — attackers (offensive). Try to break in.
- **Blue team** — defenders. Detect and respond to red team.
- **Purple team** — both working together to improve detection and response.

## 5.4 Third-Party Risk Management

**Vendor risk assessment** — evaluating security posture of vendors/partners who handle your data or connect to your systems.

**Due diligence** — thorough evaluation of vendor security before signing contracts.

**SLA (Service Level Agreement)** — contractual commitment to service levels (uptime, response time). Includes security requirements.

**MOU (Memorandum of Understanding)** — informal agreement between organizations (less binding than contract).

**NDA (Non-Disclosure Agreement)** — legally binding agreement to keep information confidential.

**Data processing agreements** — required under GDPR for vendors who process personal data on your behalf.

**Supply chain risk** — risk introduced by hardware or software components from third-party vendors. Example: SolarWinds attack (trusted software supply chain compromised).

**Right to audit** — contract clause allowing you to audit vendor security controls.

### Domain 5 Mini Quiz (8 questions)

**Q1.** A company calculates that a server worth $200,000 could be 30% damaged by a flood, and floods occur every 5 years. What is the ALE?

- A. $60,000
- B. $12,000
- C. $200,000
- D. $40,000

**Q2.** Which risk response involves purchasing cyber insurance?

- A. Risk avoidance
- B. Risk mitigation
- C. Risk transference
- D. Risk acceptance

**Q3.** Which regulation requires breach notification within 72 hours and applies to personal data of EU citizens?

- A. HIPAA
- B. PCI DSS
- C. GDPR
- D. NIST CSF

**Q4.** What type of security exercise involves the team discussing how they would respond to a ransomware attack without actually performing any technical actions?

- A. Red team exercise
- B. Penetration test
- C. Tabletop exercise
- D. War game

**Q5.** A company discovers an old SSD containing customer data that is being disposed of. Which data destruction method is MOST appropriate for SSDs?

- A. Degaussing
- B. Physical shredding or crypto-shredding
- C. Overwriting with zeros once
- D. Reformatting the SSD

**Q6.** Which NIST CSF function involves implementing safeguards to limit the impact of a potential cybersecurity event?

- A. Identify
- B. Protect
- C. Detect
- D. Respond

**Q7.** Which framework is specifically required for organizations that handle credit card payment data?

- A. HIPAA
- B. GDPR
- C. NIST SP 800-53
- D. PCI DSS

**Q8.** Risk that remains after security controls have been applied is called:

- A. Inherent risk
- B. Control risk
- C. Residual risk
- D. Transfer risk

### Domain 5 Answers

**Q1 -> B.** SLE = $200,000 × 0.30 = $60,000. ARO = 1/5 = 0.2. ALE = $60,000 × 0.2 = $12,000.

**Q2 -> C.** Insurance = transferring the financial risk to another party.

**Q3 -> C.** GDPR = EU personal data regulation, 72-hour breach notification.

**Q4 -> C.** Tabletop = discussion-based, no technical actions.

**Q5 -> B.** Degaussing doesn't work on SSDs (no magnetic media). Shredding or crypto-shredding are correct.

**Q6 -> B.** Protect = implement safeguards. Identify = understand assets. Detect = monitoring. Respond = IR.

**Q7 -> D.** PCI DSS = payment card data. HIPAA = healthcare. GDPR = EU personal data.

**Q8 -> C.** Residual risk = what's left after controls. Inherent risk = risk without any controls.

# Security+ Comparison Tables

## Authentication Protocols Compared

| Protocol | Use case | Port | Notes |
|---|---|---|---|
| Kerberos | Windows domain SSO | 88 | Ticket-based; KDC; vulnerable to Golden Ticket |
| LDAP | Directory access | 389 (636 for LDAPS) | Protocol only; used by AD |
| NTLM | Legacy Windows auth | N/A | Pass-the-hash vulnerability; replaced by Kerberos |
| RADIUS | Network access (VPN, WiFi) | 1812/1813 UDP | Encrypts only password |
| TACACS+ | Device administration (Cisco) | 49 TCP | Encrypts entire payload |
| OAuth 2.0 | API authorization | 443 | Not authentication itself |
| OIDC | Web app authentication | 443 | Auth layer on OAuth 2.0 |
| SAML | Enterprise browser SSO | 443 | XML-based; enterprise AD → cloud apps |

## Malware Types

| Type | Self-replicates | Spreads via | User action | Key trait |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Virus | Yes (attaches to files) | Files, email | Required | Attaches to other files |
| Worm | Yes | Network | Not required | Self-propagating |
| Trojan | No | Disguise as legitimate | Required | Appears legitimate |
| Rootkit | No | Other malware | — | Hides in OS/kernel |
| Ransomware | No | Email, exploit, RDP | Often required | Encrypts files for ransom |
| Fileless | No | Exploits | — | Lives in memory, no files |
| Spyware | No | Downloads, bundles | Often required | Monitors user activity |
| Botnet node | No | Other malware | — | Part of C2 network |

## Access Control Models

| Model | Who sets permissions | Basis | Example |
|---|---|---|---|
| DAC | Resource owner | Ownership | Linux file permissions |
| MAC | System (enforced) | Labels/classification | Military clearance levels, SELinux |
| RBAC | Administrator | Job role | Enterprise IT (HR role, Finance role) |
| ABAC | Administrator | Attributes (time, location, dept) | Most granular; cloud IAM policies |
| Rule-based | Administrator | Rules/ACLs | Firewall rules |

## Cryptography Summary

| Type | Keys | Speed | Use case | Examples |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Symmetric | 1 shared key | Fast | Bulk data encryption | AES, 3DES, ChaCha20 |
| Asymmetric | Public + Private pair | Slow | Key exchange, signatures | RSA, ECC, DH |
| Hashing | None (one-way) | Very fast | Integrity, password storage | SHA-256, bcrypt, MD5 (broken) |
| HMAC | Symmetric key + hash | Fast | Integrity + authentication | HMAC-SHA256 |

## Hashing Algorithms

| Algorithm | Output size | Status | Use for |
|---|---|---|---|
| MD5 | 128-bit | BROKEN (collisions) | Legacy only, not security |
| SHA-1 | 160-bit | Deprecated | Legacy only |
| SHA-256 | 256-bit | Current standard | File integrity, certificates, TLS |
| SHA-3 | Variable | Current | Alternative to SHA-2 |
| bcrypt | 60-char encoded | Current | Password hashing |
| Argon2 | Variable | Current | Password hashing (newer, better) |

## Incident Response Phases

| Phase | Key activities |
|---|---|
| Preparation | IR plan, team, tools, training, communication plan |
| Detection & Analysis | Identify incident, scope it, classify severity |
| Containment | Isolate affected systems, block IPs, disable accounts |
| Eradication | Remove malware, close backdoors, patch vulnerability |
| Recovery | Restore systems, verify clean, monitor closely |
| Post-Incident | Root cause analysis, update plan, report |

## Risk Formulas

| Formula | Calculation |
|---|---|
| SLE (Single Loss Expectancy) | Asset Value × Exposure Factor |
| ALE (Annual Loss Expectancy) | SLE × Annual Rate of Occurrence (ARO) |
| Residual Risk | Inherent Risk after controls applied |

## Network Security Controls

| Control | Layer | Active/Passive | Blocks? |
|---|---|---|---|
| Packet filter firewall | 3/4 | Active | Yes (by rule) |
| Stateful firewall | 3/4 | Active | Yes (tracks state) |
| NGFW | 3–7 | Active | Yes (deep inspection) |
| IDS | 3–7 | Passive | No (alerts only) |
| IPS | 3–7 | Active | Yes (inline) |
| WAF | 7 | Active | Yes (HTTP attacks) |
| Honeypot | N/A | Passive/Detective | No (attracts attackers) |

## Regulatory Frameworks

| Regulation | Scope | Key requirement | Penalty |
|---|---|---|---|
| HIPAA | US healthcare PHI | Security Rule + Breach notification | Up to $1.9M/year |
| PCI DSS | Payment card data globally | 12 security requirements | Fines + loss of card processing |
| GDPR | EU personal data (global reach) | 72-hr breach notification, data rights | Up to 4% global revenue |
| NIST CSF | Any organization | Identify-Protect-Detect-Respond-Recover | Voluntary (required for federal contractors) |
| SOC 2 | Service providers | Trust Services Criteria audit | N/A (audit report only) |
