> **Disclaimer**
>
> Independent study guide. Not affiliated with or endorsed by Amazon Web Services.
> "AWS," "Amazon Web Services," and related marks are trademarks of Amazon.com, Inc.
> This book contains **original practice questions only** — no real exam questions, no brain dumps.
> Always verify current exam details at the official AWS certification page before your test date.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Preface

## Who this book is for

You've got some AWS knowledge — maybe you passed CLF-C02, maybe you've been clicking around the console for a few months — and now you want the Solutions Architect Associate. This is the right next cert. It's the most in-demand AWS credential on the market and the gateway to every senior cloud role.

This book assumes you already know what S3, EC2, IAM, and VPC roughly do. We're going deeper now: **how to choose between services, when to combine them, and why one architecture beats another**. That's what SAA-C03 actually tests.

## How SAA-C03 is different from CLF-C02

CLF-C02 tests knowledge: "what is this service?" SAA-C03 tests judgment: "given this constraint, which architecture is BEST?" Almost every question gives you a scenario with a company, a problem, and four plausible answers. Three of them work technically — but only one is MOST cost-effective, MOST resilient, LEAST operational overhead, or MOST scalable. The exam rewards architecture reasoning, not memorization.

The shift you need to make: stop thinking "what does this service do?" and start thinking "which service fits this requirement?"

## Exam facts at a glance

| Item | Detail |
|---|---|
| Exam code | SAA-C03 |
| Total questions | 65 (50 scored + 15 unscored) |
| Time | 130 minutes |
| Passing score | 720 / 1000 (scaled) |
| Question types | Multiple-choice (1 of 4), multiple-response (2 or more of 5) |
| Cost | ~$300 USD |
| Validity | 3 years |
| Scoring model | Compensatory (overall score only, not per domain) |
| Guessing penalty | None — always answer every question |

## Domain weights

| Domain | Weight |
|---|---|
| Domain 1: Design Secure Architectures | 30% |
| Domain 2: Design Resilient Architectures | 26% |
| Domain 3: Design High-Performing Architectures | 24% |
| Domain 4: Design Cost-Optimized Architectures | 20% |

## Study plan

**4-week plan (if you have CLF-C02 + some AWS experience)**
- Week 1: Domains 1 & 2 (read + mini quizzes)
- Week 2: Domains 3 & 4 (read + mini quizzes)
- Week 3: All comparison tables, keyword decoder, cheat sheets + Mock 1
- Week 4: Mock 2 + Mock 3 + review all wrong answers + final cheat sheet

**6-week plan (if AWS is newer to you)**
- Weeks 1–2: Domains 1 & 2
- Weeks 3–4: Domains 3 & 4
- Week 5: Comparison tables + Mock 1 + Mock 2
- Week 6: Mock 3 + missed-topic review + final cheat sheet

**The SAA-C03 mindset to build:**
When reading a question, ask yourself: Is this about security, resilience, performance, or cost? What's the key constraint (cost, latency, AZ failure, on-premises integration)? Which service is designed exactly for that constraint? Once you can answer those three questions automatically, you'll pass.

> **Exam Tip:** SAA-C03 questions are long and scenario-heavy. Read the LAST sentence first — it usually says exactly what's being asked. Then read the scenario. You save 20 seconds per question and avoid being distracted by details that don't affect the answer.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Domain 1: Design Secure Architectures (30%)

This is the heaviest domain. It covers IAM design, VPC security, encryption, and how to build apps that are secure by default.

## 1.1 IAM Deep Dive

IAM is far more nuanced at the associate level. You need to know not just what users/groups/roles/policies are, but how to design access patterns correctly.

### Identity types

**IAM User** — A permanent identity for a person or application. Uses long-term credentials (password + access keys). Best practice: use only when a person needs consistent, named access. Avoid storing access keys on EC2 — use roles instead.

**IAM Group** — A collection of users. Policies attached to the group apply to all members. Groups cannot be nested.

**IAM Role** — A temporary identity assumed by a service, application, or another account. No long-term credentials. This is the **correct pattern** for:
- EC2 accessing S3 or DynamoDB (attach role to instance)
- Lambda accessing other AWS services
- Cross-account access
- Identity federation (users from SAML/OIDC providers assuming a role)

**IAM Policy** — JSON document with Allow/Deny statements on actions and resources. Types:
- **Identity-based policies** — attached to user/group/role
- **Resource-based policies** — attached to a resource (e.g., S3 bucket policy, KMS key policy)
- **Permission boundaries** — cap the maximum permissions an identity can have
- **Service Control Policies (SCPs)** — max permissions across accounts in Organizations
- **Session policies** — limit permissions when assuming a role

### Policy evaluation logic

When a request comes in, AWS evaluates in this order:
1. **Explicit Deny** anywhere? → Deny. Done.
2. **SCP** present and allows? → Continue. If not → Deny.
3. **Resource-based policy** allows? → Allow (unless explicit deny).
4. **Identity-based policy** allows? → Allow. Otherwise → Implicit Deny.

> **Common Trap:** The default is implicit deny. Just because there's no deny doesn't mean there's an allow. Permissions must be explicitly granted.

### IAM best practices (exam favorites)

- Use **IAM roles** for EC2 and Lambda instead of access keys on the instance.
- Enable **MFA** on the root user and privileged IAM users.
- Apply **least privilege** — grant only what's needed.
- Use **IAM Identity Center** (successor to AWS SSO) for multi-account SSO.
- Use **permission boundaries** when delegating IAM user creation to developers.
- Rotate access keys regularly; use **Secrets Manager** to store and auto-rotate them.
- Use **AWS Organizations + SCPs** to apply guardrails across all accounts.

### Cross-account access

Pattern: Account A wants to let Account B's users access a resource.

1. In Account A: create an IAM role with a **trust policy** that allows Account B's principal to assume it.
2. In Account B: attach a policy to the user/role that allows `sts:AssumeRole` on Account A's role ARN.
3. The user in Account B calls `sts:AssumeRole`, gets temporary credentials, and uses them in Account A.

### AWS STS (Security Token Service)

STS issues temporary security credentials. Key API calls:
- **AssumeRole** — assume a role in the same or another account
- **AssumeRoleWithWebIdentity** — for OIDC (Google, Facebook, Amazon Cognito)
- **AssumeRoleWithSAML** — for SAML-based enterprise federation
- **GetSessionToken** — for MFA-authenticated users

### Amazon Cognito

Two components:
- **User Pools** — a user directory. Handles sign-up, sign-in, MFA, password policies. Returns JWT tokens. Use for authenticating users into YOUR application.
- **Identity Pools (Federated Identities)** — exchange a token (from User Pool, Google, Facebook, SAML, etc.) for temporary AWS credentials to call AWS services directly. Use when your mobile/web app needs to call S3, DynamoDB, etc.

> **Exam Tip:** "Mobile app users need to access S3" → Cognito Identity Pool. "Users need to sign in to your web app" → Cognito User Pool.

### AWS Resource Access Manager (RAM)

Share AWS resources across accounts in the same Organization without copying them. Common shared resources: VPC subnets, Route 53 Resolver rules, License Manager configurations, Transit Gateways.

## 1.2 VPC Security

### VPC review: the essentials

A VPC is your private network in AWS. Key components:
- **CIDR block** — your VPC's IP address range (e.g., 10.0.0.0/16)
- **Subnets** — subdivisions of a VPC, tied to one AZ
- **Route table** — rules for where traffic goes
- **Internet Gateway (IGW)** — allows public internet traffic (bidirectional)
- **NAT Gateway** — lets private subnet instances reach the internet outbound only (no inbound). Deployed in a public subnet. Managed, HA within an AZ (for multi-AZ HA, deploy one per AZ).
- **VPC Endpoint** — private connection from VPC to AWS services without going through the internet

### Security groups vs NACLs (deep comparison)

| Feature | Security Group | Network ACL |
|---|---|---|
| Operates at | Instance (ENI) level | Subnet level |
| Stateful/Stateless | Stateful | Stateless |
| Rules | Allow only | Allow and Deny |
| Rule evaluation | All rules evaluated | Rules evaluated in number order (lowest first) |
| Default outbound | All traffic allowed | All traffic allowed |
| Use case | Fine-grained instance access | Broad subnet-level blocking |

> **Common Trap:** NACLs are stateless — you must explicitly allow BOTH inbound AND outbound rules. Security groups are stateful — return traffic is automatically allowed.

### VPC Endpoints

Allow private connectivity from your VPC to AWS services without using the internet, IGW, NAT, or VPN.

**Gateway Endpoints** — for **S3** and **DynamoDB** only. Free. Add a route to your route table. Traffic stays on the AWS network.

**Interface Endpoints (powered by AWS PrivateLink)** — for most other services (SSM, Secrets Manager, ECR, etc.). Creates an ENI in your subnet with a private IP. Costs money per hour + per GB.

> **Exam Tip:** "S3 access from private subnet without internet" → Gateway Endpoint for S3. "EC2 in private subnet needs to call Systems Manager" → Interface Endpoint for SSM.

### VPC Connectivity Options

**VPC Peering** — connect two VPCs directly. Traffic stays on AWS backbone. Works across accounts and regions. Not transitive — if A peers with B and B peers with C, A cannot reach C through B.

**AWS Transit Gateway (TGW)** — a hub that connects many VPCs and on-premises networks. Transitive routing. Single place to manage connectivity at scale.

**AWS VPN** — encrypted tunnel over the public internet. Two types:
- **Site-to-Site VPN** — connect on-premises to AWS VPC. Uses Virtual Private Gateway (VGW) on the AWS side and Customer Gateway (CGW) on the on-premises side. Max ~1.25 Gbps.
- **Client VPN** — OpenVPN-based, for remote users to connect to a VPC.

**AWS Direct Connect (DX)** — private, dedicated physical fiber link from your data center to AWS. Consistent latency, higher bandwidth, NOT encrypted by default (add VPN over DX for encryption).

**Direct Connect + VPN** = private, dedicated, AND encrypted. Best of both worlds for compliance.

> **Common Trap:** Direct Connect does NOT use the public internet and is NOT automatically encrypted. VPN is encrypted but uses the internet. For encrypted + private: run VPN over Direct Connect.

### Bastion Host (Jump Host)

A hardened EC2 instance in a public subnet used to SSH/RDP into instances in private subnets. Better alternative: **AWS Systems Manager Session Manager** — no bastion, no open port 22, fully logged.

### AWS Network Firewall

A managed, stateful firewall for VPCs. Sits at the perimeter. Supports deep packet inspection, domain filtering, IPS rules. Use when you need more than security groups and NACLs (e.g., blocking specific domains, intrusion prevention).

## 1.3 Data Security and Encryption

### Encryption at rest

**AWS KMS (Key Management Service)** — create and manage customer master keys (CMKs), now called KMS keys. Two types:
- **AWS managed keys** — created and managed by AWS on your behalf (e.g., `aws/s3`). Free. Less control.
- **Customer managed keys** — you create, you set key policies, you control rotation. $1/month/key.

KMS integrates with: S3, EBS, RDS, DynamoDB, Secrets Manager, CloudTrail, etc.

**Envelope encryption** — the data is encrypted with a data key (DEK), and the DEK is encrypted with a KMS key. KMS never stores your data directly.

**CloudHSM** — dedicated hardware security module. You manage the HSM cluster, you control the keys entirely. AWS cannot access them. Use when compliance requires customer-managed HSMs.

> **Exam Tip:** KMS = AWS managed or customer managed keys, good for most needs. CloudHSM = dedicated HSM, you own the keys entirely, needed for strict compliance (e.g., FIPS 140-2 Level 3).

### Encryption in transit

Use **TLS/SSL**. AWS Certificate Manager (ACM) provisions and manages SSL/TLS certificates for free for use with ELB, CloudFront, API Gateway.

### AWS Secrets Manager vs Parameter Store

| Feature | Secrets Manager | Parameter Store |
|---|---|---|
| Automatic rotation | Yes (built-in for RDS, Redshift, etc.) | No (manual) |
| Cost | $0.40/secret/month | Free (Standard tier) |
| Use case | DB passwords, API keys, secrets needing rotation | Config values, non-sensitive params |
| Cross-account | Yes | Yes (with resource policy) |
| Integration | Lambda, ECS, RDS | EC2, ECS, Lambda, CodeDeploy |

> **Exam Tip:** "Automatically rotate database passwords" → Secrets Manager. "Store environment variables for Lambda" → Parameter Store. "Database credentials that must rotate every 30 days" → Secrets Manager.

### S3 Security

**Bucket policies** — resource-based policies on the bucket. Apply to the bucket and its objects. Use to grant cross-account access or enforce HTTPS.

**S3 Block Public Access** — account-level or bucket-level setting that blocks public access regardless of bucket policy. Best practice: enable at the account level.

**Server-Side Encryption (SSE)**:
- **SSE-S3** — AWS manages keys using AES-256. Default since Jan 2023.
- **SSE-KMS** — you control the KMS key; audit key usage via CloudTrail.
- **SSE-C** — you provide the encryption key with each request; AWS doesn't store it.
- **Client-Side Encryption** — you encrypt before sending to S3.

**S3 Object Lock** — prevents objects from being deleted or overwritten. Two modes:
- **Governance mode** — users with special IAM permissions can override.
- **Compliance mode** — no one, not even root, can delete until retention expires.
Use for regulatory compliance and WORM (Write Once Read Many) storage.

**S3 MFA Delete** — requires MFA to permanently delete object versions. Must enable versioning first. Enabled via CLI/API only (not console).

**Pre-signed URLs** — generate a time-limited URL that lets anyone download (or upload) a specific object without AWS credentials. Generated by the SDK/CLI.

**S3 Access Points** — named access endpoints with their own policies. Simplify large-scale access control for shared datasets (e.g., different teams get different access points with different permissions).

### ACM (AWS Certificate Manager)

Free public SSL/TLS certificates for use with: ALB, CloudFront, API Gateway, Elastic Beanstalk. You cannot export the private key. For EC2 direct HTTPS, you must bring your own certificate.

## 1.4 Application Security

### AWS WAF (Web Application Firewall)

Layer 7 (HTTP/HTTPS) firewall with configurable rules. Integrates with: CloudFront, ALB, API Gateway, App Runner. Rules can block:
- IP addresses and CIDR ranges
- Geographic locations (geo-blocking)
- SQL injection patterns
- Cross-site scripting (XSS) patterns
- Rate-based rules (throttle DDoS)
- Managed rule groups (AWS or third-party)

### AWS Shield

**Standard** — automatic, free DDoS protection at Layers 3 and 4 for all AWS customers.
**Advanced** — $3,000/month subscription. Protects against larger, sophisticated DDoS attacks. Includes 24/7 DDoS Response Team (DRT), cost protection (credit for bill spikes from DDoS), and integration with WAF.

> **Exam Tip:** For basic DDoS = Shield Standard (free, automatic). For advanced DDoS with financial protection and expert support = Shield Advanced.

### Amazon GuardDuty

Threat detection service that continuously analyzes CloudTrail events, VPC flow logs, DNS logs, and S3 access logs using machine learning. Generates findings (alerts) when it detects suspicious activity. Enable with one click; no agents to install.

### Amazon Inspector

Automated vulnerability scanning for:
- EC2 instances (OS and software vulnerabilities)
- Container images in ECR
- Lambda functions
Inspector continuously scans (not just one-time). Integrates with Security Hub.

### Amazon Macie

Uses ML to discover and classify sensitive data (PII, financial data, credentials) in S3. Generates findings. Use for data privacy compliance.

### AWS Security Hub

Central security dashboard aggregating findings from GuardDuty, Inspector, Macie, Config, Firewall Manager, and third-party tools. Checks against security standards (CIS, PCI DSS).

### Domain 1 — Mini Quiz (10 questions)

**Q1.** An application on EC2 needs to read files from S3. What is the MOST secure way to grant access?

- A. Create an IAM user, store access keys as environment variables on the instance
- B. Attach an IAM role with the required S3 permissions to the EC2 instance
- C. Make the S3 bucket public
- D. Store IAM credentials in the EC2 user data script

**Q2.** A company wants to allow users from its corporate SAML identity provider to access AWS. What should they use?

- A. Create individual IAM users for each employee
- B. Configure SAML federation with an IAM identity provider and IAM roles
- C. Share the root account credentials
- D. Use Cognito User Pools only

**Q3.** Which statement about VPC security groups is TRUE?

- A. They operate at the subnet level and are stateless
- B. They operate at the instance level and are stateful
- C. They can explicitly deny traffic
- D. Rules are evaluated in numerical order

**Q4.** A company needs S3 access from a private subnet without internet traffic. What is the MOST cost-effective solution?

- A. NAT Gateway
- B. VPC Gateway Endpoint for S3
- C. VPC Interface Endpoint for S3
- D. Internet Gateway

**Q5.** Which service provides automatic rotation of database passwords?

- A. AWS KMS
- B. AWS Systems Manager Parameter Store
- C. AWS Secrets Manager
- D. AWS IAM

**Q6.** A company wants to prevent ANY deletion of objects in an S3 bucket for 7 years, even by root. Which feature?

- A. S3 MFA Delete
- B. S3 Object Lock in Governance mode
- C. S3 Object Lock in Compliance mode
- D. S3 Versioning alone

**Q7.** Two VPCs are peered (A-B, B-C). Can VPC A reach VPC C through VPC B?

- A. Yes, automatically
- B. Yes, with a route table entry
- C. No — VPC peering is not transitive
- D. Yes, using a VPC endpoint

**Q8.** Which service should be used to block specific countries from accessing a CloudFront distribution?

- A. AWS Shield
- B. AWS WAF with geo-match rules
- C. Security groups
- D. Network ACLs

**Q9.** An architect needs to provide temporary AWS credentials to mobile app users authenticated via Google. Which service?

- A. IAM users with access keys
- B. Amazon Cognito Identity Pools (Federated Identities)
- C. Amazon Cognito User Pools
- D. AWS IAM Identity Center

**Q10.** Which encryption option gives the MOST control — where AWS cannot access the keys at all?

- A. SSE-S3
- B. SSE-KMS with AWS managed key
- C. SSE-KMS with customer managed key
- D. AWS CloudHSM

### Domain 1 Answers

**Q1 -> B.** IAM role on EC2 = no long-term credentials, best practice. A stores keys insecurely; C is dangerously public; D stores credentials where they're exposed.

**Q2 -> B.** SAML federation maps corporate identities to IAM roles without creating individual IAM users.

**Q3 -> B.** Security groups operate at the instance (ENI) level and are stateful. A/C/D describe NACLs.

**Q4 -> B.** S3 Gateway Endpoint is free and routes S3 traffic within the AWS network. NAT Gateway costs money and routes through the internet. Interface Endpoint for S3 also costs money.

**Q5 -> C.** Secrets Manager has built-in rotation including for RDS, Redshift, etc.

**Q6 -> C.** Compliance mode prevents deletion even by root until the retention period expires.

**Q7 -> C.** VPC peering is NOT transitive. Use Transit Gateway if you need hub-and-spoke or transitive routing.

**Q8 -> B.** WAF has geo-match rule statements to block/allow by country. Works with CloudFront.

**Q9 -> B.** Cognito Identity Pools exchange third-party tokens (Google, Facebook, OIDC) for temporary AWS credentials.

**Q10 -> D.** CloudHSM gives you dedicated hardware where AWS has no access to keys.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Domain 2: Design Resilient Architectures (26%)

Resilience means your system keeps working even when things fail. This domain covers HA, fault tolerance, decoupling, disaster recovery, and how to design systems that survive AZ and Region failures.

## 2.1 High Availability and Fault Tolerance Patterns

### Key definitions (know the differences)

**High Availability (HA)** — minimizes downtime. If one component fails, another takes over, usually with a brief interruption. Target: 99.9% or 99.99% uptime.

**Fault Tolerance** — the system keeps running with NO interruption when a component fails. More expensive, requires active-active redundancy.

**Disaster Recovery (DR)** — the ability to recover from a catastrophic failure. Measured by:
- **RTO (Recovery Time Objective)** — max acceptable downtime (how long until you're back?)
- **RPO (Recovery Point Objective)** — max acceptable data loss (how old can your last good copy be?)

### DR strategies (know the order by cost/speed)

| Strategy | RTO | RPO | Cost | Description |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Backup & Restore | Hours | Hours | Lowest | Backup to S3; restore when needed |
| Pilot Light | Minutes–hours | Minutes | Low | Core infrastructure always on; scale up on disaster |
| Warm Standby | Minutes | Seconds–minutes | Medium | Scaled-down copy always running in secondary Region |
| Active-Active (Multi-Region) | Seconds | Near-zero | Highest | Full deployment in multiple Regions |

> **Exam Tip:** Questions about "fastest recovery" → Active-Active. "Lowest cost DR" → Backup & Restore. "Quickly scales up in secondary region" → Pilot Light. "Always running scaled-down copy" → Warm Standby.

### Multi-AZ patterns

The backbone of HA on AWS is spreading across at least two AZs:
- **EC2 + Auto Scaling across AZs + ELB** — traffic distributed, auto-replaced if instance fails
- **RDS Multi-AZ** — synchronous replication to standby in another AZ; automatic failover (~1–2 min)
- **Elasticache Multi-AZ** — Redis with Multi-AZ for automatic failover
- **DynamoDB** — always multi-AZ by default, within a Region

## 2.2 Elastic Load Balancing (ELB)

Three types to know deeply — they have different capabilities:

### Application Load Balancer (ALB) — Layer 7

- Routes based on HTTP content: path (`/api/*`), host headers (`api.example.com`), query strings, headers
- Target types: EC2 instances, IP addresses, Lambda functions, ECS containers
- Supports HTTP/2 and WebSocket
- Has sticky sessions (session affinity via cookies)
- Best for web applications, microservices, containers
- Provides access logs and can integrate with WAF

### Network Load Balancer (NLB) — Layer 4

- Routes based on TCP/UDP/TLS protocols
- Handles millions of requests per second with ultra-low latency
- Preserves the client IP address
- Supports **static IP addresses** (or Elastic IPs) — useful for whitelisting
- Best for: real-time gaming, financial systems, IoT, anything that needs extreme performance or static IPs

### Gateway Load Balancer (GWLB) — Layer 3/4

- Runs third-party virtual appliances (firewalls, IDS/IPS) transparently in the traffic path
- Traffic is sent to your appliances before reaching the application
- Uses GENEVE protocol

### Classic Load Balancer (CLB) — Legacy

Avoid in new designs. Handles Layer 4 and basic Layer 7. Still on the exam, but always wrong when ALB or NLB is an option.

> **Common Trap:** "Need static IP for the load balancer for firewall whitelisting" → NLB (has Elastic IP support). ALB does NOT support static IPs. "Need to route based on URL path" → ALB. "Latency-sensitive TCP traffic" → NLB.

### Cross-Zone Load Balancing

When enabled, each load balancer node distributes traffic evenly across all registered targets across all AZs. Without it, each node distributes only to targets in its AZ.
- ALB: always enabled (free)
- NLB/GWLB: disabled by default (enabled costs money for cross-AZ data transfer)

### ELB Health Checks

ELB constantly checks the health of targets and only sends traffic to healthy ones. If a target fails the health check, ELB removes it from rotation (even if Auto Scaling hasn't terminated the instance yet).

## 2.3 Auto Scaling

### Types of scaling policies

**Target Tracking** — keep a metric at a target value (e.g., keep CPU at 50%). Simplest to configure, recommended for most use cases.

**Step Scaling** — add/remove instances based on CloudWatch alarm thresholds, with different step sizes for different alarm levels. More control than target tracking.

**Scheduled Scaling** — scale in/out at specific times (e.g., add instances before 9am every Monday).

**Predictive Scaling** — uses ML to forecast load and proactively scale in advance of predicted demand.

### Lifecycle hooks

Pause an instance during launch or termination to run custom actions (install software, deregister from monitoring system). The instance stays in a wait state (up to 2 hours by default) while you run your scripts.

### Auto Scaling + ELB integration

When Auto Scaling launches a new instance, ELB's health check must pass before traffic is sent to it. When an instance is unhealthy, Auto Scaling can terminate and replace it.

### Auto Scaling Group (ASG) core settings

- **Minimum capacity** — never go below this
- **Maximum capacity** — never go above this
- **Desired capacity** — current target number of instances
- **Cooldown period** — wait time after scaling event before triggering another (default 300 sec)

## 2.4 Amazon RDS for Resilience

### Multi-AZ vs Read Replicas (most tested RDS concept)

| Feature | Multi-AZ | Read Replica |
|---|---|---|
| Purpose | High availability / failover | Read performance scaling |
| Replication | Synchronous | Asynchronous |
| Promotion | Automatic failover | Manual promotion |
| Used for reads? | No (standby is not accessible) | Yes |
| Cross-region? | Yes (for some engines) | Yes |
| Durability | Identical copy | Near-identical (slight lag) |

> **Common Trap:** The Multi-AZ standby is NOT readable — it exists only for failover. If you want to scale reads, use Read Replicas. If you want HA, use Multi-AZ.

### Amazon Aurora specifics (exam-heavy)

Aurora is AWS's cloud-native relational database. Compatible with MySQL and PostgreSQL but faster.

**Aurora storage:** Automatically grows in 10GB increments up to 128TB. Stores 6 copies of data across 3 AZs (4 of 6 needed for writes, 3 of 6 for reads). Automatically repairs corrupted data.

**Aurora Replicas:** Up to 15 read replicas. Failover to a replica takes under 30 seconds (vs 1–2 minutes for RDS Multi-AZ).

**Aurora Global Database:** Primary in one Region; up to 5 secondary Regions with read replicas. Cross-region replication in under 1 second. Secondary Region can be promoted to primary in under 1 minute for DR.

**Aurora Serverless v2:** Automatically scales Aurora capacity in fine-grained increments based on load. Great for unpredictable workloads. You pay per ACU-second.

**Aurora Multi-Master:** All nodes can read AND write. Active-active. Use for continuous write availability.

> **Exam Tip:** "Reduce RTO for global Aurora failure" → Aurora Global Database. "Variable workload, serverless DB" → Aurora Serverless. "Scale Aurora reads" → Aurora Replicas (up to 15).

## 2.5 Amazon S3 for Resilience

### S3 versioning

Keeps all versions of an object. Protects against accidental deletion (delete creates a delete marker, doesn't remove versions). Required for replication and MFA Delete.

### S3 replication

**S3 CRR (Cross-Region Replication)** — replicates to a bucket in a different Region. Requires versioning on both source and destination. Use for compliance, DR, or reducing latency for users in another Region.

**S3 SRR (Same-Region Replication)** — replicates to a bucket in the same Region. Use for log aggregation, test environment replication.

> Replication is asynchronous. Only new objects are replicated after you enable it (existing objects need S3 Batch Operations).

### S3 Transfer Acceleration

Routes uploads through CloudFront's edge network for faster uploads from distant locations. Good for global upload scenarios.

## 2.6 Decoupling with Messaging

Decoupling means components communicate via a queue/topic/event bus instead of calling each other directly. This makes systems more resilient — if one component goes down, messages queue up and processing resumes when it comes back.

### Amazon SQS (Simple Queue Service)

Managed message queue for decoupling. Messages stay in the queue until a consumer reads and deletes them.

**Key concepts:**
- **Standard Queue** — high throughput, at-least-once delivery, best-effort ordering. Messages may arrive out of order or be received more than once.
- **FIFO Queue** — exactly-once processing, strict ordering. Throughput limited to 3,000 messages/second (with batching).
- **Visibility timeout** — after a consumer picks up a message, others can't see it. Default 30 sec. If consumer crashes before deleting it, the message becomes visible again for retry.
- **Dead Letter Queue (DLQ)** — messages that fail processing N times go here for analysis.
- **Message retention** — 1 minute to 14 days (default 4 days).
- **Long polling** — wait up to 20 seconds for a message to arrive (reduces empty responses, lowers cost).
- **Max message size** — 256 KB.

> **Exam Tip:** "Order must be preserved, no duplicates" → SQS FIFO. "High throughput, ordering not required" → SQS Standard. "Process failed messages separately" → DLQ.

### Amazon SNS (Simple Notification Service)

Pub/sub messaging. One publisher, many subscribers. Fans out a message to multiple endpoints simultaneously.

Subscribers can be: SQS queues, Lambda functions, HTTP/HTTPS endpoints, email, SMS, mobile push.

**Fan-out pattern:** SNS topic → multiple SQS queues → each queue triggers independent processing. Decouples the producer from multiple consumers.

### Amazon EventBridge

Serverless event bus. Routes events from AWS services, custom apps, and SaaS partners to targets (Lambda, SQS, SNS, Step Functions, etc.).

**Event rules** filter by event source, event type, and content. More powerful than SNS for event-driven architectures.

**EventBridge Scheduler** — schedule events at a specific time or on a recurring schedule (cron).

### AWS Step Functions

Serverless visual workflow orchestration. Coordinates multiple AWS services into multi-step workflows with error handling, retries, parallel steps, and wait states. Use for:
- Multi-step order processing
- Data ETL pipelines
- ML model training workflows
- Human approval workflows

### Domain 2 — Mini Quiz (10 questions)

**Q1.** A company needs its RDS database to automatically fail over to a standby if the primary fails. Which feature provides this?

- A. RDS Read Replica
- B. RDS Multi-AZ deployment
- C. RDS Automated Backups
- D. RDS Multi-Region cluster

**Q2.** An application needs load balancing based on the URL path (/api/* goes to one group, /images/* goes to another). Which load balancer?

- A. Classic Load Balancer
- B. Network Load Balancer
- C. Application Load Balancer
- D. Gateway Load Balancer

**Q3.** A company has a global application. They want < 1-second cross-Region replication and can promote a secondary region to primary in < 1 minute for DR. Which Aurora feature?

- A. Aurora Multi-Master
- B. Aurora Serverless
- C. Aurora Global Database
- D. RDS Multi-AZ

**Q4.** Which SQS feature ensures a message that has been retrieved but not processed cannot be picked up by another consumer immediately?

- A. Dead Letter Queue
- B. Long polling
- C. Visibility timeout
- D. FIFO ordering

**Q5.** A message processing system must ensure no duplicate messages and strict ordering. Which SQS queue type?

- A. Standard Queue
- B. FIFO Queue
- C. Standard Queue with DLQ
- D. SQS with SNS

**Q6.** An architect is designing a fan-out pattern where one order event triggers parallel processing by 5 independent services. What is the BEST architecture?

- A. SQS Standard Queue with 5 consumers polling
- B. SNS topic with 5 SQS queues subscribed
- C. Lambda function calling 5 APIs
- D. SQS FIFO with 5 consumers

**Q7.** A company wants the lowest RTO and RPO for their critical database running in us-east-1. What DR strategy should they use?

- A. Backup & Restore in us-west-2
- B. Pilot Light in us-west-2
- C. Active-Active Multi-Region
- D. Warm Standby with 1-hour RTO

**Q8.** An Auto Scaling Group needs to ensure EC2 instances are properly configured (software installed) before receiving traffic. Which feature?

- A. Health check grace period only
- B. Lifecycle hooks with a wait state during launch
- C. Scheduled scaling
- D. Cooldown period

**Q9.** A company needs its load balancer to have a static IP for customers to whitelist. Which load balancer?

- A. Application Load Balancer
- B. Classic Load Balancer
- C. Network Load Balancer
- D. Gateway Load Balancer

**Q10.** S3 CRR is configured but no existing objects were replicated to the destination bucket. Why?

- A. Versioning is not enabled on the destination
- B. CRR only replicates objects uploaded AFTER replication is configured
- C. CRR requires bucket policies to be identical
- D. S3 CRR only works within the same account

### Domain 2 Answers

**Q1 -> B.** RDS Multi-AZ provides synchronous standby + automatic failover. Read Replicas are for performance.

**Q2 -> C.** ALB does content-based (Layer 7) routing including path-based routing.

**Q3 -> C.** Aurora Global Database = cross-region replication <1 second, promotion <1 minute.

**Q4 -> C.** Visibility timeout hides the message from other consumers while one consumer processes it.

**Q5 -> B.** FIFO = exactly-once processing + strict ordering.

**Q6 -> B.** SNS fans out to multiple SQS queues — each queue is independently consumed. Clean fan-out pattern.

**Q7 -> C.** Active-Active Multi-Region = lowest RTO and RPO (near-zero). Most expensive but highest resilience.

**Q8 -> B.** Lifecycle hooks pause the instance in a wait state so scripts can run before traffic is sent.

**Q9 -> C.** NLB supports Elastic IPs (static IPs). ALB does not.

**Q10 -> B.** S3 CRR only replicates NEW objects after configuration. Use S3 Batch Operations for existing objects.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Domain 3: Design High-Performing Architectures (24%)

Performance isn't just speed — it's choosing the right service for the job. This domain tests whether you can match a workload's requirements to the right compute, storage, database, and network solution.

## 3.1 Compute: Choosing the Right Option

### EC2 Instance Families

Know which family to use for which workload:

| Family | Optimized for | Example use cases |
|---|---|---|
| General Purpose (M, T) | Balanced CPU/memory | Web servers, dev/test, small DBs |
| Compute Optimized (C) | High CPU, low memory ratio | HPC, ML inference, video encoding, gaming |
| Memory Optimized (R, X, z) | High RAM | In-memory databases, real-time analytics, SAP HANA |
| Storage Optimized (I, D, H) | High local I/O, NVMe | NoSQL DBs, data warehousing, distributed file systems |
| Accelerated Computing (P, G, Inf, Trn) | GPU/custom chips | ML training, graphics rendering, video transcoding |

> **Exam Tip:** "High I/O performance" → Storage Optimized. "ML training" → Accelerated Computing (P or G instance). "Large in-memory cache" → Memory Optimized. "Cost-effective web server" → General Purpose (T for burstable, M for steady).

### EC2 Placement Groups

Control where instances are placed physically:

**Cluster** — all instances in a single rack in one AZ. Lowest network latency, highest throughput (10 Gbps enhanced networking). Risk: if the rack fails, all instances fail. Use for: HPC, tightly coupled workloads.

**Spread** — each instance on a different physical rack. Max 7 instances per AZ per group. Best for: critical instances that must not share a failure point (small number of high-importance instances).

**Partition** — instances divided into partitions (max 7 per AZ), each partition on separate racks. Partitions don't share hardware. Use for: HDFS, HBase, Cassandra — large distributed workloads.

### EC2 storage options

**Instance Store (Ephemeral)** — physically attached NVMe SSDs. Extremely high IOPS. Data is LOST when the instance stops, hibernates, or terminates. Good for: temp files, caches, buffers, scratch data.

**EBS (Elastic Block Store)** — network-attached persistent block storage. Survives instance stop/terminate. One EBS volume attaches to one EC2 at a time (except for EBS Multi-Attach on io2).

EBS volume types:
- **gp3** — general purpose SSD. Baseline 3,000 IOPS, up to 16,000 IOPS independently from size. Boot volumes, dev/test, most workloads.
- **gp2** — older gen SSD. IOPS tied to size (3 IOPS/GB). Getting replaced by gp3.
- **io2 Block Express** — provisioned IOPS SSD. Up to 256,000 IOPS. For I/O intensive databases (Oracle, SAP HANA). Supports Multi-Attach.
- **st1** — throughput optimized HDD. Sequential reads/writes. Big data, log processing. Cannot be a boot volume.
- **sc1** — cold HDD. Lowest cost. Infrequently accessed data. Cannot be a boot volume.

> **Exam Tip:** "Need consistent, very high IOPS for database" → io2. "Cost-effective throughput for big data" → st1. "General boot volume" → gp3.

### Lambda for serverless compute

Lambda runs code in response to events without you managing servers. Key characteristics:
- Max execution time: **15 minutes**
- Memory: 128 MB – 10,240 MB
- Ephemeral storage: /tmp up to 10 GB
- No persistent state (use DynamoDB, S3, or ElastiCache for state)
- Billing: per request + per 100ms of duration
- Scales automatically (thousands of concurrent executions)

**Lambda triggers:** S3, DynamoDB Streams, Kinesis, SQS, SNS, API Gateway, EventBridge, ALB, Cognito, and more.

**Lambda + VPC:** Lambda can run in a VPC to access private resources (RDS, ElastiCache). Needs NAT Gateway (or VPC endpoints) to reach the internet or AWS services from within the VPC.

**Lambda concurrency:** Default 1,000 concurrent executions per Region. Reserve concurrency for critical functions; use provisioned concurrency to eliminate cold starts.

### Containers: ECS vs EKS vs Fargate

**Amazon ECS (Elastic Container Service)** — AWS-native container orchestration. Simpler to operate than Kubernetes. Two launch types:
- **EC2 launch type** — you manage the underlying EC2 instances
- **Fargate launch type** — serverless, AWS manages the infrastructure

**Amazon EKS (Elastic Kubernetes Service)** — managed Kubernetes. Use when you're already using Kubernetes or need its rich ecosystem. Can run on EC2 or Fargate.

**AWS Fargate** — serverless compute for containers. Works with both ECS and EKS. No servers to manage, auto-scales, pay per vCPU/memory second.

> **Exam Tip:** "Least operational overhead for containers" → Fargate. "Already using Kubernetes" → EKS. "Simple container deployment with AWS-native tools" → ECS with Fargate.

### AWS Lambda vs ECS Fargate

| | Lambda | ECS Fargate |
|---|---|---|
| Max execution | 15 minutes | Unlimited |
| Startup | Cold start (~100ms–few sec) | Slower start (~30 sec) |
| State | Stateless | Can have state via EFS mount |
| Trigger | Event-driven | Always-on or task-based |
| Use case | Short, event-driven functions | Long-running containers |

## 3.2 Storage: Choosing the Right Option

### S3 storage classes — performance context

| Class | Retrieval latency | Use case |
|---|---|---|
| S3 Standard | Milliseconds | Frequently accessed data |
| S3 Intelligent-Tiering | Milliseconds | Unknown or changing access patterns |
| S3 Standard-IA | Milliseconds | Infrequent access, still needs fast retrieval |
| S3 One Zone-IA | Milliseconds | Infrequent access, can recreate if AZ fails |
| S3 Glacier Instant Retrieval | Milliseconds | Archive with occasional instant access |
| S3 Glacier Flexible Retrieval | Minutes–hours | Archive, expedited/standard/bulk retrieval |
| S3 Glacier Deep Archive | Up to 12 hours | Long-term archival, lowest cost |

**S3 Intelligent-Tiering** automatically moves objects between tiers based on access patterns. Small monitoring fee per object. Zero retrieval fee. Best when you don't know how often data will be accessed.

### S3 performance optimizations

- **Multi-part upload** — upload large objects in parallel parts. Required for objects > 5 GB, recommended for > 100 MB. Speeds up uploads dramatically.
- **Byte-range fetches** — download specific byte ranges in parallel. Speeds up downloads and allows resumable downloads.
- **S3 Transfer Acceleration** — uses CloudFront edge for faster uploads from distant locations.
- **S3 prefix performance** — S3 can handle 3,500 PUT/COPY/POST/DELETE and 5,500 GET/HEAD requests per second per prefix. Spread objects across prefixes for higher throughput.

### S3 Select and Glacier Select

Run SQL expressions on S3 objects to retrieve only a subset of data. Reduces data transferred and processing time. Works on CSV, JSON, Parquet files.

### AWS Storage Gateway

Hybrid storage bridge between on-premises and AWS. Three modes:

**File Gateway** — on-premises NFS/SMB file shares backed by S3. Files appear local, stored in S3. Caches frequently accessed data locally.

**Volume Gateway** — iSCSI block storage. Two modes:
- Cached: primary data in S3, hot data cached locally
- Stored: primary data stored locally, backed up to S3 as EBS snapshots

**Tape Gateway** — virtual tape library backed by S3 and Glacier. Drop-in for existing tape backup software.

### AWS DataSync

Automated data transfer service. Moves large amounts of data between on-premises NAS/SAN/HDFS and S3, EFS, FSx. Faster than traditional tools (uses parallel transfers, automatic encryption). Good for one-time migration or ongoing replication.

## 3.3 Databases: Choosing the Right Type

### Decision matrix: which database to use?

| Requirement | Best Database |
|---|---|
| Relational/SQL, managed | RDS (MySQL, PostgreSQL, etc.) |
| Relational, high performance, cloud-native | Aurora |
| NoSQL, key-value, millisecond latency, serverless | DynamoDB |
| Analytics, data warehouse, petabyte-scale SQL | Redshift |
| In-memory cache, microsecond latency | ElastiCache (Redis/Memcached) |
| Document store (JSON documents) | DocumentDB (MongoDB compatible) |
| Graph database (relationships) | Neptune |
| Time-series data | Timestream |
| Ledger (immutable transaction history) | QLDB |
| Search and full-text search | OpenSearch Service |

> **Exam Tip:** Read the keyword in the question: "caching" → ElastiCache, "graph" → Neptune, "ledger/immutable" → QLDB, "time-series" → Timestream, "search" → OpenSearch, "document/JSON" → DocumentDB.

### DynamoDB deep dive

Key characteristics:
- Serverless, fully managed NoSQL
- Single-digit millisecond performance at any scale
- Automatically replicates across 3 AZs
- Table has a **Partition Key** (required) and optional **Sort Key**
- Two capacity modes:
  - **Provisioned** — specify read/write capacity units; use Auto Scaling; predictable cost
  - **On-Demand** — pay per request; scales automatically; good for unknown/spiky traffic

**DynamoDB Accelerator (DAX)** — in-memory cache for DynamoDB. Microsecond read latency (vs millisecond). Fully managed, no code change to read through. Use when DynamoDB read performance isn't enough.

**DynamoDB Streams** — ordered stream of item-level changes in a DynamoDB table. Triggers Lambda for real-time processing (e.g., send notifications on new orders).

**Global Tables** — multi-region, active-active DynamoDB. Replicates across multiple Regions with eventual consistency. For global applications that need local read/write.

**DynamoDB Indexes:**
- **LSI (Local Secondary Index)** — same partition key, different sort key. Created at table creation only.
- **GSI (Global Secondary Index)** — different partition key AND sort key. Can be created anytime. Has its own throughput.

### ElastiCache

**Redis** — more feature-rich: persistence, replication, complex data types (lists, sets, sorted sets), Pub/Sub, Lua scripting, Geospatial, Multi-AZ with failover.

**Memcached** — simpler, pure caching, multi-threaded, no replication, no persistence.

> **Exam Tip:** "Caching session data with failover" → Redis. "Simple, multi-threaded caching, no persistence needed" → Memcached. "Real-time leaderboard" → Redis Sorted Sets.

### Amazon Redshift

Columnar data warehouse. Designed for analytics on structured data (billions of rows). Integrates with S3, DynamoDB, EMR, etc.

**Redshift Spectrum** — query data directly in S3 from Redshift without loading it into the warehouse. Extends Redshift to query exabytes of data in S3.

**Redshift Serverless** — automatically scales Redshift capacity; no cluster to manage.

## 3.4 Network Performance

### Amazon CloudFront — CDN

CloudFront caches content at **edge locations** near users to reduce latency.

**Origins:** S3 bucket, ALB, EC2, custom HTTP origin.

**Behaviors:** rules that determine cache settings per path (e.g., `/api/*` → no cache, forward to origin; `/images/*` → cache 1 day).

**Cache invalidation** — manually remove objects from the cache. Costs per invalidation path.

**Signed URLs vs Signed Cookies:**
- Signed URLs — grant access to a single object. Use for individual file downloads.
- Signed Cookies — grant access to multiple objects with one cookie. Use for premium content subscribers.

**Origin Access Control (OAC)** — replaces Origin Access Identity (OAI). Restricts S3 bucket access so only CloudFront can read from it (bucket not publicly accessible directly).

**CloudFront Functions vs Lambda@Edge:**
- CloudFront Functions — ultra-lightweight, run at edge, sub-millisecond, for URL rewrites, header manipulation, auth at edge
- Lambda@Edge — full Lambda at edge, more powerful, for complex processing like A/B testing, rendering

### Amazon Route 53 — DNS and Routing Policies

Route 53 routing policies — must know all of them:

| Policy | Use case |
|---|---|
| Simple | Default, single resource. No health check-based routing. |
| Weighted | Split traffic by percentage (A/B testing, blue/green) |
| Latency-based | Route to Region with lowest latency for the user |
| Failover | Active-passive. Route to primary; fail to secondary if health check fails |
| Geolocation | Route based on user's country/continent |
| Geoproximity | Route based on geographic distance; can bias toward a Region |
| Multivalue Answer | Returns multiple IPs (up to 8), each checked for health. Not a replacement for ELB. |
| IP-based | Route based on the client's IP address/CIDR |

> **Exam Tip:** "Active-passive failover" → Failover policy. "Blue/green deployment" → Weighted. "Users in Asia go to Tokyo, users in US go to us-east-1" → Geolocation. "Route to closest server" → Latency-based.

### AWS Global Accelerator

Routes user traffic through AWS's global network to the nearest edge, then on AWS's backbone to the origin — instead of over the unpredictable public internet.

Provides **static Anycast IP addresses** (2 static IPs) that route globally. Works at the network layer (Layer 3/4).

**CloudFront vs Global Accelerator:**
- CloudFront: caches content at edge (good for static content, reduces origin load)
- Global Accelerator: no caching, just optimal routing for any TCP/UDP traffic (good for non-HTTP, gaming, IoT, applications needing static IPs)

### Domain 3 — Mini Quiz (10 questions)

**Q1.** An ML training workload needs hundreds of EC2 instances with the lowest possible network latency between them. Which placement group?

- A. Spread
- B. Partition
- C. Cluster
- D. No placement group needed

**Q2.** A database application requires 100,000 IOPS consistently. Which EBS volume type?

- A. gp3
- B. st1
- C. io2
- D. sc1

**Q3.** A Lambda function must access an RDS database in a private subnet. What does the architect need to configure?

- A. Attach an Elastic IP to Lambda
- B. Run Lambda inside the VPC with a NAT Gateway for internet access if needed
- C. Make RDS publicly accessible
- D. Lambda cannot access VPC resources

**Q4.** A company wants to query data in S3 directly from Redshift without loading it into the warehouse. Which feature?

- A. Amazon Athena
- B. AWS Glue
- C. Redshift Spectrum
- D. Amazon EMR

**Q5.** A gaming application needs microsecond latency for a real-time leaderboard with persistence and failover. Which service?

- A. ElastiCache for Memcached
- B. ElastiCache for Redis
- C. DynamoDB
- D. RDS MySQL

**Q6.** A company has a React app with static assets (HTML, CSS, JS) and an API backend on EC2. Which setup gives the BEST performance for global users?

- A. EC2 with enhanced networking in us-east-1
- B. CloudFront distribution with S3 for static assets and ALB as origin for API
- C. Route 53 with latency routing only
- D. Direct S3 static website hosting without CloudFront

**Q7.** An architect needs Route 53 to send 80% of traffic to a new version of an app and 20% to the old version. Which routing policy?

- A. Failover
- B. Geolocation
- C. Weighted
- D. Latency

**Q8.** Which DynamoDB feature provides microsecond read performance without code changes?

- A. DynamoDB Streams
- B. DynamoDB Global Tables
- C. DynamoDB DAX
- D. DynamoDB On-Demand mode

**Q9.** A company needs consistent static IP addresses for their globally deployed application so clients can whitelist them in firewalls. Which service provides this BEST?

- A. CloudFront
- B. Application Load Balancer
- C. AWS Global Accelerator
- D. Route 53 Multivalue

**Q10.** A company is migrating 50 TB of on-premises NAS data to EFS. Which service provides the FASTEST automated transfer?

- A. Physical shipment via Snowball
- B. AWS DataSync
- C. S3 Transfer Acceleration
- D. Manual rsync over Direct Connect

### Domain 3 Answers

**Q1 -> C.** Cluster placement group = lowest latency between instances in one AZ. Ideal for HPC/ML training.

**Q2 -> C.** io2 supports up to 256,000 IOPS. gp3 maxes at 16,000 IOPS.

**Q3 -> B.** Lambda runs inside the VPC to access private RDS. If Lambda needs internet access from VPC, add a NAT Gateway in a public subnet.

**Q4 -> C.** Redshift Spectrum queries S3 data directly without loading it. Athena also does this but uses Athena, not Redshift.

**Q5 -> B.** Redis has persistence, Multi-AZ failover, and sorted sets (for leaderboards). Memcached has none of these.

**Q6 -> B.** CloudFront + S3 for static assets = global caching at edge. ALB as origin for API = dynamic content handled efficiently.

**Q7 -> C.** Weighted routing splits traffic by percentage. Perfect for blue/green or gradual rollouts.

**Q8 -> C.** DAX is the in-memory cache for DynamoDB, providing microsecond reads with no code change.

**Q9 -> C.** Global Accelerator provides 2 static Anycast IPs that work globally.

**Q10 -> B.** DataSync is designed for automated, high-speed data transfer from NAS/HDFS to EFS/S3/FSx.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Domain 4: Design Cost-Optimized Architectures (20%)

Cost optimization isn't about being cheap — it's about getting the right resources for the right price. This domain tests your ability to match AWS pricing models to workload patterns.

## 4.1 Cost-Optimized Compute

### EC2 purchasing options — when to use each

**On-Demand** — pay by second/hour, no commitment. Use for: unpredictable workloads, first-time apps, short-term jobs.

**Reserved Instances (RIs)** — 1- or 3-year commitment in exchange for up to 72% discount.
- **Standard RI** — specific instance family/size/region. Highest discount. Can sell on RI Marketplace.
- **Convertible RI** — can change instance family/OS/tenancy. Lower discount. Cannot sell on Marketplace.
- Payment options: All Upfront (highest discount), Partial Upfront, No Upfront (lowest discount).

**Savings Plans** — commit to a consistent amount of compute usage ($/hour) for 1 or 3 years.
- **Compute Savings Plans** — apply across EC2, Fargate, Lambda. Most flexible, up to 66% savings.
- **EC2 Instance Savings Plans** — specific instance family in a region. Up to 72% savings. Less flexible.

**Spot Instances** — use spare AWS capacity at up to 90% discount. Can be reclaimed by AWS with 2-minute notice. Use for: fault-tolerant, stateless, batch, flexible jobs. NOT for databases, stateful apps, or anything that can't be interrupted.

**Dedicated Hosts** — physical server dedicated to you. Use for: software licenses (Windows Server, SQL Server per-socket), compliance requirements that need physical isolation.

**Dedicated Instances** — instances run on hardware dedicated to you but at the instance level. Don't use for BYOL licensing (need Dedicated Hosts for that).

> **Cost Optimization Rule:** Steady, predictable = Reserved/Savings Plans. Unpredictable spikes = On-Demand or Savings Plans. Interruptible batch = Spot. Licensing compliance = Dedicated Hosts.

### Spot Instances and Spot Fleet

**Spot Fleet** — a collection of Spot Instances and optionally On-Demand Instances. You define pools (instance types, AZs), a target capacity, and a max price. The fleet picks the cheapest pool that meets the target.

**Spot Instance interruption:** When AWS needs capacity back, it sends a 2-minute warning via instance metadata and EventBridge. Handle interruptions gracefully: save state, drain connections, use SQS to track work.

### AWS Lambda cost optimization

Lambda charges per request + per GB-second of execution. Optimization:
- Set memory appropriately (also controls CPU allocation)
- Minimize execution time (optimize code)
- Use Graviton2 (arm64) — 20% cheaper, 19% faster for many workloads
- Avoid cold starts for latency-sensitive: use Provisioned Concurrency (but costs more)
- Use S3 or EFS for large assets; don't package unnecessarily large deployment archives

### AWS Fargate cost optimization

Pay per vCPU + GB-memory per second. Optimization:
- Right-size your task definitions
- Use Graviton (arm64) Fargate — cheaper
- Use Fargate Spot for non-critical tasks (~70% savings, interruption possible)

## 4.2 Cost-Optimized Storage

### S3 cost optimization

Largest levers:
- Use **lifecycle policies** to automatically move objects to cheaper storage classes as they age.
- Use **S3 Intelligent-Tiering** when access patterns are unknown.
- Delete unused incomplete multipart uploads (use lifecycle policy to abort after N days).
- Enable **S3 Inventory** to analyze your bucket and find large/old/infrequently accessed objects.
- Delete old versions if you don't need them (versioning + lifecycle on noncurrent versions).

### EBS cost optimization

- **Delete unused snapshots** — they accumulate quietly.
- Use **gp3 over gp2** — cheaper with independently configurable IOPS (don't over-provision IOPS).
- Delete unattached EBS volumes.
- Use **EBS Snapshots** to back up and restore instead of keeping large volumes attached to idle instances.

### EFS vs FSx for Windows cost optimization

EFS is priced per GB stored. Use **EFS IA (Infrequent Access)** tier with lifecycle policies to move cold files automatically. EFS Intelligent-Tiering does this automatically.

## 4.3 Cost-Optimized Databases

**RDS Reserved Instances** — major savings for steady-state databases. Up to 69% discount.

**DynamoDB On-Demand vs Provisioned:**
- On-Demand: pay per request, no planning. Higher per-request cost. Good for unknown/spiky traffic.
- Provisioned: reserve capacity units. Much cheaper per unit. Good for steady or predictable traffic.
- Use **DynamoDB Auto Scaling** with provisioned mode to handle spikes while saving on baseline.

**RDS vs Aurora Serverless:**
- Stopped RDS instances cost storage only (compute charges stop when stopped — but can only stop for 7 days).
- Aurora Serverless v2 scales to zero (minimum is 0.5 ACU) — great for dev/test databases.

**ElastiCache Reserved Nodes** — like EC2 RIs, for steady-state caching.

## 4.4 Cost-Optimized Networking

**Data transfer costs matter:**
- Data in to AWS: generally free
- Data out to internet: charges per GB (this is where bills spike)
- Data between AZs in the same Region: small charge each direction
- Data between Regions: higher charge
- Data between services in the same AZ using private IPs: free

**Optimization strategies:**
- Use **VPC Endpoints** to keep S3/DynamoDB traffic within the AWS network (avoid NAT Gateway costs)
- Use **S3** to deliver large files instead of EC2 (cheaper data transfer)
- Use **CloudFront** to reduce origin data transfer costs (CloudFront-to-internet is cheaper than EC2-to-internet; cached hits don't hit origin at all)
- Use **Direct Connect** for consistently high-volume data transfer (lower per-GB rate than internet)

### Cost monitoring tools (revisit from CLF at a deeper level)

**AWS Cost Explorer** — visualize and analyze spend. Group by service, tag, account, region. 12-month forecast. Use to identify the most expensive services.

**AWS Budgets** — set alerts for actual or forecasted spend. Also supports RI/Savings Plan utilization budgets.

**Cost Allocation Tags** — tag resources by project/team/environment; view in Cost Explorer and Cost & Usage Report.

**AWS Cost and Usage Report (CUR)** — most granular billing data. Used with Athena + QuickSight for custom dashboards.

**AWS Trusted Advisor** — checks for cost optimization opportunities: idle RIs, underutilized EC2, unattached EBS volumes, idle load balancers.

**AWS Compute Optimizer** — analyzes EC2, Lambda, ECS, EBS usage and recommends right-sizing. Uses ML to compare your workload against optimal configurations.

> **Exam Tip:** "Automatically right-size EC2 for cost" → Compute Optimizer. "Alert when spending too much" → Budgets. "Find underutilized resources" → Trusted Advisor. "Analyze past spending by tag" → Cost Explorer.

### Domain 4 — Mini Quiz (10 questions)

**Q1.** A company runs a batch processing job every weekend that can be interrupted. Which EC2 option minimizes cost?

- A. On-Demand Instances
- B. Reserved Instances (1-year)
- C. Spot Instances
- D. Dedicated Hosts

**Q2.** A company wants the MOST flexible purchasing discount that applies to both EC2 and Lambda. Which option?

- A. Standard Reserved Instances
- B. Convertible Reserved Instances
- C. Compute Savings Plans
- D. EC2 Instance Savings Plans

**Q3.** S3 objects are frequently accessed for the first month after creation, then rarely accessed after 90 days. What S3 cost optimization should be applied?

- A. Delete all objects after 90 days
- B. Configure a lifecycle policy to transition objects to S3 Glacier after 90 days
- C. Enable S3 Transfer Acceleration
- D. Replicate objects to a cheaper Region

**Q4.** A company pays high data transfer charges for EC2 instances sending data to S3 through a NAT Gateway. What is the MOST cost-effective fix?

- A. Upgrade to Direct Connect
- B. Add an S3 Gateway Endpoint to the VPC
- C. Move EC2 instances to a public subnet
- D. Use S3 Transfer Acceleration

**Q5.** Which service uses machine learning to identify right-sizing opportunities for EC2 instances?

- A. AWS Trusted Advisor
- B. AWS Cost Explorer
- C. AWS Compute Optimizer
- D. AWS Budgets

**Q6.** A company runs a development RDS database that is only used during business hours. How can they reduce costs?

- A. Delete the DB every evening and restore from snapshot each morning
- B. Switch to DynamoDB
- C. Stop the RDS instance outside business hours (up to 7 days at a time)
- D. Use Reserved Instances for 3 years

**Q7.** For a Spot Instance, how much notice does AWS give before reclaiming it?

- A. 30 seconds
- B. 2 minutes
- C. 5 minutes
- D. 1 hour

**Q8.** A company needs to reduce CloudFront data transfer costs. Their origin is an EC2 server. What BEST approach reduces costs?

- A. Move origin to Direct Connect
- B. Cache static content in CloudFront (CloudFront-to-origin requests and CloudFront-to-internet rates are cheaper than direct EC2-to-internet)
- C. Use S3 instead of CloudFront
- D. Enable enhanced networking on EC2

**Q9.** A DynamoDB table has very spiky traffic — millions of requests during flash sales, near-zero otherwise. Which capacity mode minimizes cost?

- A. Provisioned with maximum capacity
- B. On-Demand mode
- C. Provisioned with Auto Scaling set to max
- D. Global Tables

**Q10.** A company wants a Reserved Instance that gives the flexibility to change instance type within the same family. Which type?

- A. Standard Reserved Instance
- B. Convertible Reserved Instance
- C. Savings Plan (Compute)
- D. Dedicated Host

### Domain 4 Answers

**Q1 -> C.** Spot Instances are cheapest for interruptible batch jobs.

**Q2 -> C.** Compute Savings Plans apply across EC2, Fargate, and Lambda — the most flexible.

**Q3 -> B.** S3 Lifecycle policy transitions to Glacier after 90 days — pays for cheap archival, not expensive Standard.

**Q4 -> B.** S3 Gateway Endpoint routes S3 traffic within the AWS network, eliminating NAT Gateway data processing costs.

**Q5 -> C.** AWS Compute Optimizer uses ML to recommend right-sizing for EC2, EBS, Lambda, ECS.

**Q6 -> C.** RDS instances can be stopped (compute charges stop) for up to 7 days. AWS auto-starts them after 7 days.

**Q7 -> B.** 2-minute Spot Instance interruption notice.

**Q8 -> B.** CloudFront caches content at edge, reducing the number of requests hitting the EC2 origin. CloudFront's data transfer rate to the internet is lower than direct EC2.

**Q9 -> B.** DynamoDB On-Demand scales with the traffic level automatically — no waste on provisioned capacity during quiet periods.

**Q10 -> B.** Convertible RIs can change instance family, OS, and tenancy. Standard RIs cannot.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Must-Know Comparison Tables

## ALB vs NLB vs GWLB

| Feature | ALB | NLB | GWLB |
|---|---|---|---|
| OSI Layer | 7 (HTTP/HTTPS) | 4 (TCP/UDP/TLS) | 3/4 |
| Content-based routing | Yes | No | No |
| Static IP | No | Yes (Elastic IP) | No |
| WebSocket / HTTP/2 | Yes | No | N/A |
| Ultra-low latency | No | Yes | N/A |
| Use case | Web apps, microservices | High-perf TCP, static IP | Virtual appliances (FW, IPS) |

## S3 vs EBS vs EFS vs FSx

| | S3 | EBS | EFS | FSx |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Type | Object | Block | File (NFS) | File (specialized) |
| Access | HTTP API | One EC2 (io2: Multi-Attach) | Many EC2 (Linux) | Many EC2 (Windows/HPC) |
| Persistence | Always | Persists (lost if deleted) | Always | Always |
| Max size | Unlimited | 64 TB per volume | Petabytes | Petabytes |
| Latency | ms | sub-ms (SSD) | sub-ms | sub-ms |
| Use case | Backup, web, archive | OS disk, DB volumes | Shared Linux files | Windows share, HPC |

## RDS vs Aurora vs DynamoDB vs Redshift

| | RDS | Aurora | DynamoDB | Redshift |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Type | Relational | Relational | NoSQL | Columnar warehouse |
| Query language | SQL | SQL | PartiQL/API | SQL |
| Scale | Vertical | Auto (storage) | Horizontal (serverless) | Node-based |
| Read replicas | 5 max | 15 max | N/A (GSI) | 1 (Redshift) |
| Use case | Standard RDBMS | High-perf RDBMS | Key-value scale | Analytical queries |
| Serverless option | No | Aurora Serverless v2 | Always serverless | Redshift Serverless |

## Route 53 Routing Policies

| Policy | Logic | Best for |
|---|---|---|
| Simple | One record, no health check | Single resource |
| Weighted | % split | A/B testing, blue/green |
| Latency | Lowest latency for user | Global multi-region |
| Failover | Active → passive on failure | DR |
| Geolocation | User's country/continent | Content compliance |
| Geoproximity | Distance + bias | Traffic shifting |
| Multivalue | Multiple IPs, health-checked | Pseudo load balancing |

## EC2 Purchasing Options

| Option | Discount | Commitment | Interruptible | Use case |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| On-Demand | None | None | No | Short-term, unpredictable |
| Reserved (1-yr) | ~40% | 1 year | No | Steady workloads |
| Reserved (3-yr) | ~72% | 3 years | No | Long-term steady |
| Savings Plans | up to 66–72% | 1 or 3 years | No | Flexible steady workloads |
| Spot | up to 90% | None | Yes (2-min notice) | Fault-tolerant batch |
| Dedicated Host | Variable | 1 or 3 years | No | BYOL, compliance |

## SQS vs SNS vs EventBridge

| | SQS | SNS | EventBridge |
|---|---|---|---|
| Pattern | Point-to-point (queue) | Pub/sub (push) | Event bus |
| Message persistence | Yes (up to 14 days) | No | No |
| Ordering | FIFO only | No | No |
| Filtering | No | Yes (message filter) | Yes (event pattern) |
| Use case | Decouple producers/consumers | Fan-out notifications | Event-driven between services |

## CloudFront vs Global Accelerator vs Route 53

| | CloudFront | Global Accelerator | Route 53 |
|---|---|---|---|
| Purpose | CDN (cache at edge) | Network routing optimization | DNS |
| Caching | Yes | No | No |
| Static IP | No | Yes (Anycast) | No |
| Protocol | HTTP/HTTPS | Any TCP/UDP | DNS |
| Use case | Static content, web apps | Non-HTTP, static IP, global gaming | DNS routing, health checks, failover |

## EBS Volume Types

| Type | IOPS | Throughput | Bootable | Use case |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| gp3 | up to 16,000 | up to 1,000 MB/s | Yes | Default for most, boot volumes |
| gp2 | up to 16,000 (tied to size) | up to 250 MB/s | Yes | Legacy, use gp3 instead |
| io2 Block Express | up to 256,000 | up to 4,000 MB/s | Yes | High-IOPS databases |
| st1 | Low | up to 500 MB/s | No | Big data, sequential reads |
| sc1 | Lowest | up to 250 MB/s | No | Cold infrequent access |
