# ============================================================
# CHAPTER 6: CKA — Certified Kubernetes Administrator
# ============================================================

# Certified Kubernetes Administrator (CKA)
## Read-to-Pass Complete Guide

Version 1.0 — June 2026 | Kubernetes v1.32+ | Exam code: **CKA**

---

> **Disclaimer:** Independent study guide. Not affiliated with CNCF or The Linux Foundation.
> All practice tasks are original scenarios. Always verify current exam objectives at
> training.linuxfoundation.org before your exam date.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Preface

## What makes CKA different from every other cert in this series

Every other cert you've done is multiple-choice. CKA is pure terminal. Seventeen real tasks on a real (or simulated) Kubernetes cluster. You type kubectl commands, write YAML manifests, debug broken nodes, and fix networking issues — all within 2 hours. There is no guessing, no elimination strategy. You either know how to do the thing or you don't.

This means your preparation must be hands-on. Reading this guide is necessary but not sufficient. You need to build the muscle memory of typing `kubectl` commands fast, without Googling. The official kubernetes.io documentation **is** allowed during the exam — but searching for syntax there costs you 3–5 minutes per lookup. Your goal is to only look things up for complex YAML you can't remember, not basic commands.

## Exam facts

| Item | Detail |
|---|---|
| Provider | CNCF / The Linux Foundation |
| Exam format | Performance-based (hands-on terminal only — no MCQs) |
| Tasks | ~17 tasks across multiple Kubernetes clusters |
| Time | 2 hours (120 minutes) |
| Passing score | 66% |
| Cost | ~$445 USD (includes one free retake + Killer Shell simulator × 2) |
| Kubernetes version | v1.32/1.33 (check training.linuxfoundation.org for current) |
| Resources allowed | kubernetes.io/docs, kubernetes.io/blog (and GitHub kubernetes/website) |
| Validity | 3 years |

## Domain weights

| Domain | Weight | Focus |
|---|---|---|
| Troubleshooting | 30% | **Highest — invest most time here** |
| Cluster Architecture, Installation & Configuration | 25% | kubeadm, RBAC, etcd |
| Services & Networking | 20% | Services, DNS, NetworkPolicy, Ingress |
| Workloads & Scheduling | 15% | Pods, Deployments, ConfigMaps, Secrets |
| Storage | 10% | PV, PVC, StorageClass |

## How the exam works

You'll work in a browser-based terminal connected to several pre-built Kubernetes clusters. Each task specifies which cluster to use — you switch between them using the `kubectl config use-context` command provided in the task.

Each task is worth a certain percentage of your total score. Tasks are independent — failing one doesn't affect others.

**The two-pass strategy:**
1. First pass: complete tasks you know confidently (2–5 min each).
2. Second pass: return to flagged/harder tasks with remaining time.

Never spend more than 10 minutes on a single task before moving on and coming back.

## Study plan

**6-week plan (if you have basic Kubernetes knowledge):**
- Week 1: Cluster Architecture + kubectl basics + YAML mastery
- Week 2: Workloads & Scheduling + Storage
- Week 3: Services & Networking
- Week 4: Troubleshooting (spend the most time here)
- Week 5: Full practice clusters (Killer Shell, KodeKloud, Killercoda)
- Week 6: Timed mock tasks + weak area review

**Tools you MUST practice with:**
- **KodeKloud CKA course** (Mumshad Mannambeth) — best structured learning
- **Killer Shell** (included with exam purchase) — closest to real exam difficulty
- **Killercoda** — free Kubernetes scenarios
- **kind/k3s/minikube** — local cluster for daily practice

> **Exam Tip:** Speed is everything. If you can't do a task fast enough, the knowledge doesn't matter. Practice until you can create a Deployment in under 90 seconds without looking anything up.

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# The kubectl Cheat Sheet (memorize this)

> During the exam, you can use these. But looking things up costs time. Memorize the ones marked ⭐.

## Core imperative commands ⭐

```bash
# Cluster info
kubectl cluster-info
kubectl get nodes -o wide
kubectl describe node <node-name>

# Pods
kubectl run nginx --image=nginx                          # create pod
kubectl run nginx --image=nginx --dry-run=client -o yaml  # generate YAML ⭐
kubectl get pods -A                                     # all namespaces
kubectl get pods -n kube-system
kubectl describe pod <pod-name>
kubectl logs <pod-name>
kubectl logs <pod-name> -c <container-name>             # multi-container
kubectl exec -it <pod-name> -- /bin/bash
kubectl exec -it <pod-name> -- sh
kubectl exec <pod-name> -- <command>
kubectl delete pod <pod-name> --force --grace-period=0  # force delete

# Deployments
kubectl create deployment nginx --image=nginx --replicas=3  ⭐
kubectl scale deployment nginx --replicas=5
kubectl set image deployment/nginx nginx=nginx:1.19
kubectl rollout status deployment/nginx
kubectl rollout history deployment/nginx
kubectl rollout undo deployment/nginx
kubectl rollout undo deployment/nginx --to-revision=2

# Services ⭐
kubectl expose pod nginx --port=80 --target-port=80 --type=ClusterIP
kubectl expose deployment nginx --port=80 --type=NodePort
kubectl get svc
kubectl get endpoints

# ConfigMaps and Secrets ⭐
kubectl create configmap myconfig --from-literal=key=value
kubectl create configmap myconfig --from-file=config.txt
kubectl create secret generic mysecret --from-literal=password=secret123

# Namespaces
kubectl create namespace dev
kubectl get all -n dev

# Resource info
kubectl api-resources                    # list all resource types
kubectl explain pod.spec                 # explain fields
kubectl explain deployment.spec.template

# Copying files
kubectl cp <pod>:/path/to/file ./local-file
kubectl cp ./local-file <pod>:/path/to/file

# Context switching ⭐
kubectl config get-contexts
kubectl config use-context <context-name>
kubectl config current-context

# Labels and selectors
kubectl label pod nginx app=web
kubectl get pods -l app=web
kubectl get pods --selector=app=web

# Taints and tolerations
kubectl taint node node1 key=value:NoSchedule
kubectl taint node node1 key=value:NoSchedule-         # remove taint

# Drain and cordon (node maintenance) ⭐
kubectl cordon node1        # mark node unschedulable
kubectl drain node1 --ignore-daemonsets --delete-emptydir-data
kubectl uncordon node1      # re-enable scheduling

# Top (resource usage — requires metrics-server)
kubectl top nodes
kubectl top pods
kubectl top pods -n kube-system
```

## Essential `--dry-run -o yaml` pattern ⭐

This is the most important CKA exam trick. Generate YAML from imperative commands:

```bash
# Generate any resource's YAML without creating it
kubectl run nginx --image=nginx --dry-run=client -o yaml > pod.yaml
kubectl create deployment web --image=nginx --dry-run=client -o yaml > deploy.yaml
kubectl create configmap myconfig --from-literal=key=val --dry-run=client -o yaml
kubectl expose pod nginx --port=80 --dry-run=client -o yaml

# Then edit the YAML and apply
kubectl apply -f pod.yaml
```

## JSONPath and sorting output

```bash
kubectl get pods -o jsonpath='{.items[*].metadata.name}'
kubectl get nodes -o jsonpath='{.items[*].status.addresses[?(@.type=="InternalIP")].address}'
kubectl get pods --sort-by=.metadata.name
kubectl get pods --sort-by=.status.startTime
```

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Domain 1: Cluster Architecture, Installation & Configuration (25%)

## 1.1 Kubernetes Architecture

### Control Plane components

**kube-apiserver** — the front door to Kubernetes. All kubectl commands hit this. Validates requests, persists state to etcd. Horizontally scalable.

**etcd** — distributed key-value store. Single source of truth for all cluster state. Always back this up before cluster changes. Runs on port 2379 (client) and 2380 (peer).

**kube-scheduler** — watches for pods with no node assigned, selects the best node based on resource requirements, taints/tolerations, affinity rules.

**kube-controller-manager** — runs all the controllers: Node Controller, ReplicaSet Controller, Endpoint Controller, etc. Each controller watches state and reconciles to desired state.

**cloud-controller-manager** — manages cloud-provider-specific resources (load balancers, volumes on AWS/GCP/Azure).

### Worker Node components

**kubelet** — agent running on every node. Watches API server, ensures containers in pods are running. Talks to container runtime via CRI.

**kube-proxy** — runs on every node. Maintains network rules (iptables or ipvs) for Service routing. Ensures traffic to Service IPs is forwarded to the right pods.

**Container runtime** — runs containers. Must implement CRI (Container Runtime Interface). Options: containerd (default), CRI-O. Docker is no longer directly supported (uses containerd underneath).

### Extension interfaces
- **CRI** — Container Runtime Interface (kubelet ↔ container runtime)
- **CNI** — Container Network Interface (networking plugins: Calico, Flannel, Weave, Cilium)
- **CSI** — Container Storage Interface (storage drivers: AWS EBS, Azure Disk, NFS)

## 1.2 kubeadm Cluster Setup

Understanding kubeadm is essential — you'll be asked to initialize clusters, add nodes, and upgrade.

### Initialize a control plane
```bash
# On the control plane node:
kubeadm init --pod-network-cidr=10.244.0.0/16   # for Flannel
kubeadm init --pod-network-cidr=192.168.0.0/16  # for Calico

# After init, set up kubeconfig:
mkdir -p $HOME/.kube
cp -i /etc/kubernetes/admin.conf $HOME/.kube/config
chown $(id -u):$(id -g) $HOME/.kube/config

# Install CNI (e.g., Calico):
kubectl apply -f https://docs.projectcalico.org/manifests/calico.yaml
```

### Join worker nodes
```bash
# Get the join command from kubeadm init output, or regenerate:
kubeadm token create --print-join-command

# On each worker node, run the join command:
kubeadm join <control-plane-ip>:6443 --token <token> \
  --discovery-token-ca-cert-hash sha256:<hash>
```

### Cluster upgrade with kubeadm

> **Important — package repo changed in Kubernetes 1.24+:** The old `packages.cloud.google.com` repo is deprecated. Since v1.24, Kubernetes uses `pkgs.k8s.io`. Packages are now versioned as `kubeadm=1.32.0-1.1` (not the old `1.29.0-00` format). Always check the exam's Kubernetes version and adjust accordingly.

```bash
# ── STEP 0: Set target version as a variable (makes commands reusable) ─────────
TARGET_VERSION=1.32.0    # Replace with the actual version from the exam question

# ── ON THE CONTROL PLANE NODE ───────────────────────────────────────────────────

# Step 1: Add the correct package repo (one-time setup if not already done)
echo "deb [signed-by=/etc/apt/keyrings/kubernetes-apt-keyring.gpg] https://pkgs.k8s.io/core:/stable:/v1.32/deb/ /"   | tee /etc/apt/sources.list.d/kubernetes.list

# Step 2: Unhold kubeadm so we can upgrade it
apt-mark unhold kubeadm

# Step 3: Install the new kubeadm version
apt-get update && apt-get install -y kubeadm=1.32.0-1.1

# Step 4: Hold kubeadm again to prevent accidental upgrades
apt-mark hold kubeadm

# Step 5: Check upgrade plan (shows what will change)
kubeadm upgrade plan

# Step 6: Apply the upgrade to the control plane
kubeadm upgrade apply v1.32.0

# Step 7: Upgrade kubelet and kubectl on the control plane
apt-mark unhold kubelet kubectl
apt-get install -y kubelet=1.32.0-1.1 kubectl=1.32.0-1.1
apt-mark hold kubelet kubectl
systemctl daemon-reload && systemctl restart kubelet

# ── FOR EACH WORKER NODE (repeat this block for every worker) ───────────────────

# Step 8: Drain the worker node (on control plane)
kubectl drain worker1 --ignore-daemonsets --delete-emptydir-data

# Step 9: SSH to the worker node and upgrade it
ssh worker1
  apt-mark unhold kubeadm
  apt-get update && apt-get install -y kubeadm=1.32.0-1.1
  apt-mark hold kubeadm
  kubeadm upgrade node        # upgrades worker node config (no "apply" needed)
  apt-mark unhold kubelet kubectl
  apt-get install -y kubelet=1.32.0-1.1 kubectl=1.32.0-1.1
  apt-mark hold kubelet kubectl
  systemctl daemon-reload && systemctl restart kubelet
exit   # back to control plane

# Step 10: Uncordon the worker node (on control plane)
kubectl uncordon worker1
kubectl get nodes              # verify node shows upgraded version
```

## 1.3 etcd Backup and Restore ⭐ (HIGH PRIORITY)

etcd backup is almost always on the CKA exam. Know this cold.

```bash
# Check etcd pod for endpoints and certificate locations:
kubectl describe pod etcd-controlplane -n kube-system

# BACKUP etcd:
ETCDCTL_API=3 etcdctl \
  snapshot save /opt/etcd-backup.db \
  --endpoints=https://127.0.0.1:2379 \
  --cacert=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/ca.crt \
  --cert=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/server.crt \
  --key=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/server.key

# Verify backup:
ETCDCTL_API=3 etcdctl snapshot status /opt/etcd-backup.db

# RESTORE etcd (stop etcd first or restore to new directory):
ETCDCTL_API=3 etcdctl snapshot restore /opt/etcd-backup.db \
  --data-dir=/var/lib/etcd-from-backup

# Update etcd manifest to use new data directory:
# Edit /etc/kubernetes/manifests/etcd.yaml
# Change: --data-dir=/var/lib/etcd
# To:     --data-dir=/var/lib/etcd-from-backup
# Also update the hostPath volume mount to match
```

> **Exam Tip:** The `etcdctl` certificates path is always under `/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/`. The `--cacert`, `--cert`, and `--key` flags are all mandatory for etcdctl commands. Run `kubectl describe pod etcd-controlplane -n kube-system` to find the exact paths used.

## 1.4 RBAC (Role-Based Access Control) ⭐

RBAC controls what users and service accounts can do in the cluster.

### Key resources
- **Role** — permissions within a single namespace
- **ClusterRole** — permissions across the entire cluster (or cluster-level resources)
- **RoleBinding** — binds a Role to a user/group/serviceaccount in a namespace
- **ClusterRoleBinding** — binds a ClusterRole cluster-wide

```yaml
# Role — allows get/list/watch pods in "dev" namespace
apiVersion: rbac.authorization.k8s.io/v1
kind: Role
metadata:
  namespace: dev
  name: pod-reader
rules:
- apiGroups: [""]
  resources: ["pods"]
  verbs: ["get", "list", "watch"]
---
# RoleBinding — bind the role to a user
apiVersion: rbac.authorization.k8s.io/v1
kind: RoleBinding
metadata:
  name: read-pods
  namespace: dev
subjects:
- kind: User
  name: john
  apiGroup: rbac.authorization.k8s.io
roleRef:
  kind: Role
  name: pod-reader
  apiGroup: rbac.authorization.k8s.io
```

```bash
# Quick RBAC creation (imperative) ⭐
kubectl create role pod-reader --verb=get,list,watch --resource=pods -n dev
kubectl create rolebinding read-pods --role=pod-reader --user=john -n dev
kubectl create clusterrole node-reader --verb=get,list --resource=nodes
kubectl create clusterrolebinding read-nodes --clusterrole=node-reader --user=john

# Check permissions:
kubectl auth can-i get pods --as=john -n dev
kubectl auth can-i create deployments --as=john
kubectl auth can-i '*' '*'    # check if you're cluster admin
```

### ServiceAccounts
```bash
kubectl create serviceaccount my-sa -n default

# Bind a role to a ServiceAccount:
kubectl create rolebinding my-sa-binding \
  --role=pod-reader \
  --serviceaccount=default:my-sa \
  -n default
```

## 1.5 KubeConfig

```bash
# View kubeconfig:
kubectl config view
kubectl config get-clusters
kubectl config get-contexts

# Set context:
kubectl config use-context prod-cluster

# Add a new cluster to kubeconfig:
kubectl config set-cluster new-cluster --server=https://1.2.3.4:6443
kubectl config set-credentials new-user --client-certificate=user.crt --client-key=user.key
kubectl config set-context new-context --cluster=new-cluster --user=new-user
```

## 1.6 Admission Controllers and API access

**Admission controllers** — intercept API requests after authentication and authorization, before persisting to etcd. Examples: `NamespaceLifecycle`, `LimitRanger`, `ResourceQuota`, `PodSecurity`.

```bash
# View enabled admission controllers:
kube-apiserver --help | grep admission

# Check admission controllers in running cluster:
kubectl describe pod kube-apiserver-controlplane -n kube-system | grep admission
```

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Domain 2: Workloads & Scheduling (15%)

## 2.1 Pod Deep Dive

### Multi-container pods

```yaml
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: multi-container
spec:
  containers:
  - name: main-app
    image: nginx
    ports:
    - containerPort: 80
  - name: sidecar
    image: busybox
    command: ['sh', '-c', 'while true; do echo logging; sleep 5; done']
  initContainers:
  - name: init-setup
    image: busybox
    command: ['sh', '-c', 'echo "init complete"']
```

### Resource limits and requests ⭐
```yaml
spec:
  containers:
  - name: app
    image: nginx
    resources:
      requests:
        memory: "64Mi"
        cpu: "250m"
      limits:
        memory: "128Mi"
        cpu: "500m"
```

> `250m` cpu = 0.25 of one CPU core. `64Mi` = 64 mebibytes RAM.

### Environment variables from ConfigMaps and Secrets ⭐
```yaml
spec:
  containers:
  - name: app
    image: nginx
    env:
    - name: DB_HOST
      valueFrom:
        configMapKeyRef:
          name: my-config
          key: database-host
    - name: DB_PASSWORD
      valueFrom:
        secretKeyRef:
          name: my-secret
          key: password
    envFrom:
    - configMapRef:
        name: my-config     # load all keys from ConfigMap as env vars
```

### Volume mounts ⭐
```yaml
spec:
  volumes:
  - name: config-vol
    configMap:
      name: my-config
  - name: secret-vol
    secret:
      secretName: my-secret
  - name: empty-dir
    emptyDir: {}
  containers:
  - name: app
    image: nginx
    volumeMounts:
    - name: config-vol
      mountPath: /etc/config
    - name: empty-dir
      mountPath: /tmp/data
```

## 2.2 Deployments and ReplicaSets

```bash
# Quick deployment operations ⭐
kubectl create deployment web --image=nginx:1.18 --replicas=3
kubectl scale deployment web --replicas=5
kubectl set image deployment/web nginx=nginx:1.19
kubectl rollout status deployment/web
kubectl rollout undo deployment/web
kubectl rollout history deployment/web

# Pause/resume rollout:
kubectl rollout pause deployment/web
kubectl rollout resume deployment/web
```

```yaml
# Deployment with rolling update strategy
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata:
  name: web
spec:
  replicas: 3
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: web
  strategy:
    type: RollingUpdate
    rollingUpdate:
      maxSurge: 1           # can have 1 extra pod during update
      maxUnavailable: 1     # can have 1 pod unavailable during update
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: web
    spec:
      containers:
      - name: nginx
        image: nginx:1.19
```

## 2.3 Scheduling

### NodeSelector and NodeAffinity

```yaml
# Simple: schedule on node with label disktype=ssd
spec:
  nodeSelector:
    disktype: ssd

# Advanced: NodeAffinity
spec:
  affinity:
    nodeAffinity:
      requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:   # hard requirement
        nodeSelectorTerms:
        - matchExpressions:
          - key: disktype
            operator: In
            values: ["ssd", "nvme"]
      preferredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:  # soft preference
      - weight: 1
        preference:
          matchExpressions:
          - key: zone
            operator: In
            values: ["us-east-1a"]
```

### Taints and Tolerations ⭐

```bash
# Taint a node (prevent scheduling unless tolerated)
kubectl taint node node1 key=value:NoSchedule
kubectl taint node node1 key=value:NoExecute     # evicts existing pods too
kubectl taint node node1 key=value:PreferNoSchedule

# Remove a taint (add - at end)
kubectl taint node node1 key=value:NoSchedule-
```

```yaml
# Pod tolerating a taint
spec:
  tolerations:
  - key: "key"
    operator: "Equal"
    value: "value"
    effect: "NoSchedule"
  - key: "key"
    operator: "Exists"   # matches any value for this key
    effect: "NoSchedule"
```

### Static Pods ⭐

Static pods are managed directly by kubelet (not the API server). They're always located in `/etc/kubernetes/manifests/` on the node. Used for control plane components.

```bash
# To create a static pod:
# Copy a YAML file to /etc/kubernetes/manifests/ on the node
# kubelet automatically picks it up and creates the pod

# To remove a static pod:
# Delete the YAML file from /etc/kubernetes/manifests/
```

### DaemonSets and Jobs

```yaml
# DaemonSet — runs one pod per node
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: DaemonSet
metadata:
  name: monitoring-agent
spec:
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: monitoring
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: monitoring
    spec:
      containers:
      - name: agent
        image: monitoring-agent:latest
---
# Job — run to completion
apiVersion: batch/v1
kind: Job
metadata:
  name: data-processor
spec:
  completions: 3      # run 3 successful completions
  parallelism: 2      # 2 at a time
  template:
    spec:
      containers:
      - name: processor
        image: busybox
        command: ['sh', '-c', 'echo processing; sleep 5']
      restartPolicy: Never   # Jobs must have Never or OnFailure
---
# CronJob — scheduled job
apiVersion: batch/v1
kind: CronJob
metadata:
  name: backup
spec:
  schedule: "0 2 * * *"   # daily at 2am
  jobTemplate:
    spec:
      template:
        spec:
          containers:
          - name: backup
            image: backup-tool:latest
          restartPolicy: OnFailure
```

## 2.4 ConfigMaps and Secrets ⭐

```bash
# Create ConfigMap
kubectl create configmap app-config \
  --from-literal=DB_HOST=mysql \
  --from-literal=DB_PORT=3306
kubectl create configmap app-config --from-file=config.properties

# Create Secret (base64 encoded automatically)
kubectl create secret generic db-secret \
  --from-literal=DB_PASSWORD=mysecretpassword
kubectl create secret docker-registry regcred \
  --docker-server=myregistry.io \
  --docker-username=user \
  --docker-password=password

# View (decode) a secret
kubectl get secret db-secret -o jsonpath='{.data.DB_PASSWORD}' | base64 -d
```

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Domain 3: Services & Networking (20%)

## 3.1 Services ⭐

```bash
# ClusterIP — internal only (default)
kubectl expose deployment nginx --port=80 --target-port=80 --type=ClusterIP

# NodePort — exposes on each node's IP at a static port (30000-32767)
kubectl expose deployment nginx --port=80 --target-port=80 --type=NodePort --name=nginx-np

# LoadBalancer — cloud provider external LB
kubectl expose deployment nginx --port=80 --type=LoadBalancer

# Get service details
kubectl get svc
kubectl describe svc nginx
kubectl get endpoints nginx   # see which pod IPs are backing the service
```

```yaml
apiVersion: v1
kind: Service
metadata:
  name: my-service
spec:
  selector:
    app: nginx        # selects pods with this label
  ports:
  - protocol: TCP
    port: 80          # service port (what clients connect to)
    targetPort: 8080  # container port
    nodePort: 30080   # NodePort only (optional, auto-assigned if omitted)
  type: ClusterIP
```

### DNS in Kubernetes

Every service gets a DNS name: `<service-name>.<namespace>.svc.cluster.local`

Pods can reach a service as:
- `my-service` (same namespace)
- `my-service.dev` (other namespace)
- `my-service.dev.svc.cluster.local` (fully qualified)

```bash
# Test DNS from inside a pod:
kubectl run test --image=busybox --rm -it -- nslookup kubernetes
kubectl run test --image=busybox --rm -it -- wget -O- http://nginx-service
```

## 3.2 Ingress

Ingress exposes HTTP/HTTPS routes to services. Requires an Ingress Controller (nginx, traefik, etc.).

```yaml
apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: Ingress
metadata:
  name: my-ingress
  annotations:
    nginx.ingress.kubernetes.io/rewrite-target: /
spec:
  ingressClassName: nginx
  rules:
  - host: myapp.example.com
    http:
      paths:
      - path: /api
        pathType: Prefix
        backend:
          service:
            name: api-service
            port:
              number: 8080
      - path: /
        pathType: Prefix
        backend:
          service:
            name: web-service
            port:
              number: 80
```

## 3.3 NetworkPolicy ⭐

NetworkPolicies control which pods can communicate with which other pods/services. By default, all pods can communicate with all other pods. A NetworkPolicy restricts this.

```yaml
# Deny all ingress to a namespace, then allow specific traffic
apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: NetworkPolicy
metadata:
  name: deny-all-ingress
  namespace: prod
spec:
  podSelector: {}     # applies to ALL pods in namespace
  policyTypes:
  - Ingress
  # No ingress rules = deny all ingress
---
# Allow ingress only from pods with label role=frontend
apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: NetworkPolicy
metadata:
  name: allow-frontend
  namespace: prod
spec:
  podSelector:
    matchLabels:
      app: backend          # applies to pods labeled app=backend
  policyTypes:
  - Ingress
  ingress:
  - from:
    - podSelector:
        matchLabels:
          role: frontend    # only allow from pods with this label
    ports:
    - protocol: TCP
      port: 8080
---
# Allow egress to specific namespace
apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: NetworkPolicy
metadata:
  name: allow-dns-egress
spec:
  podSelector: {}
  policyTypes:
  - Egress
  egress:
  - to:
    - namespaceSelector:
        matchLabels:
          kubernetes.io/metadata.name: kube-system
    ports:
    - protocol: UDP
      port: 53
```

> **Exam Tip:** NetworkPolicies require a CNI that supports them (Calico, Cilium, Weave). Flannel does NOT enforce NetworkPolicies. Always test with `kubectl exec` after applying.

## 3.4 CoreDNS and DNS Troubleshooting

```bash
# Check CoreDNS pods
kubectl get pods -n kube-system -l k8s-app=kube-dns

# Check CoreDNS ConfigMap
kubectl describe configmap coredns -n kube-system

# Test DNS resolution from a pod
kubectl run dns-test --image=busybox --rm -it -- nslookup kubernetes
kubectl run dns-test --image=busybox --rm -it -- nslookup nginx-service.default.svc.cluster.local

# Check kube-proxy
kubectl get pods -n kube-system -l k8s-app=kube-proxy
kubectl logs -n kube-system -l k8s-app=kube-proxy
```

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Domain 4: Storage (10%)

## 4.1 Persistent Volumes and Claims ⭐

```yaml
# PersistentVolume (PV) — cluster-level resource, admin creates this
apiVersion: v1
kind: PersistentVolume
metadata:
  name: my-pv
spec:
  capacity:
    storage: 1Gi
  volumeMode: Filesystem
  accessModes:
  - ReadWriteOnce    # RWO: one node read-write
  # ReadOnlyMany (ROX), ReadWriteMany (RWX) also available
  persistentVolumeReclaimPolicy: Retain  # Retain, Recycle, or Delete
  storageClassName: manual
  hostPath:                              # local path (for testing only)
    path: /mnt/data
---
# PersistentVolumeClaim (PVC) — user requests storage
apiVersion: v1
kind: PersistentVolumeClaim
metadata:
  name: my-pvc
spec:
  accessModes:
  - ReadWriteOnce
  storageClassName: manual
  resources:
    requests:
      storage: 500Mi
---
# Use the PVC in a Pod
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: pvc-pod
spec:
  volumes:
  - name: storage
    persistentVolumeClaim:
      claimName: my-pvc    # reference the PVC
  containers:
  - name: app
    image: nginx
    volumeMounts:
    - name: storage
      mountPath: /data
```

## 4.2 StorageClass (dynamic provisioning)

```yaml
apiVersion: storage.k8s.io/v1
kind: StorageClass
metadata:
  name: fast
provisioner: kubernetes.io/aws-ebs    # cloud provider
parameters:
  type: gp3
reclaimPolicy: Delete
allowVolumeExpansion: true
```

```bash
kubectl get pv
kubectl get pvc
kubectl get sc    # storage classes

# Check PV/PVC binding status
kubectl get pvc my-pvc   # STATUS should be "Bound"
```

### Access modes summary

| Mode | Abbreviation | Meaning |
|---|---|---|
| ReadWriteOnce | RWO | One node can read and write |
| ReadOnlyMany | ROX | Many nodes can read |
| ReadWriteMany | RWX | Many nodes can read and write |
| ReadWriteOncePod | RWOP | One pod can read and write |

<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>

# Domain 5: Troubleshooting (30%) ⭐⭐⭐

**This is 30% of your score. Master it.**

## 5.1 Troubleshooting Framework

When you see a broken system, follow this order:
1. **Check the symptoms** — what's the error? What's not working?
2. **Check the object** — `kubectl describe` → look at Events section
3. **Check logs** — `kubectl logs` (and previous logs with `--previous`)
4. **Check node status** — is the node Ready?
5. **Check control plane** — is kube-apiserver/scheduler/etcd running?

## 5.2 Pod Troubleshooting

```bash
# Pod is Pending
kubectl describe pod <pod> | grep -A 10 Events
# Common causes: no suitable node (resource, taints), PVC not bound, image pull waiting

# Pod is CrashLoopBackOff
kubectl logs <pod>              # current logs
kubectl logs <pod> --previous  # previous container logs (after restart)
kubectl describe pod <pod>     # check last state, exit code

# Pod is ImagePullBackOff
# Wrong image name, wrong tag, or private registry without auth
kubectl describe pod <pod> | grep -i image

# Pod is stuck in Terminating
kubectl delete pod <pod> --force --grace-period=0

# Debug a running pod
kubectl exec -it <pod> -- /bin/sh
kubectl exec -it <pod> -- curl http://other-service

# Run a temporary debug pod
kubectl run debug --image=busybox --rm -it -- sh
kubectl run debug --image=nicolaka/netshoot --rm -it -- sh   # for networking
```

### Common error patterns

| Error | Cause | Fix |
|---|---|---|
| `ImagePullBackOff` | Wrong image or no registry access | Fix image name; add imagePullSecret |
| `CrashLoopBackOff` | App crashes on start | Check logs, fix app config |
| `Pending` (Insufficient memory/cpu) | Not enough resources on nodes | Scale cluster or reduce resource requests |
| `Pending` (unbound PVC) | PV doesn't exist or wrong storageClass | Create matching PV or correct PVC |
| `Error: container has runAsNonRoot` | SecurityContext mismatch | Adjust runAsUser or SecurityContext |

## 5.3 Node Troubleshooting ⭐

```bash
# Check node status
kubectl get nodes
kubectl describe node <node-name>   # check Conditions, Capacity, Allocatable

# Node is NotReady — SSH to the node and check:
ssh <node>
systemctl status kubelet            # is kubelet running?
systemctl restart kubelet           # restart it
journalctl -u kubelet -f            # kubelet logs
journalctl -u kubelet --since="10 min ago"

# Check kubelet config
cat /etc/kubernetes/kubelet.conf
cat /var/lib/kubelet/config.yaml

# containerd runtime issues
systemctl status containerd
journalctl -u containerd

# Check disk pressure
df -h                               # disk usage
free -m                             # memory
```

## 5.4 Control Plane Troubleshooting ⭐

Control plane components run as static pods in `/etc/kubernetes/manifests/`. If you break them, edit those YAML files to fix.

```bash
# Check control plane pods
kubectl get pods -n kube-system

# If control plane pods are failing (they're static pods):
ls /etc/kubernetes/manifests/     # kube-apiserver.yaml, etcd.yaml, etc.
cat /etc/kubernetes/manifests/kube-apiserver.yaml

# Check component logs
kubectl logs kube-apiserver-controlplane -n kube-system
kubectl logs kube-scheduler-controlplane -n kube-system
kubectl logs kube-controller-manager-controlplane -n kube-system

# If kubectl isn't working (API server down), check logs directly:
crictl ps                          # list containers using crictl
crictl logs <container-id>         # get container logs

# kube-scheduler logs if pod not scheduling
kubectl logs kube-scheduler-controlplane -n kube-system | tail -50
```

## 5.5 Networking Troubleshooting

```bash
# Service not reachable
kubectl get svc <service>           # check selector and ports
kubectl get endpoints <service>     # are there any backing pod IPs?
kubectl describe svc <service>

# If no endpoints: labels on pods don't match service selector
kubectl get pods --show-labels      # check pod labels
kubectl get svc -o yaml             # check selector

# Test connectivity from inside a pod
kubectl exec -it <pod> -- curl http://service-name.namespace.svc.cluster.local
kubectl exec -it <pod> -- nslookup kubernetes.default

# DNS not working
kubectl get pods -n kube-system | grep coredns
kubectl logs coredns-<hash> -n kube-system

# NetworkPolicy blocking traffic
# Check if a NetworkPolicy is applied to the namespace
kubectl get networkpolicy -A
kubectl describe networkpolicy <name> -n <namespace>

# kube-proxy issues
kubectl get pods -n kube-system | grep kube-proxy
kubectl logs kube-proxy-<hash> -n kube-system
```

## 5.6 Useful Troubleshooting One-liners

```bash
# Get all events sorted by time (great for spotting issues)
kubectl get events --sort-by=.lastTimestamp -A

# Get events for a specific resource
kubectl get events --field-selector=involvedObject.name=<pod-name>

# Check resource usage
kubectl top nodes
kubectl top pods -A

# Check all failing pods across namespaces
kubectl get pods -A | grep -v Running | grep -v Completed

# Check certificate expiry
kubeadm certs check-expiration

# Renew certificates
kubeadm certs renew all
```

# CKA Practice Tasks

> CKA is performance-based. These tasks mirror the real exam format. Set a timer: aim to complete each task within 5-7 minutes. Use `--dry-run=client -o yaml` to generate manifests.

## Task Set 1: Core Operations

**Task 1.1** — Create a pod named `web-pod` in namespace `production` using image `nginx:1.19`. Ensure the pod has a label `app=web` and a resource request of 100m CPU and 128Mi memory.

**Solution:**
```bash
kubectl create namespace production 2>/dev/null
kubectl run web-pod --image=nginx:1.19 -n production \
  --labels=app=web \
  --requests=cpu=100m,memory=128Mi \
  --dry-run=client -o yaml > web-pod.yaml
kubectl apply -f web-pod.yaml
# Verify:
kubectl get pod web-pod -n production --show-labels
```

---

**Task 1.2** — Scale the deployment `frontend` in namespace `default` to 5 replicas. Then update the image to `nginx:1.20`. Record the change.

```bash
kubectl scale deployment frontend --replicas=5
kubectl set image deployment/frontend nginx=nginx:1.20
kubectl rollout status deployment/frontend
kubectl rollout history deployment/frontend
```

---

**Task 1.3** — Create a ConfigMap named `app-config` with key `APP_COLOR=blue` and `APP_MODE=production`. Then create a pod `config-pod` that uses these as environment variables.

```bash
kubectl create configmap app-config \
  --from-literal=APP_COLOR=blue \
  --from-literal=APP_MODE=production

kubectl run config-pod --image=nginx --dry-run=client -o yaml > pod.yaml
# Edit pod.yaml to add envFrom: - configMapRef: name: app-config
kubectl apply -f pod.yaml
kubectl exec config-pod -- env | grep APP
```

---

**Task 1.4** — Back up the etcd data to `/opt/backup/etcd-snapshot.db`. The etcd is running on the control plane node at `https://127.0.0.1:2379`. Certificates are in `/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/`.

```bash
mkdir -p /opt/backup
ETCDCTL_API=3 etcdctl snapshot save /opt/backup/etcd-snapshot.db \
  --endpoints=https://127.0.0.1:2379 \
  --cacert=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/ca.crt \
  --cert=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/server.crt \
  --key=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/server.key
ETCDCTL_API=3 etcdctl snapshot status /opt/backup/etcd-snapshot.db
```

---

**Task 1.5** — Create a user `john` who can only read pods in the `dev` namespace. Create the necessary Role and RoleBinding.

```bash
kubectl create namespace dev 2>/dev/null
kubectl create role pod-reader \
  --verb=get,list,watch \
  --resource=pods \
  -n dev
kubectl create rolebinding john-pod-reader \
  --role=pod-reader \
  --user=john \
  -n dev
# Verify:
kubectl auth can-i get pods --as=john -n dev   # should say: yes
kubectl auth can-i delete pods --as=john -n dev  # should say: no
```

## Task Set 2: Networking and Storage

**Task 2.1** — Create a NetworkPolicy named `restrict-backend` in namespace `prod`. Only pods with label `role=frontend` should be able to access pods with label `app=backend` on port 8080. All other ingress should be blocked.

```yaml
# Apply this manifest:
apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
kind: NetworkPolicy
metadata:
  name: restrict-backend
  namespace: prod
spec:
  podSelector:
    matchLabels:
      app: backend
  policyTypes:
  - Ingress
  ingress:
  - from:
    - podSelector:
        matchLabels:
          role: frontend
    ports:
    - protocol: TCP
      port: 8080
```

---

**Task 2.2** — Create a PersistentVolume named `data-pv` with 500Mi capacity, `ReadWriteOnce` access mode, `manual` storage class, and hostPath `/mnt/data`. Then create a PVC named `data-pvc` requesting 300Mi from that storage class.

```yaml
# PV:
apiVersion: v1
kind: PersistentVolume
metadata:
  name: data-pv
spec:
  capacity:
    storage: 500Mi
  accessModes:
  - ReadWriteOnce
  persistentVolumeReclaimPolicy: Retain
  storageClassName: manual
  hostPath:
    path: /mnt/data
---
# PVC:
apiVersion: v1
kind: PersistentVolumeClaim
metadata:
  name: data-pvc
spec:
  accessModes:
  - ReadWriteOnce
  storageClassName: manual
  resources:
    requests:
      storage: 300Mi
```
```bash
kubectl apply -f pv-pvc.yaml
kubectl get pvc data-pvc   # verify STATUS=Bound
```

---

**Task 2.3** — Expose the deployment `api-server` in namespace `backend` as a NodePort service on port 9090, targeting port 8080 on the pods.

```bash
kubectl expose deployment api-server \
  --port=9090 \
  --target-port=8080 \
  --type=NodePort \
  -n backend \
  --name=api-service
kubectl get svc api-service -n backend
```

## Task Set 3: Troubleshooting

**Task 3.1** — The pod `broken-pod` in namespace `debug` is in `CrashLoopBackOff`. Investigate and document the cause without fixing it.

```bash
kubectl describe pod broken-pod -n debug  # check Events + Last State
kubectl logs broken-pod -n debug           # current logs
kubectl logs broken-pod -n debug --previous  # logs from crashed instance
```

---

**Task 3.2** — A worker node `worker2` is in `NotReady` state. Investigate and fix the issue.

```bash
kubectl describe node worker2   # check Conditions
ssh worker2
systemctl status kubelet
journalctl -u kubelet -n 50
systemctl restart kubelet
exit
kubectl get nodes   # verify worker2 is Ready again
```

---

**Task 3.3** — The kube-scheduler appears to not be scheduling pods. Pods are stuck in `Pending` (no error about resources). Investigate.

```bash
kubectl get pods -n kube-system | grep scheduler
kubectl describe pod kube-scheduler-controlplane -n kube-system
# If the static pod manifest is broken:
cat /etc/kubernetes/manifests/kube-scheduler.yaml
# Look for misconfigured flags or wrong image
# Edit to fix:
vi /etc/kubernetes/manifests/kube-scheduler.yaml
# kubelet auto-restarts the static pod
kubectl get pods -n kube-system | grep scheduler  # wait for Running
```

# CKA Cheatsheets and Quick Reference

## YAML Template Library (memorize the patterns)

### Pod (bare minimum)
```yaml
apiVersion: v1
kind: Pod
metadata:
  name: my-pod
  namespace: default
spec:
  containers:
  - name: container
    image: nginx
```

### Deployment
```yaml
apiVersion: apps/v1
kind: Deployment
metadata:
  name: my-deploy
spec:
  replicas: 3
  selector:
    matchLabels:
      app: my-app
  template:
    metadata:
      labels:
        app: my-app
    spec:
      containers:
      - name: container
        image: nginx
```

### Service (ClusterIP)
```yaml
apiVersion: v1
kind: Service
metadata:
  name: my-svc
spec:
  selector:
    app: my-app
  ports:
  - port: 80
    targetPort: 8080
```

### PV + PVC
```yaml
apiVersion: v1
kind: PersistentVolume
metadata:
  name: my-pv
spec:
  capacity:
    storage: 1Gi
  accessModes: [ReadWriteOnce]
  storageClassName: manual
  hostPath:
    path: /mnt/data
---
apiVersion: v1
kind: PersistentVolumeClaim
metadata:
  name: my-pvc
spec:
  accessModes: [ReadWriteOnce]
  storageClassName: manual
  resources:
    requests:
      storage: 500Mi
```

## CKA Exam Tips

1. **Switch contexts before every task** — read the task, run `kubectl config use-context <name>` immediately.
2. **Use `--dry-run=client -o yaml`** to generate base YAML you then edit — 10× faster than writing from scratch.
3. **Use `kubectl explain`** when you forget a field: `kubectl explain pod.spec.containers.resources`.
4. **Keyboard shortcuts in vim**: `dd` delete line, `yy` copy line, `p` paste, `:wq` save, `dG` delete to end of file.
5. **`kubectl api-resources -o name | grep <keyword>`** — find the right resource type name.
6. **When debugging**, always check `kubectl describe` Events section first.
7. **etcd backup path** is always `/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/` for certificates.
8. **Static pods** live at `/etc/kubernetes/manifests/` — edit those files to fix control plane issues.
9. **Time management**: If a task takes more than 7 minutes, flag and move on. Return at the end.
10. **Verify every task** after completing it: run the kubectl get/describe command to confirm success.

## Readiness checklist
- [ ] Can create any resource type imperatively with `--dry-run=client -o yaml` in <60 seconds.
- [ ] Know the etcd backup commands by heart (all flags).
- [ ] Can set up RBAC (Role, RoleBinding, ClusterRole, ClusterRoleBinding) in <3 minutes.
- [ ] Can troubleshoot a NotReady node by checking and restarting kubelet.
- [ ] Can create and bind a PV/PVC correctly.
- [ ] Can write a NetworkPolicy from scratch.
- [ ] Know how to drain/uncordon a node for maintenance.
- [ ] Completed at least 2 Killer Shell sessions with passing scores.
- [ ] Can navigate kubernetes.io docs in <30 seconds to find any YAML example.


<div style="page-break-after: always;"></div>
